المقالة الثانية في الأحكام
79
ولا شناعة فيه واعلم أنه قد ثبت انفصال نفس الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء ببراهين لا تدحضها شبهة أصلاً، لكنهم ما اكتفوا بهذا بل ادعوا أن في غير الآخر نفس الوجوب فقط، وأما وجوب الأداء، فإنما يتحقق في الآخر، ويتعلق الخطاب فيه وهو وقت التضييق، وأورد عليه أنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لكان الطلب مع المطلوب، إذ حال التضييق، حال وجود الواجب، وقبله ليس وجوب الأداء، وهذا الإيراد لا اختصاص له بهذا المقام فإن في الصوم أيضاً يلزم ذلك، لأن اليوم وقت الصوم، وقبله ليل ولا طلب فيه للصوم والجواب أن الآن السابق زمان يسع الواجب فقط، ويتضيق عن غيره والآن السابق على اليوم المقارن للصوم يتوجه الخطاب بأن يصلي في وقت التضييق ويصوم في اليوم فلا فساد واستدلوا على ما ادعوا أن فيما قبل الآخران أدى سقط الفرض، فهناك وجوب البتة، وإن أخر فلا إثم، فليس هناك طلب، وإلاّ لأثم لمخالفة الأمر، فهناك وجوب من دون وجوب الأداء، وأما في الآخر فيأثم بالتأخير، ففيه توجه الخطاب، ولا يخفى ما فيه فإنه لا يدل إلا على انتفاء الطلب المضيق، فإنه إن كان طلب في الأول موسعاً إلى الآخر بحيث يتخير المكلف أن يؤدي في أي وقت شاء فلا يلزم الإثم بالتأخير ولا محذور، ولعله من ها هنا زعم المصنف أن المطلوب نفي الطلب الحتمي، لكن تنبو عنه كلماتهم في مواضع ثم إنه يلزم عليهم أن لا يتحقق امتثال أصلاً، ولا يكون الرسول عليه وآله الصلاة والسلام والصحابة رضوان الله عليهم ممتثلين للأوامر الإلهية، فإن الامتثال ايقاع المأمور به كما هو مأمور، وقد فرض أنه لم يتعلق أمر قبل الآخر، وفي الآخر لم يتعلق لمنع الأداء في الوقت السابق على الآخر عن التعلق فيه ولا يبعد أن يتكلف ويقال : إن عدم ثبوت الامتثال لأجل ثبوت ما هو أعلى منه، وهو ابتغاء رضا الله بالمبادرة إلى الفعل قبل طلبه غير قبيح، ثم أنه يلزم أن لا يتحقق التكليف المنجز في الصلاة إلا على أقل القليل من المكلفين الآتين بالفعل حال التضيق أو القاضين، ولعلهم يلتزمونه : هذا وقد وقع في هذه المسألة نوع إطناب، لأنه من مطارح الأذكياء، وزل فيها أقدام كثر من المهرة، والله تعالى ولي
السداد.
The second article on rulings. 79. There is no objection in it. Know that the separation of the obligation itself from the obligation of performance has been established by proofs that no doubt can refute. But they (some scholars) were not content with this, rather they claimed that in other than the last part (of time) there is only the obligation itself. As for the obligation of performance, it only occurs in the last part, and the address (of command) is attached to it, and it is the time of restriction. It was objected: if that were the case, then the demand would coincide with the demanded, because the state of restriction is the state of the existence of the obligatory act, and before it there is no obligation of performance. This objection is not specific to this context, because in fasting also the same applies, for the day is the time of fasting and before it is night with no demand for fasting. The answer: the preceding moment is a time that accommodates only the obligatory act and is restricted from other than it. The moment preceding the day coinciding with fasting is addressed by the command to pray in the restricted time and to fast on the day, so there is no corruption. They argued for their claim: before the last part, if one performs, the obligation is discharged, so there is definitely an obligation; if one delays, there is no sin, so there is no demand; otherwise, there would be sin for violating the command. So there is obligation without obligation of performance. In the last part, one sins by delay, so there is direction of address. One cannot hide what is in it: it only indicates the negation of the restricted demand. For if there is a demand in the first part that is broadened until the last part such that the responsible person may choose to perform at any time, then no sin is incurred by delay and no problem. Perhaps from here the author concluded that what is intended is the negation of the definitive demand, but their words elsewhere diverge from this. Then it follows from them that no compliance occurs at all, and the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) and the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) would not be compliant with divine commands, for compliance is performing the commanded act as commanded, and it has been assumed that no command was attached before the last part, and in the last part it was not attached because the performance in the time preceding the last part prevents its attachment there. It is not far-fetched to say that the lack of compliance is due to what is higher than it, namely seeking Allah's pleasure by rushing to the act before its demand—this is not ugly. Then it follows that fully effective obligation (taklif munajjaz) in prayer is only realized for the very few who perform in the restricted time or make it up. Perhaps they accept this. This issue has become verbose, as it is a field for the sharp-witted, and many experts have slipped here. Allah is the Guide to correctness.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a theoretical usul discussion on the nature of obligation (wujub) and its relation to the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada). The author asserts that it is established by irrefutable proofs that the obligation itself is separate from the obligation of performance. According to some (likely the Hanafi school), in the non-final portion of time (e.g., before the last moment for a prayer), there is only the obligation itself, not the obligation of performance. The obligation of performance only arises in the final, restricted time (waqt al-tadyiq). An objection is raised: if that were the case, then at the time of restriction the demand would coincide with the existence of the obligatory act, which is problematic. The response is that the preceding time can accommodate the obligatory act, so there is no overlap. The author then critiques the argument that before the final time there is no demand because one can delay without sin; he explains that if the demand is broadened (wasi'), then delay does not entail sin, so it does not prove absence of demand. He points out the consequence that compliance would never truly occur, which is problematic for prophets and Companions. The discussion is noted to be complex and a source of error for many scholars.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The general legal obligation to perform an act, abstracted from its specific time.
وجوب الأداء (wujub al-ada): The obligation to perform the act specifically in its designated time, especially the restricted final portion.
التضييق (al-tadyiq): The restriction of time for performance, typically the final part of the time window.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view discussed, what exists in the non-final portion of time for a prayer?
A) Both the obligation and the obligation of performance
B) Only the general obligation, not the obligation of performance
C) Neither obligation nor obligation of performance
D) A conditional obligation that becomes effective upon delay

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul separates the general obligation (wujub) from the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada), with the latter binding only at the final restricted time.

Definitions of Ada, Qada, and I'ada in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Defining the three categories of wajib performance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of ada, qada, and i'ada and distinguish between them based on time and defect.

Core Idea
Ada is on-time performance; i'ada is within-time repetition due to defect; qada is after-time recovery.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut defines three key categories of performing wajib acts: ada (performance), i'ada (repetition), and qada (make-up). Understanding these is essential for determining when an obligation is fulfilled and when a defect requires compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope
The passage begins by stating that wajib is two types: ada and qada. Note that i'ada is presented as a subcategory under ada.
I'ada is treated as a type of ada because it is done within the time.
2
Parse the definition of ada
Read the definition of ada: performing the wajib in its Shariah-appointed time. Note the inclusive scope: it covers both the beginning and the end of the time. Also note the alternative definition requiring the beginning of the act within time.
Hanafis define ada as the entire act in time; Shafi'is require the start in time.
3
Parse the definition of i'ada
I'ada is repeating the same wajib within the same time due to a defect other than invalidity. The defect is leaving a wajib (like Fatiha). Note the dispute: some say it is mandub, but the correct Hanafi view is wajib if the defect was leaving a wajib.
The liability remains occupied until the missing wajib is made up.
4
Parse the definition of qada
Qada is performing the wajib after its time as a recovery. It includes intentional and unintentional misses, with or without excuse. Non-timed acts are not ada or qada.
Qada is a second obligation to make up for the missed one.
5
Consider the comprehensive definition
Imam Fakhr al-Islam gives a broader definition covering huquq al-ibad: ada is delivering the exact thing commanded; qada is delivering its equivalent. This extends beyond worship to rights.
This shows the definitions are not limited to ibadat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [أقسام الواجب]
الواجب قسمان : أداء وقضاء (الأداء : فعل الواجب في وقته المقدر له شرعاً) ودخل فيه المؤدي في الآخر من الوقت وغير الآخر، فيشمل الواجب بنفس الوجوب وواجب الأداء، الأداء غير ما سبق (وقيل) الأداء فعل ابتدائه كالتحريمة عند الحنفية وركعة عند الشافعية في وقته المقدر شرعاً ليدخل ما شرع في الوقت وأتم خارجه (ومنه) أي الأداء من (الإعادة، وهو الفعل فيه أي في وقته المقدر شرعاً :(ثانياً : لخلل واقع في الفعل الأول غير الفساد كترك الفاتحة على مذهبنا واختلف فيه قيل : مندوب لأن الصلاة الواجبة قد تمت، فلا جهة للوجوب مرة أخرى والأصح أنه (واجب) إذا كان الخلل أداءها مع كراهة تحريم، كترك الواجب، لأن الذمة بقيت مشغولة بهذا الواجب المتروك، فلا بد من أدائه وإذا لم يعرف

فواتح الرحموت شرح مسلم الثبوت الجزء الأول
قربة إلا في ضمن صلاة فوجبت الصلاة ليكون الواجب مؤدى، فتكون هذه جابرة للأولى التي وقعت فرداً خلافاً لأبي اليسر فإنه يقول : الثاني يصير فرضاً. وهذا له وجه أيضاً (والقضاء فعله بعده أي فعل الواجب بعد الوقت المقدر شرعاً واستدراكاً لما فات عمداً أو سهواً تمكن من فعله، كالمسافر ، أو لم يتمكن لمانع شرعاً، كالحيض، أو عقلاً كالنوم) فعلى هذه العبادة الغير الموقتة ليست أداء ولا قضاء، إذ لا وقت مقدر هناك فتسمية الحج الصحيح بعد الفاسد قضاء مجاز فإنه وإن كان فعلاً في غير وقته الذي وجب إتمامه فيه بالإحرام، لكن ليس ذلك وقته المقدر شرعاً ومن جعل الأداء والقضاء في غير الواجب بدل الواجب) في التعريف (بالعبادة) وقال : الأداء فعل العبادة في وقته الخ والقضاء فعل العبادة في غير وقته، ثم إن هذين التفسيرين للأداء والقضاء لا يشملان الحقوق العبادية، وكذا تفسير القضاء للقضاء بمثل غير معقول، والتفسير الجامع ما قال الإمام فخر الإسلام الأداء تسليم عين الثابت بالأمر، كالعبادة في وقتها وتسليم عين المغصوب والقضاء تسليم مثل الثابت بالأمر، كالصوم للصوم، أو الفدية له في حق الشيخ الفاني، وتسليم قيمة الفرس المغصوب وأوردها هنا تقسيمات
،
وتعريفات كما هو دأبه الشريف أعرضنا عنها مخافة أن يطول الكلام.
Issue: [Sections of the wajib]. The wajib is two types: performance and make-up (ada and qada). (Ada: performing the wajib in its time estimated by Shariah.) It includes what is performed in the last part of time and other than it, so it encompasses the wajib by itself and the wajib of performance. Ada is other than what preceded. (It is said) Ada is the act of beginning it, like the takbira (tahrima) according to the Hanafis and one rak'ah according to the Shafi'is, in its time estimated by Shariah, so as to include what one starts in the time and completes outside it. (From it) i.e., from ada (comes) i'ada (repetition), which is the act in it, i.e., in its time estimated by Shariah, a second time due to a defect occurring in the first act other than invalidity, like leaving the Fatiha according to our madhhab. It is disputed: some say it is recommended because the obligatory prayer is already complete, so there is no ground for obligation again. The correct view is that it is obligatory if the defect is performing it with abominable prohibition, like leaving a wajib, because the liability remained occupied with this left wajib, so it must be performed. And if it is not known to be a means of closeness except within a prayer, then the prayer becomes obligatory so that the wajib is performed, so this second prayer becomes compensatory for the first that occurred deficient, contrary to Abu al-Yusr, who says the second becomes obligatory (fard). This also has a face. (And qada: performing it after it, i.e., performing the wajib after the time estimated by Shariah, as a recovery of what was missed intentionally or forgetfully while able to do it, like the traveler, or unable due to a Shariah impediment like menstruation, or mentally like sleep.) According to this, a non-timed act of worship is neither ada nor qada, since there is no estimated time there. Thus calling a valid Hajj after an invalid one 'qada' is metaphorical, for although it is an act outside its time that must be completed by ihram, that time is not the Shariah-estimated time. And whoever makes ada and qada apply to non-obligatory acts replaces 'wajib' with 'act of worship' in the definition and says: ada is performing the act of worship in its time, etc., and qada is performing the act of worship outside its time. Then these two interpretations of ada and qada do not cover the rights between servants (huquq 'ibadiyya), similarly the interpretation of qada as 'like' is unintelligible. The comprehensive interpretation is what Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: Ada is delivering the exact thing that is established by command, like the act of worship in its time and delivering the exact usurped property. Qada is delivering the equivalent of what is established by command, like fasting for fasting, or compensation for it in the case of the decrepit old man, and delivering the value of a usurped horse. He presented here divisions and definitions as is his noble habit; we have refrained from them for fear of lengthening the discussion.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on the types of wajib (obligatory acts). It defines ada (performance) as doing the wajib within its Shariah-appointed time. It notes that this includes both the general wujub and the wujub al-ada. An alternate definition from others says ada is the beginning of the act (like the takbira for Hanafis or a rak'ah for Shafi'is) within the time. Then it defines i'ada (repetition) as doing the same act again within the same time due to a defect like omitting a wajib such as Fatiha. The Hanafi view is that i'ada is obligatory if the act was deficient, because the liability is not discharged. Abu al-Yusr says the second prayer becomes a new fard. Then qada (make-up) is defined as performing the wajib after its time, whether missed intentionally or due to an excuse. Non-timed worships are not ada or qada. There is a discussion about the scope: some replace 'wajib' with 'ibada' in definitions. The comprehensive definition given by Imam Fakhr al-Islam differentiates between ada (delivering the exact thing) and qada (delivering its equivalent). The author refrains from further divisions to avoid length.

Key Terms
الأداء (al-ada): Performing the wajib in its Shariah-appointed time.
القضاء (al-qada): Performing the wajib after its appointed time as a recovery.
الإعادة (al-i'ada): Repeating the wajib within its time due to a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on i'ada when a wajib element (like al-Fatiha) is omitted in prayer?
A) It is forbidden (haram).
B) It is recommended (mandub).
C) It is obligatory (wajib).
D) It is permissible but not required (mubah).

Key Takeaway
Ada, qada, and i'ada are fundamental categories for understanding when an obligation is fulfilled, when it must be repeated, and when it is made up after the time.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced

Delaying a Broad Wajib with Expectation of Death
Ruling on delaying a time-flexible obligation when one expects death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why, according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, delaying a broad wajib with expectation of death is not sinful, and evaluate the counterarguments against this position.

Core Idea
No sin in delaying a broad wajib before its final time, even if expecting death, because the address is not yet binding.

This passage delves into a nuanced usul al-fiqh question: if someone has a time-flexible obligation (like Dhuhr prayer) and expects to die before the time ends, does delaying constitute a sin? The author presents the Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, that it does not, and critically examines the alleged consensus and other arguments to the contrary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
Recognize the case: a person has a broad wajib (e.g., praying Dhuhr anytime between zawal and asr) and delays it with a strong expectation of death before the time ends.
The key is that the wajib is 'broad' (muwassa'), not restricted to a specific moment.
2
State the Claimed Consensus and Hanafi Rejection
The text begins by noting a claim that such delay is sinful by consensus. Then it challenges the validity of this consensus and argues that on the Hanafi view it is not sinful.
Imam Fakhr al-Islam is cited as the authority for the Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Reasoning
The Hanafi argument: the Lawgiver granted a choice (takhyeer) within the entire time, so no demand exists before the final moment. Therefore, no sin is incurred if one delays and dies before the final time.
This hinges on the nature of the address (khitab) – it only targets the final time for performance.
4
Evaluate the Counterarguments
Two opposing arguments are addressed: (1) that broadening is conditional on not expecting death, and (2) that the delay reflects a corrupt determination (azima fasida). Both are refuted: the first changes the text without proof; the second fails because the determination is to a permissible act.
The refutation relies on the principle that permissible acts do not generate sin.
5
Critique the Hashiya Interpretation
The author rejects a marginal note (hashiya) that tries to argue the final time can be considered subjectively based on expectation. This is deemed an unacceptable interpretation without transmitted evidence, and leading to logical contradictions (e.g., implying sin even with expectation of safety).
The critique underscores the importance of relying on the text (nass) and not subjective factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرع
(تأخير (الفعل) الواجب الموسع (مع ظن الموت في جزء من الوقت معصية اتفاقاً) لا لدعوى الاتفاق من دليل ولا يستقيم الحكم بالمعصية على رأينا كيف ولم يتوجه الخطاب عندنا في غير الآخر، ولا معصية من غير مخالفة الخطاب، قال الإمام فخر الإسلام وفي مسألتنا لم توجد المطالبة بدلالة أن الشرع خيره في وقت الأداء فلا يلزمه الأداء إلا أن يسقط خياره بالتضيق للوقت، ولهذا قلنا : إذا مات قبل آخر الوقت لا شيء عليه، ثم هذه الدعوى لا تستقيم على القول بالوجوب للأداء موسعاً أيضاً، وإن تعلق الخطاب في أول الوقت، فإن الشارع وسع إلى الآخر، فالتأخير جائز، ولا معصية في الجائز، والقول بأن التوسيع ليس إلا عند عدم الظن بالموت تغيير للنص، فلا بد لذلك من دليل، والقول بأن المعصية لفساد العزيمة كالعزم لترك الواجب، وإن كان أقرب من الأول، لكنه غير صحيح، إذ لا فساد في العزيمة ها هنا، فإنه ما عزم إلا بالترك الجائز ومن ها هنا ظهر لك فساد ما في «الحاشية»، أقول: فيه دليل على أن الآخر الذي يتعين للسببية ويتضيق به الموسع أعم من أن يكون بحسب الواقع، أو باعتبار ظن المكلف، فالموت يجعل البعض كلاً انتهى ؛ وذلك لأن هذا تأويل لا يسمع من غير موجب مأثور، فكيف مخالفة كلام مثل هذا الإمام فخر الإسلام، ثم يرد عليه أنه حينئذ مع يلزم أن يعصي من أخر مع ظن السلامة، لأنه عند الموت تضيق الواجب كما قلت والترك التضيق يوجب الإثم ولا يتجه الجواب بأن لو قيل إن الإثم إنما يلزم بترك الواجب في حين الوقت كله وها هنا ترك في البعض بالاختيار، وفي البعض بالموت لا أنه ترك في كل الوقت بالاختيار، لأن الموت جعل البعض كلاً مع أنه يجري فيما إذا أخر بظن الموت، وكذا لا يصح
Subsidiary issue: Delaying the broad wajib with the expectation of death in a part of the time is a sin by consensus. There is no claim of consensus from a proof. And the ruling of sin is not sound according to our view. How? The address has not been directed in our view in other than the final time, and there is no sin without violation of the address. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: In our issue, the demand is not found, indicated by the fact that the Lawgiver gave him a choice in the time of performance, so performance is not incumbent upon him unless his choice is canceled by the restriction of time. For this reason we said: if he dies before the final time, nothing is upon him. Then this claim is not sound on the view that there is an obligation of performance broadly also, even if the address is attached at the beginning of the time, for the Lawgiver broadened it until the final time, so delay is permissible, and there is no sin in the permissible. The statement that broadening is only when there is no expectation of death is a change of the text, so it requires a proof. The statement that the sin is due to the corruption of determination, like determination to leave a wajib, although closer than the first, is not correct, for there is no corruption of determination here, because he only determined to do a permissible leaving. From here the corruption of what is in the 'hashiya' appeared. I say: In it there is proof that the final part that becomes definite for causality and by which the broad becomes restricted is more general than whether it is according to reality or according to the responsible person's expectation. For death makes a part become the whole. End. This is because this is an interpretation not heard without a transmitted reason. How can it contradict the words of such an Imam as Fakhr al-Islam? Then it is objected to him that then it follows that one who delays with expectation of safety would sin, because at death the wajib becomes restricted as you said, and leaving the restricted necessitates sin. And the answer that sin only applies by leaving the wajib in the entire time, and here he left part by choice and part by death, not that he left the entire time by choice, is not valid, because death makes the part as the whole, and this is similar to the case when he delays with expectation of death. Likewise, it is not sound.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a subsidiary ruling (far') addressing a specific case: delaying a broad (wasi') wajib, such as praying Dhuhr anytime before the end of its time, when one has an expectation of death before the time expires. The author states that it is sinful by consensus, but then critically examines whether this consensus holds on the Hanafi view. According to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, there is no demand to perform before the final time, so if one delays and dies, no sin is incurred. The objection arises: if delay with expectation of death is sinful, then the broadening is conditional on not expecting death, which changes the text. Another argument links sin to corrupt determination (azima fasida), but that is also refuted. The author then critiques a marginal note (hashiya) that tries to argue that the final time can be considered in terms of the person's expectation. He deems this interpretation unacceptable without transmitted evidence and shows its logical flaws, such as implying that delaying with expectation of safety would also be sinful. The text ends abruptly mid-argument.

Key Terms
الواجب الموسع (al-wajib al-muwassa'): An obligatory act with a broad time frame, where performance is valid anytime within that frame.
ظن الموت (zann al-mawt): A high probability or expectation of death before the end of the time, which may affect the ruling.
العزيمة (al-azima): The firm determination to perform or omit an act; in this context, determination to delay the wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, what is the key reason that delaying a broad wajib with expectation of death is not sinful?
A) The person may repent before dying
B) The Lawgiver gave a choice in the time of performance
C) The person's expectation of death is uncertain
D) The obligation is not actual until the final moment

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on a broad wajib is that the address does not target any specific moment within the time, so delaying before the final time, even with expectation of death, does not incur sin.

Definitions of Ada and Qada in Hanafi Usul
Defining ada and qada for worship and rights of people

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definitions of ada and qada to both acts of worship and rights of people, and identify which definition covers a given scenario.

Core Idea
Ada is delivering the exact commanded thing; qada is delivering its substitute — both cover worship and rights.

In this passage, the author critiques narrow definitions that restrict ada and qada to worship only. He presents the comprehensive definition of Imam Fakhr al-Islam, which encompasses both acts of worship and rights of people (huquq al-ibadi). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly categorizing legal obligations and their substitutes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with narrow definitions
The author points out that definitions limited to worship exclude rights of people. Consider why this is a problem.
2
Examine the standard definition of qada
Qada is performing an obligation after its legally determined time due to intentional or unintentional omission. Note that it requires ability or valid excuse.
3
Understand the critique of the narrowed scope
The author argues that such definitions do not cover rights because rights like returning usurped goods are not time-bound acts.
4
Learn Imam Fakhr al-Islam's comprehensive definitions
Ada: delivering the exact thing commanded (e.g., prayer in its time, actual usurped good). Qada: delivering a similar thing (e.g., fasting in place of fasting, value of usurped horse).
Apply to both worship and rights.
5
Note the metaphorical extension
The author mentions that calling hajj after a spoiled one 'qada' is metaphorical because it is not a make-up of a timed duty but a completion.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The comprehensive interpretation is what Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: ada is the delivery of the very thing established by command, like the worship in its time and the delivery of the very thing usurped; and qada is the delivery of a similar thing to that established by command, like fasting for fasting, or the ransom for it in the case of the decrepit old man, and the delivery of the value of the usurped horse. Commentary
This passage is a usul definition lesson. The author defines the terms 'ada' (performance) and 'qada' (make-up) in the context of Hanafi legal theory. He begins by discussing a scenario where a prayer is performed to complete a previous defective one, noting Abu al-Yusr's opinion that the second becomes obligatory. He then provides a standard definition of qada: performing an obligatory act after its time due to intentional or unintentional omission. However, he points out that this definition does not apply to non-timed acts of worship like hajj after a spoiled one; such naming is metaphorical. The author criticizes narrow definitions that limit ada and qada to acts of worship, as they exclude rights of people (huquq al-ibadi). He then presents the comprehensive definition of Imam Fakhr al-Islam: ada is delivering the exact item commanded (like worship in its time or the actual usurped good), while qada is delivering a similar item (like fasting in place of fasting or the value of a usurped horse). The author refrains from further divisions to avoid lengthening the discussion.

Key Terms
الأداء (al-ada'): Delivery of the very thing established by command, e.g., worship in its time or the actual usurped good.
القضاء (al-qada'): Delivery of a similar thing to that established by command, e.g., fasting in place of fasting or the value of a usurped horse.

Key Takeaway
Ada and qada in Hanafi usul are defined broadly to include both acts of worship and rights of people, with ada being the exact delivery and qada being the substitute.

Delaying Wajib Musawwa' with Fear of Death
Dispute on delaying broadly-timed wajib with fear of death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delaying a broadly-timed wajib when fearing death, identify the opposing view, and apply the principle of expansion (tawsi'ah) to determine that no sin occurs.

Core Idea
Fear of death does not make delay sinful; expansion remains until the time's end.

This passage tackles a subtle question in usul al-fiqh: does the fear of death transform the permissibility of delaying a broadly-timed obligation into sin? The Hanafi school insists it does not, because the obligation only attaches to the final part of the time. The passage refutes opposing claims and clarifies that the legal time is not altered by subjective fear. Understanding this ruling guards against the common error of imposing premature obligations based on mere conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The text opens with a claim that delaying a broadly-timed wajib with fear of death is a sin 'by consensus.' Recognize that this is the position being challenged. The Hanafi school rejects it.
The 'consensus' is asserted without proof; the Hanafi response will dismantle it.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
According to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, there is no sin because the obligation only becomes specific at the very end of the time. The Lawgiver gave the mukallaf a choice to perform at any point within the extended period. Fear of death does not remove that choice.
The key evidence: the shar' extended the time without conditioning it on safety from death.
3
Examine the opposing argument
The opponent claims that fear of death contracts the time, making the obligation specific earlier. They analogize it to a situation where the time is actually constricted. Al-Qadi even says that if the person survives and prays, it becomes qada'.
This argument relies on treating 'zann al-mawt' as equivalent to actual constriction.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the legal time is defined by the Lawgiver, not by the mukallaf's estimation. Fear does not change the ruling unless evidence proves so. The condition of safety (salama) is not a condition for permissibility; expansion inherently includes the risk of death.
If the condition were safety, then no one could safely delay, leading to an impossible obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the person delays with fear of death and survives, according to the Hanafi school it is ada' (done in time), while according to al-Qadi it would be qada' (make-up). If the person dies before the end, no sin or liability in either view.
Ibn al-Humam notes the dispute is verbal if qada' does not require specific intention.

Source Text

Branch: Delaying the broadly-timed obligatory act with fear of death in part of the time is a sin by consensus — not due to a claim of consensus without evidence, and the ruling of sin is not sound according to our view, how can it when the address does not apply to any other than the final part? And there is no sin without violating the address. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: In our issue, there is no demand, indicated by the fact that the Lawgiver gave him a choice in the time of performance; therefore performance is not obligatory unless his choice is removed by the constriction of time. Hence we say: if he dies before the final part of time, nothing is incumbent upon him. Then this claim is not sound even on the view that obligation attaches at the beginning of the time, for the Lawgiver extended the time to the end; delaying is permissible, and there is no sin in the permissible. The statement that extension only applies when there is no fear of death is a change to the text, requiring proof. The statement that sin is due to the corruption of resolve, like intending to abandon the obligation, although closer to the first, is not correct, for there is no corruption of resolve here; he only intended a permissible postponement. From here it appears what is in the 'Hashiyah' is corruption. I say: in it is evidence that the final part which becomes specific for causality and by which the broadly-timed is constricted is general — whether it is based on reality or the estimation of the responsible person; death makes a part a totality. End. That is because this interpretation is not accepted without a transmitted reason; how can it contradict the words of Imam Fakhr al-Islam? Then it is replied that then it necessarily follows that one who delays with fear of safety commits sin, because at death the obligation becomes constricted as you said, and abandoning during constriction necessitates sin. And the answer is not that sin only results from abandoning the obligation during the entire time, and here he abandoned part by choice and part by death — it is not that he abandoned the whole time by choice, because death makes a part a totality, and it applies when one delays due to fear of death. Likewise, the answer that death is not a cause for sin does not work, because we did not make death a cause; rather, his choice to abandon during his entire time. And the answer that the one who makes a part a totality is the fear of death, not death itself, is not sound because he is asked to distinguish; how? Death is total inability, while fear, if proven false, has no inability, so death is more deserving of making a part a totality than fear. The truth is what we said: there is no sin at all. (If he does not die) and his fear proves false (and performs it in its time, then the majority hold it is ada, because the definition applies: it is performed in the legally determined time. (And al-Qadi said: it is qada, because its legal time according to his estimation was before, since when he feared death, performance before was determined. Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said this is far-fetched if he requires the intention of qada; otherwise the dispute is verbal. And it is refuted by the case of one who believes the time has ended before its entry and delays; he is certainly sinful because he intended to oppose the command. (If the error becomes clear and he performs) in the time, it is ada by agreement, and it follows that it is qada, because its legally determined time was before, since he was sinful for delaying. (I say: the difference between what al-Qadi said in that case and this case is clear: in the first, there is belief of absence of time absolutely — neither time for qada nor ada; in the second, belief of absence of time for ada only. So the first is constricted from every aspect, so it accommodates neither ada nor qada, unlike the second; thus the difference is clear. (So contemplate) — an indication that it does not help, because al-Qadi's cause exists here too: that its legal time becomes before the estimation, otherwise he would not be sinful for delaying. So the truth, on the view of sin, is that the estimation is considered unless error appears; when error appears, the ruling returns to the original, and the performance occurs in the time without doubt. (Whoever delays with fear of safety and dies suddenly, the correct view is that he does not sin, even if it is said he does sin, because delay is permissible for one who fears safety, and no sin in the permissible. The statement that the condition for permissibility—i.e., permissibility of delaying to the end—is safety of the outcome, and if he dies suddenly, the condition has failed, so he should sin, is refuted by the fact that it would entail obligation of the impossible, as safety of outcome is unknown; whereas fear is present in this case, so based on it, delay is permissible, so no sin. And it was said: permissibility is not a matter of obligation, so there is no obligation of the impossible; but there is something in it, for although it is not obligation, it is permission, and there is no permission in the impossible. The author indicated the weakness of this refutation by saying: (the condition for permissibility is safety itself, not knowledge of safety, so it does not lead to obligation of the impossible, for knowledge of safety is normally impossible, but safety itself is real.) This view entails (choosing between the possible, which is delay in safety, and the impossible, which is delay when there is no safety, because the conditional is impossible when the condition is absent. This choice removes the reality of expansion. So ponder: expansion requires that the responsible person be free to delay; this choice requires that he be cautious and not delay. Therefore, one must rely on fear, so delay is permissible, so no sin. If you say: this is refuted by the life-long obligation, for delay in it is permissible when there is safety; also, the purpose of stipulating safety is to allow delay on condition of not abandoning; if one dies in the middle of the time before performance, abandonment is found, and there is no choice between possible and impossible. I say: the text exists in the broadly-timed regarding permissibility of delay to the end of the time without condition, so if safety of outcome is stipulated, it entails an addition, and delay would be permissible in some times and not others, and a choice between delay with performance and delay with safety, and likewise delay with death.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a usul rule and dispute lesson regarding the ruling on delaying a broadly-timed obligatory act (wajib musawwa') when one fears death in part of the time. The author begins by stating that it is claimed as sin by consensus, but he rejects this. He explains that according to Imam Fakhr al-Islam, there is no sin because the obligation only becomes specific at the end of the time; the person has a choice to delay until then. If he dies before, nothing is due. The author then critiques several arguments: (1) that extension depends on no fear of death — this changes the text; (2) that sin is due to corrupt intention — but the intention is permissible postponement. He addresses the view of al-Qadi that if the person survives and performs it, it is qada, but the majority say it is ada. Ibn al-Humam remarks that the dispute is verbal if qada intention is not required. The author then discusses the case of one who delays with fear of safety and dies suddenly; the correct view is no sin, because the condition for permissibility is safety of outcome, but requiring knowledge of safety leads to impossible obligation. He concludes that expansion requires reliance on the fear, making delay permissible and no sin. The text ends with a rebuttal to an objection regarding life-long obligation.

Key Terms
الواجب الموسع (al-wajib al-musawwa'): A duty whose performance can be fulfilled at any time within a broad period, e.g., the five daily prayers within their times.
ظن الموت (zann al-mawt): A strong presumption that one will die before the end of the time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on one who delays a broadly-timed wajib with fear of death and then dies before the end of the time?
A) He is sinful
B) He is not sinful (no sin)
C) It is considered qada
D) He must make up the prayer

Key Takeaway
Fear of death does not transform permissible delay into sin; the broadly-timed wajib remains expansive until its final moment.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
15 min
No Sin for Delaying with Expectation of Safety Even if Death Occurs
Ruling on delaying with expectation of safety and sudden death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delaying an expanded obligatory act with expectation of safety followed by sudden death, and explain why the condition of eventual safety is rejected using the argument of taklif bi al-muhal (obligation of the impossible).

Core Idea
Sin is judged at the time of action; sudden death does not retroactively make permissible delay sinful.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut investigates a delicate case: a person delays a time-bound obligatory act (e.g., Fajr prayer) based on a reasonable expectation of safety, but dies suddenly before performing. Does this person incur sin? The Hanafi answer is no, and the reasoning hinges on the nature of legal expansion (tawassu') and the principle that permissibility (jawaz) does not cause sin. The text refutes opposing views that condition permissibility on eventual safety, showing that such a condition leads to impossible obligation and undermines the very concept of expandability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenario and ruling
The passage asks: if a person delays an expanded wajib (e.g., prayer) expecting safety but dies suddenly, do they sin? The Hanafi answer is: no sin, because the delay was permissible based on the expectation.
The ruling is unconditional: even if death occurs, no sin.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that the condition for permissible delay is actual safety of outcome; since death negates that condition, the delay becomes impermissible retroactively, and thus sinful.
This view conditions permissibility on an unknowable future event.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation: taklif bi al-muhal
The Hanafi response: if permissibility required actual safety, then no one could know whether delay is permissible until after death, leading to an impossible obligation (تكليف بالمحال). The expectation (zann) is a valid basis because it is knowable at the time of action.
The refutation also points out that stipulating knowledge of safety is impossible customarily.
4
Connect to the concept of tawassu'
The text argues that expandability (tawassu') grants the mukallaf genuine freedom to delay until the end. If permissibility were conditional on safety, delay would only be permissible when safety is certain, which removes the essence of expansion.
Tawassu' is a real legal flexibility, not a mere theoretical option.
5
Apply the reasoning to a concrete example
Consider a person who delays Zuhr prayer until late afternoon expecting to be safe, but dies suddenly. According to the Hanafi view, no sin because the delay was permissible at the time it was made.
The outcome does not retroactively change the ruling.

بأن شرط الجواز أي جواز التأخير إلى الآخر سلامة) العاقبة إليه، وإذا مات فجأة فقد فات شرط الجواز للتأخير، فينبغي أن يأثم، ورد بأنه يلزم حينئذ التكليف بالمحال إذ لا تعلم سلامة العاقبة، وأما الظن فهو متحقق في هذه الصورة، فعلی اعتباره جاز التأخير فلا يأثم، وقيل: الجواز ليس من باب التكليف، فلا تكليف بالممتنع وفيه ما فيه، فإنه وإن لم يكن تكليفاً لكنه إباحة ولا إباحة في الممتنع وأشار المصنف إلى ضعف هذا الردّ بقوله (لا العلم به أي شرط جواز التأخير نفس السلامة لا العلم بالسلامة (حتى يؤدي إلى التكليف بالمحال فإن العلم بالسلامة محال عادة، وأما نفس السلامة فواقع (يقتضي) هذا القول (التخيير بين الممكن وهو التأخير حال السلامة والممتنع وهو) التأخير عند عدم السلامة، لاستحالة المشروط عند عدم الشرط (وهو) أي التخيير على هذا الوجه (يرفع حقيقة التوسع فتدبر فإن التوسع يقتضي أن يتخير المكلف في التأخير، وهذا التخيير يقتضي أن يحتاط ولا يؤخر، فإذن لا بد من البناء على الظن فجاز التأخير فلا إثم، فإن قلت: هذا منقوض بالواجب العمري، فإنه يجوز فيه التأخير عند السلامة وأيضاً: المقصود من اشتراط السلامة أن يجوز التأخير بشرط عدم الترك، فلو مات في وسط الوقت قبل الأداء وجد تركه، وليس فيه التخيير بين ممكن وممتنع قلت قد وجد النص في الموسع على جواز التأخير إلى آخر الوقت من غير فصل، فلو شرط سلامة العاقبة لزم الزيادة عليه، وكان التأخير جائزاً في وقت دون وقت، وتخييراً بين التأخير مع الأداء ومع السلامة، والتأخير كذلك مع الموت، وهذا يرفع حقيقة التوسع المفهوم من النص، وهذا بخلاف الواجب العمري، إذ لا نص فيه على التأخير إلى آخر الوقت بل التأخير مقيد فيه، بشرط عدم الإخلال، وإلا جاز الترك عمداً من غير عذر إلى أن يموت وهذا رافع حقيقة الوجوب فافهم (وفرق) الشيخ (ابن الحاجب بين ما وقته العمر كالحج فيعصي بالتأخير، وإن كان مع ظن السلامة والموت فجأة (وبين غيره) أي غير ما وقته العمر، وهو الواجب الموسع فلا يعصي بالتأخير مع ظن السلامة وإن مات فجأة (ليس بسديد لأن الوجوب مشترك بين الواجب العمري والموسع، فإن كان سبب العصيان في الأول الوجوب فينبغي أن يعصي في الثاني أيضاً وعذر الفجأة عام) فيهما، فلو قبل عذر الفجأة في الموسع قبل في العمري فلا فرق وفيه ما فيه) فإن الفرق ظاهر، لأن العمري وقته العمر كله فالوجوب فيه يقتضي أن يعصي بالتأخير عن العمر، فإذا أخر الحج مثلاً ومات فجأة فقد تركه في تمام وقته المقدر وهو إثم، وإلا ارتفع الوجوب بخلاف الواجب الموسع، فإنه إذا مات قبل الآخر فما تركه في تمام وقته بتقصير منه، لكن حينئذ ينبغي أن لا فيما إذا ظن الموت، وأيضاً الوجوب ووجوب الأداء مفترقان في الموسع، فلا يأثم قبل الآخر لعدم وجوب الأداء، وفي العمرى لا ينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر فيأثم بالترك، هذا والله الغفور للمأثم يغفر لمن يشاء ويعذب من يشاء.
The answer is that death is not a cause for disobedience, so he does not sin because we did not make death a cause, rather his leaving (the act) by his own choice in its entire time. Similarly, the answer that making part as whole is only the thought of death, not death itself, is not valid because he is required to differentiate: how? Death is inability entirely, while thought has no inability if it turns out false. So death is more deserving than thought. This is the correct view we stated: no sin at all. (If he did not die) and his thought proved false (and he performed it in its time), then the majority say it is ada because the definition applies to it: he performed it in the time estimated by law. (Al-Qadi said: it is qada, because its time by law according to his thought was before, because when he thought death, performance before became incumbent. Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said this is unlikely if he says obligation of intention of qada, otherwise the dispute is verbal. It is objected to him: if he believes the time has ended before its entry and delays, then he definitely sins due to intending to oppose the command. (When the error becomes clear and he performs) in the time, it is ada by agreement, but it follows that it is qada because its estimated time by law was before such that he sinned by leaving in it. (I say: the difference between what al-Qadi said and this case is clear: in the first, there is absolute belief in absence of time) — neither time of qada nor time of ada, (and in the second, belief in absence of time of ada) only. So the first is constrained in every aspect, so neither ada nor qada is possible, unlike the second, hence the difference is clear. (So reflect) — an indication that it does not avail, because al-Qadi's cause exists here too: his time by law becoming before the thought, otherwise he would not have sinned by delay. Therefore, the truth on the view of sin is that they should say: the thought is considered as long as error does not appear, and when it appears, the ruling returns to the original, and the performed act occurs in time without doubt. (And whoever delays with expectation of safety and dies suddenly, the verification is that he does not sin, even if it is said that he sins, because delay) for one expecting safety (is permissible, and there is no sin for the permissible. And the statement that the condition for permissibility — i.e., permissibility of delay to the end — is safety of the outcome, and if he dies suddenly, the condition for permissibility of delay has been lost, so he should sin, is refuted by the fact that it would entail impossible obligation since the safety of the outcome is not known. As for the thought, it is realized in this case, so upon its consideration, delay is permissible, so he does not sin. And it is said: permissibility is not a matter of obligation, so there is no obligation of the impossible, but there is something in this: for even if it is not an obligation, it is permission, and there is no permission in the impossible. The author pointed to the weakness of this refutation by saying: (Not knowledge of it) — i.e., the condition for permissibility of delay is safety itself, not knowledge of safety — (such that it leads to obligation of the impossible; for knowledge of safety is impossible customarily, but safety itself is actual. (This statement requires) (choice between the possible — delay in case of safety, and the impossible —) delay in case of absence of safety, because the conditioned is impossible when the condition is absent. (And this) — i.e., choice in this manner — (removes the reality of expandability. So ponder, for expandability requires that the obligated person be free to choose delay, whereas this choice requires that he be cautious and not delay. So then one must build on the thought, so delay is permissible, no sin. If you say: this is contradicted by the lifelong obligation, for delay is permissible there with safety; also the purpose of stipulating safety is that delay is permissible on condition of non-abandonment, so if he dies in the middle of the time before performance, his abandonment is found, and there is no choice between possible and impossible. I say: there is a text regarding the expanded obligation about permissibility of delay to the end of its time without exception. If safety of outcome were stipulated, an increase would be required, and delay would be permissible at one time and not another, and a choice between delay with performance and with safety, and delay similarly with death, and this removes the reality of expandability understood from the text. This is unlike the lifelong obligation, for there is no text there about delay to the end of time; rather, delay is restricted there with the condition of non-violation, otherwise intentional abandonment without excuse until death becomes permissible, and this removes the reality of obligation. So understand. (And Sheikh Ibn al-Hajib differentiated between what has a lifetime time like hajj, in which he sins by delay even with expectation of safety and sudden death,) (and other than it) — i.e., other than what has lifetime time, which is the expanded obligation, so he does not sin by delay with expectation of safety even if he dies suddenly. (This is not sound, because obligation is common between lifelong and expanded obligations: if the cause of sin in the first is obligation, then he should sin in the second as well, and the excuse of sudden death is common in both. So if the excuse of sudden death is accepted in the expanded, it should be accepted in the lifelong, so no difference. There is something in it: because the difference is apparent, since the lifelong obligation's time is the entire life, so the obligation requires that he sin by delay beyond life. So if he delays hajj for example and dies suddenly, he has abandoned it in its entire estimated time, which is sin; otherwise obligation would be removed. Unlike the expanded obligation, for if he dies before the end, he has not abandoned it in its entire time through negligence. But then it should not be [sin?] when he thought death. Also, obligation and obligation of performance are distinct in the expanded, so he does not sin before the end due to lack of obligation of performance, whereas in the lifelong, one is not separated from the other, so he sins by abandonment. This, and Allah is the Forgiver of sins, He forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a person who delays a time-bound obligatory act (e.g., prayer) due to expecting death (or safety) and then either dies or finds the thought false. The central question is whether such delay incurs sin. The author affirms the Hanafi position that there is no sin if the delay was based on a reasonable expectation (ظن) of safety, because death is not a cause of disobedience — the person chose to delay within the entire time. The text addresses several objections: (1) The argument that death is inability and thus the person should have performed earlier is rejected because the person acted on a valid thought; death does not retroactively make the delay sinful. (2) If the thought proves false and the person performs in the remaining time, the majority hold it is 'ada' (timely performance), while al-Qadi holds it is 'qada' (make-up) because the person thought the time had passed. The author clarifies the difference: in al-Qadi's case, the person believes the time has absolutely passed (no ada or qada possible), whereas here only the time for ada is thought to have passed. (3) Regarding sudden death after delaying with expectation of safety, the investigation is that there is no sin. The condition for permissibility of delay is safety itself, not knowledge of safety. Stipulating knowledge would lead to impossible obligation. The discussion concludes that the nature of expansion (توسع) in a broad obligation (واجب موسع) requires that the person be free to delay until the end, and imposing a condition of eventual safety undermines that freedom. The text also distinguishes the lifelong obligation (واجب عمري) like Hajj, where delay past the entire life incurs sin, unlike the expanded obligation where sin occurs only if one delays past the entire time with negligence. The author refutes Ibn al-Hajib's attempt to differentiate the two types, showing that the key distinction lies in the nature of obligation and the time frame.

Key Terms
التوسع (al-tawassu'): The legal flexibility that allows the obligated person to perform an obligatory act at any time within the entire time frame without immediate obligation of performance at the beginning.
الواجب العمري (al-wajib al-'umri): An obligation whose entire time is the person's lifetime, such as Hajj; delay past the entire time constitutes abandonment.
ظن السلامة (zann al-salamah): The expectation that one will survive until the end of the time; considered a valid basis for delaying an expanded obligation.
تكليف بالمحال (taklif bi al-muhal): Obligation of the impossible: requiring someone to do something that is not within their power or knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi investigation in this passage, a person who delays Fajr prayer until just before sunrise based on a strong expectation of safety, then dies suddenly before performing, is considered:
A) Sinful because death occurred
B) Not sinful because the delay was permissible based on his expectation
C) Sinful only if the expectation was based on false hope
D) The ruling is unknown until the afterlife

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, delaying an expanded wajib with a reasonable expectation of safety does not incur sin, even if sudden death occurs, because permissibility is judged at the time of action, not by outcome.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
15 min
Distinguishing Expanded vs. Lifetime Obligations (Wajib Mawsu' vs. Wajib 'Umri)
Distinction between expanded and lifetime obligations regarding sin of delay with expectation of safety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an expanded obligation (wajib mawsu') and a lifetime obligation (wajib 'umri) in terms of the ruling on delaying with expectation of safety and sudden death, and evaluate the critique of Ibn al-Hajib's attempted differentiation.

Core Idea
In the expanded obligation, obligation and obligation of performance are distinct; in the lifetime obligation, they are inseparable, which affects the sin of delay.

The passage extends the discussion to compare expanded obligations (like prayer) with lifetime obligations (like Hajj). Some scholars, including Ibn al-Hajib, argue that in the latter, delay with expectation of safety followed by death does incur sin. The text critiques this view and offers a nuanced distinction based on the nature of obligation and performance. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly applying rulings across different types of wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of obligations
The passage distinguishes expanded obligations (واجب موسع) whose time is a defined period (e.g., prayer window) from lifetime obligations (واجب عمري) whose time is the entire life (e.g., Hajj).
The nature of the time frames leads to different implications for delay.
2
Understand Ibn al-Hajib's differentiation
Ibn al-Hajib held that in lifetime obligations, one sins by delay even with expectation of safety and death, but in expanded obligations, one does not.
He bases this on the idea that in 'umri, the entire life is the time, so death means the time ended with abandonment.
3
Analyze the critique of Ibn al-Hajib's view
The text critiques that obligation is common to both types, and the excuse of sudden death is common, so the difference is not sound unless further refined.
The critique challenges the logical consistency of differentiating based solely on the span of time.
4
Learn the Hanafi resolution: separation of obligation and obligation of performance
The Hanafis argue that in expanded obligations, the obligation to perform (wujub al-ada) does not arise until the end of the time; delay before that is not a violation. In lifetime obligations, the obligation and its performance are simultaneous from the start, so delay is a violation.
This is the key point: in mawsu', one can delay without sin because ada is not yet required; in 'umri, it is required from the outset.
5
Apply the distinction to the case of sudden death
Thus, if a person delays Hajj with expectation of safety and dies, they sin because they failed to perform a lifelong obligation. If they delay Fajr prayer and die, they do not sin because the obligation of performance had not yet become binding.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction and preserves the integrity of both types.

بأن شرط الجواز أي جواز التأخير إلى الآخر سلامة) العاقبة إليه، وإذا مات فجأة فقد فات شرط الجواز للتأخير، فينبغي أن يأثم، ورد بأنه يلزم حينئذ التكليف بالمحال إذ لا تعلم سلامة العاقبة، وأما الظن فهو متحقق في هذه الصورة، فعلی اعتباره جاز التأخير فلا يأثم، وقيل: الجواز ليس من باب التكليف، فلا تكليف بالممتنع وفيه ما فيه، فإنه وإن لم يكن تكليفاً لكنه إباحة ولا إباحة في الممتنع وأشار المصنف إلى ضعف هذا الردّ بقوله (لا العلم به أي شرط جواز التأخير نفس السلامة لا العلم بالسلامة (حتى يؤدي إلى التكليف بالمحال فإن العلم بالسلامة محال عادة، وأما نفس السلامة فواقع (يقتضي) هذا القول (التخيير بين الممكن وهو التأخير حال السلامة والممتنع وهو) التأخير عند عدم السلامة، لاستحالة المشروط عند عدم الشرط (وهو) أي التخيير على هذا الوجه (يرفع حقيقة التوسع فتدبر فإن التوسع يقتضي أن يتخير المكلف في التأخير، وهذا التخيير يقتضي أن يحتاط ولا يؤخر، فإذن لا بد من البناء على الظن فجاز التأخير فلا إثم، فإن قلت: هذا منقوض بالواجب العمري، فإنه يجوز فيه التأخير عند السلامة وأيضاً: المقصود من اشتراط السلامة أن يجوز التأخير بشرط عدم الترك، فلو مات في وسط الوقت قبل الأداء وجد تركه، وليس فيه التخيير بين ممكن وممتنع قلت قد وجد النص في الموسع على جواز التأخير إلى آخر الوقت من غير فصل، فلو شرط سلامة العاقبة لزم الزيادة عليه، وكان التأخير جائزاً في وقت دون وقت، وتخييراً بين التأخير مع الأداء ومع السلامة، والتأخير كذلك مع الموت، وهذا يرفع حقيقة التوسع المفهوم من النص، وهذا بخلاف الواجب العمري، إذ لا نص فيه على التأخير إلى آخر الوقت بل التأخير مقيد فيه، بشرط عدم الإخلال، وإلا جاز الترك عمداً من غير عذر إلى أن يموت وهذا رافع حقيقة الوجوب فافهم (وفرق) الشيخ (ابن الحاجب بين ما وقته العمر كالحج فيعصي بالتأخير، وإن كان مع ظن السلامة والموت فجأة (وبين غيره) أي غير ما وقته العمر، وهو الواجب الموسع فلا يعصي بالتأخير مع ظن السلامة وإن مات فجأة (ليس بسديد لأن الوجوب مشترك بين الواجب العمري والموسع، فإن كان سبب العصيان في الأول الوجوب فينبغي أن يعصي في الثاني أيضاً وعذر الفجأة عام) فيهما، فلو قبل عذر الفجأة في الموسع قبل في العمري فلا فرق وفيه ما فيه) فإن الفرق ظاهر، لأن العمري وقته العمر كله فالوجوب فيه يقتضي أن يعصي بالتأخير عن العمر، فإذا أخر الحج مثلاً ومات فجأة فقد تركه في تمام وقته المقدر وهو إثم، وإلا ارتفع الوجوب بخلاف الواجب الموسع، فإنه إذا مات قبل الآخر فما تركه في تمام وقته بتقصير منه، لكن حينئذ ينبغي أن لا فيما إذا ظن الموت، وأيضاً الوجوب ووجوب الأداء مفترقان في الموسع، فلا يأثم قبل الآخر لعدم وجوب الأداء، وفي العمرى لا ينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر فيأثم بالترك، هذا والله الغفور للمأثم يغفر لمن يشاء ويعذب من يشاء.
The answer is that death is not a cause for disobedience, so he does not sin because we did not make death a cause, rather his leaving (the act) by his own choice in its entire time. Similarly, the answer that making part as whole is only the thought of death, not death itself, is not valid because he is required to differentiate: how? Death is inability entirely, while thought has no inability if it turns out false. So death is more deserving than thought. This is the correct view we stated: no sin at all. (If he did not die) and his thought proved false (and he performed it in its time), then the majority say it is ada because the definition applies to it: he performed it in the time estimated by law. (Al-Qadi said: it is qada, because its time by law according to his thought was before, because when he thought death, performance before became incumbent. Sheikh Ibn al-Humam said this is unlikely if he says obligation of intention of qada, otherwise the dispute is verbal. It is objected to him: if he believes the time has ended before its entry and delays, then he definitely sins due to intending to oppose the command. (When the error becomes clear and he performs) in the time, it is ada by agreement, but it follows that it is qada because its estimated time by law was before such that he sinned by leaving in it. (I say: the difference between what al-Qadi said and this case is clear: in the first, there is absolute belief in absence of time) — neither time of qada nor time of ada, (and in the second, belief in absence of time of ada) only. So the first is constrained in every aspect, so neither ada nor qada is possible, unlike the second, hence the difference is clear. (So reflect) — an indication that it does not avail, because al-Qadi's cause exists here too: his time by law becoming before the thought, otherwise he would not have sinned by delay. Therefore, the truth on the view of sin is that they should say: the thought is considered as long as error does not appear, and when it appears, the ruling returns to the original, and the performed act occurs in time without doubt. (And whoever delays with expectation of safety and dies suddenly, the verification is that he does not sin, even if it is said that he sins, because delay) for one expecting safety (is permissible, and there is no sin for the permissible. And the statement that the condition for permissibility — i.e., permissibility of delay to the end — is safety of the outcome, and if he dies suddenly, the condition for permissibility of delay has been lost, so he should sin, is refuted by the fact that it would entail impossible obligation since the safety of the outcome is not known. As for the thought, it is realized in this case, so upon its consideration, delay is permissible, so he does not sin. And it is said: permissibility is not a matter of obligation, so there is no obligation of the impossible, but there is something in this: for even if it is not an obligation, it is permission, and there is no permission in the impossible. The author pointed to the weakness of this refutation by saying: (Not knowledge of it) — i.e., the condition for permissibility of delay is safety itself, not knowledge of safety — (such that it leads to obligation of the impossible; for knowledge of safety is impossible customarily, but safety itself is actual. (This statement requires) (choice between the possible — delay in case of safety, and the impossible —) delay in case of absence of safety, because the conditioned is impossible when the condition is absent. (And this) — i.e., choice in this manner — (removes the reality of expandability. So ponder, for expandability requires that the obligated person be free to choose delay, whereas this choice requires that he be cautious and not delay. So then one must build on the thought, so delay is permissible, no sin. If you say: this is contradicted by the lifelong obligation, for delay is permissible there with safety; also the purpose of stipulating safety is that delay is permissible on condition of non-abandonment, so if he dies in the middle of the time before performance, his abandonment is found, and there is no choice between possible and impossible. I say: there is a text regarding the expanded obligation about permissibility of delay to the end of its time without exception. If safety of outcome were stipulated, an increase would be required, and delay would be permissible at one time and not another, and a choice between delay with performance and with safety, and delay similarly with death, and this removes the reality of expandability understood from the text. This is unlike the lifelong obligation, for there is no text there about delay to the end of time; rather, delay is restricted there with the condition of non-violation, otherwise intentional abandonment without excuse until death becomes permissible, and this removes the reality of obligation. So understand. (And Sheikh Ibn al-Hajib differentiated between what has a lifetime time like hajj, in which he sins by delay even with expectation of safety and sudden death,) (and other than it) — i.e., other than what has lifetime time, which is the expanded obligation, so he does not sin by delay with expectation of safety even if he dies suddenly. (This is not sound, because obligation is common between lifelong and expanded obligations: if the cause of sin in the first is obligation, then he should sin in the second as well, and the excuse of sudden death is common in both. So if the excuse of sudden death is accepted in the expanded, it should be accepted in the lifelong, so no difference. There is something in it: because the difference is apparent, since the lifelong obligation's time is the entire life, so the obligation requires that he sin by delay beyond life. So if he delays hajj for example and dies suddenly, he has abandoned it in its entire estimated time, which is sin; otherwise obligation would be removed. Unlike the expanded obligation, for if he dies before the end, he has not abandoned it in its entire time through negligence. But then it should not be [sin?] when he thought death. Also, obligation and obligation of performance are distinct in the expanded, so he does not sin before the end due to lack of obligation of performance, whereas in the lifelong, one is not separated from the other, so he sins by abandonment. This, and Allah is the Forgiver of sins, He forgives whom He wills and punishes whom He wills.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a person who delays a time-bound obligatory act (e.g., prayer) due to expecting death (or safety) and then either dies or finds the thought false. The central question is whether such delay incurs sin. The author affirms the Hanafi position that there is no sin if the delay was based on a reasonable expectation (ظن) of safety, because death is not a cause of disobedience — the person chose to delay within the entire time. The text addresses several objections: (1) The argument that death is inability and thus the person should have performed earlier is rejected because the person acted on a valid thought; death does not retroactively make the delay sinful. (2) If the thought proves false and the person performs in the remaining time, the majority hold it is 'ada' (timely performance), while al-Qadi holds it is 'qada' (make-up) because the person thought the time had passed. The author clarifies the difference: in al-Qadi's case, the person believes the time has absolutely passed (no ada or qada possible), whereas here only the time for ada is thought to have passed. (3) Regarding sudden death after delaying with expectation of safety, the investigation is that there is no sin. The condition for permissibility of delay is safety itself, not knowledge of safety. Stipulating knowledge would lead to impossible obligation. The discussion concludes that the nature of expansion (توسع) in a broad obligation (واجب موسع) requires that the person be free to delay until the end, and imposing a condition of eventual safety undermines that freedom. The text also distinguishes the lifelong obligation (واجب عمري) like Hajj, where delay past the entire life incurs sin, unlike the expanded obligation where sin occurs only if one delays past the entire time with negligence. The author refutes Ibn al-Hajib's attempt to differentiate the two types, showing that the key distinction lies in the nature of obligation and the time frame.

Key Terms
التوسع (al-tawassu'): The legal flexibility that allows the obligated person to perform an obligatory act at any time within the entire time frame without immediate obligation of performance at the beginning.
الواجب العمري (al-wajib al-'umri): An obligation whose entire time is the person's lifetime, such as Hajj; delay past the entire time constitutes abandonment.
ظن السلامة (zann al-salamah): The expectation that one will survive until the end of the time; considered a valid basis for delaying an expanded obligation.
وجوب الأداء (wujub al-ada): The obligation of performance: the duty to actually perform the act at a particular time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following statements is true?
A) The ruling on delay with expectation of safety and sudden death is the same for all wajib acts
B) In expanded obligations, there is no sin; in lifetime obligations, there is sin
C) In lifetime obligations, there is no sin; in expanded obligations, there is sin
D) The ruling depends on the strength of the expectation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the ruling on delaying a wajib with expectation of safety and sudden death depends on whether the obligation is expanded (no sin) or lifetime (sin), due to the separation of wujub and wujub al-ada in the former.
