Obligation of Prerequisites (Muqaddimat al-Wajib)
Introducing the debate on prerequisite obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that prerequisites of obligatory acts are themselves wajib, and evaluate the counterarguments using the concept of impossibility.

Core Idea
Commanding an act obligates its prerequisites; otherwise, the command would entail the impossible.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut introduces a foundational debate in usul al-fiqh: Are the prerequisites (muqaddimat) of an obligatory act themselves obligatory? The Hanafi school holds that every prerequisite—whether a cause, condition, rational or customary—is wajib. The central proof is that without obligating the prerequisite, the command for the main act would apply when the prerequisite is absent, leading to a command to do the impossible. This lesson unpacks the four views, the Hanafi argument, and the entailment (istitba') principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four positions
Read the passage and list the four views on the obligation of prerequisites: (1) all are obligatory, (2) only the cause, (3) only the legal condition (Ibn al-Hajib's view), (4) none are obligatory.
The text uses 'قيل' for the restricted views.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof
The author states: 'لنا أن التكليف به بدون تكليف المقدمة يؤدي إلى التكليف بالمحال.' Grasp that this is a rational (aqli) argument: if the prerequisite is not commanded, the command for the main act would apply even when the prerequisite is missing, which is impossible.
Relate to the example of prayer without wudu.
3
Examine the opposing views and their limitations
The passage mentions that some restrict obligation to causes or legal conditions. Consider why they might reject the entailment—they may require an explicit command for each prerequisite.
Ibn al-Hajib's position is specifically mentioned as 'مختار ابن الحاجب'.
4
Recognize the consensus (ijma) on causes
The author notes: 'ألا ترى تحصيل أسباب الواجب واجب و تحصيل أسباب الحرام حرام بالإجماع.' This is a supporting argument, not the primary proof.
Consensus reinforces the Hanafi view but also shows the principle extends to causes.
5
Clarify the entailment (istitba')
The text concludes: 'بل المراد من وجوب المقدمة أنه أي الأمر بالواجب يستتبعه أي الأمر بالمقدمة.' Understand that the obligation of the prerequisite is not an explicit command but is entailed by the command for the main act.
This is a key Hanafi usul tool: the command for an act includes its means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ الواجب المطلق] المقدمات (مقدمة الواجب المطلق) أي الواجب الذي وجوبه غير متوقف على المقدمة (واجب مطلقاً أي سبباً كان أو شرطاً شرعاً كالوضوء، أو عقلاً كترك الضد، أو عادة كغسل جزء من الرأس لغسل الوجه، وقيل الوجوب (في السبب فقط دون غيره من المقدمات (وقيل في الشرط الشرعي فقط) وهو مختار ابن الحاجب (وقيل: لا وجوب) لشيء من مطلقاً لنا أن التكليف (به أي بالواجب (بدون تكليف المقدمة أي بدون التكليف بمقدمته يؤدي إلى التكليف بالمحال إذ الشيء بدون السبب أو الشرط محال لا يقال المحال ليس إلا التكليف بالواجب مأخوذاً مع عدم الشرط مثلاً لا بالواجب مطلقاً، فإن الصلاة بدون الوضوء محال لا الصلاة المطلقة لأنا نقول : التكليف بالواجب إما مقارن بالمقدمة فهو المدعي أو مطلق بحيث يصلح للمقارنة وعدمها فحينئذ التكليف تكليف بما يتناول المحال، فالمحال مكلف ولو بجزء، وهذا ظاهر جداً فلا تغفل ألا ترى تحصيل أسباب الواجب واجب و) تحصيل أسباب الحرام، حرام (بالإجماع أي قد أجمع على وجوب أسباب الواجب وحرمة أسباب الحرام لئلا يلزم التكليف بالمحال لا أن هذه المقدمة ثبتت بالإجماع ليرد عليه أنه قد ثبت بدليل آخر منفصل عن دليل وجوب الواجب، وقد كان الكلام في الوجوب بإيجاب ذي المقدمة فتأمل وما قيل لا نسلم لزوم التكليف بالمحال وإنما يلزم لو كان التكليف بالواجب من غير وجوب المقدمة أصلاً في نفس الأمر ، وهو غير لازم إذ (يجوز أن يكون وجوبها لغيره) أي لغير موجب الواجب (كالإيمان) فإنه واجب بنفسه، سواء وجبت العبادات أم لا ففيه أن الكلام) كان (بالنظر إليه يعني أنه لو لم تكن المقدمة واجبة بالنظر إلى الواجب لكان التكليف بالواجب متناولاً له حال عدم المقارنة بالمقدمة، فصار التكليف بالواجب تكليفاً بالمحال (فإن قلت كيف تجب المقدمة وهي غير مأمورة إذ لا يلزم الأمر صريحاً؟ قلت: لا نزاع في ذلك بل المراد من وجوب المقدمة (أنه) أي الأمر بالواجب (يستتبعه) أي الأمر بالمقدمة فهي واجبة
Issue [The Absolute Obligatory] – Preliminaries (The prerequisite of the absolute obligatory) i.e., the obligatory whose obligation does not depend on the prerequisite (is obligatory absolutely, whether it be a cause, like wudu, or a condition legally, like wudu, or rationally, like avoiding the opposite, or customarily, like washing part of the head for washing the face. And it was said: obligation is (only in the cause, not other prerequisites). And it was said: (only in the legal condition) – this is the choice of Ibn al-Hajib. And it was said: (there is no obligation) for any of them absolutely. For us: The imposition of duty (on the obligatory) without the imposition of the prerequisite leads to imposing the impossible, because the thing without its cause or condition is impossible. Do not say that the impossible is only the obligation of the obligatory taken together with the absence of the condition, for example, not the absolute obligatory. For we say: The imposition of duty on the obligatory is either conjoined with the prerequisite — that is what we claim — or absolute such that it is suitable for conjoining and not. Then the imposition of duty is an imposition that includes the impossible, so the impossible is made duty-bound, even if partially. This is very clear, so do not neglect. Do you not see that acquiring the causes of the obligatory is obligatory, and acquiring the causes of the forbidden is forbidden, by consensus. That is, there is consensus on the obligation of the causes of the obligatory and the prohibition of causes of the forbidden, to avoid imposing the impossible. Not that this prerequisite was established by consensus, so that it could be objected that it is established by another proof separate from the proof of the obligation of the obligatory. And the discussion was about obligation by the imposer of the act that has a prerequisite, so reflect. And what was said: We do not accept the necessity of imposing the impossible; rather it would be necessary only if the imposition of duty on the obligatory were without any obligation of the prerequisite in reality, and that is not necessary because (it is possible that its obligation is for other than the obligator of the obligatory), like faith, for it is obligatory by itself whether acts of worship are obligatory or not. But the discussion was (from the perspective of it), meaning that if the prerequisite were not obligatory from the perspective of the obligatory act, then the imposition of duty on the obligatory would encompass it while it is not conjoined with the prerequisite, making the imposition of duty on the obligatory an imposition of the impossible. (If you say: How is the prerequisite obligatory when it is not commanded, since a clear command does not necessarily follow? I say: There is no dispute about that; rather what is meant by the obligation of the prerequisite is that (the command for the obligatory) (entails it), i.e., the command for the prerequisite, so it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul discussion on the obligation of prerequisites (muqaddimat) of an obligatory act. The author presents four views: (1) All prerequisites (cause, legal condition, rational condition, customary requirement) are obligatory. (2) Only the cause (like wudu for prayer) is obligatory. (3) Only the legal condition (e.g., purity) is obligatory – this is the view of Ibn al-Hajib. (4) No prerequisites are obligatory at all. The Hanafi position (supported by the author) is the first: every prerequisite is obligatory. The proof is that if the prerequisite were not obligatory, the command for the main act would be a command to do something impossible when the prerequisite is absent, because the act cannot exist without it. For example, commanding prayer without wudu is impossible. The command for prayer must be understood as applying only when the condition is present; but if the condition is not obligatory, then the command would apply both with and without it, including the case without, which is impossible. Thus, to avoid commanding the impossible, the prerequisite must be commanded (obligatory). The author also notes consensus (ijma') that causes of obligatory acts are themselves obligatory, and causes of haram are forbidden. A counterargument suggests that the prerequisite might be obligatory for another reason (like faith being obligatory regardless of acts), but the author clarifies that the discussion is about the prerequisite being obligatory because of the commanded act itself. Finally, the author clarifies that the obligation of the prerequisite is not necessarily explicit in the text but is entailed (istitba') by the command for the main act. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul, supporting the obligation of means (wasa'il) to ends (maqasid).

Key Terms
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimat al-wajib): A prerequisite that is necessary for the performance of an obligatory act, without which the act cannot be performed.
الوجوب بالغير (al-wujoob bi al-ghayr): An obligation that is derived from another obligation, like the prerequisite being obligatory because the main act is obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the primary Hanafi argument for the obligation of prerequisites?
A) Consensus (ijma).
B) Avoidance of commanding the impossible.
C) Explicit command in the Quran.
D) Imitation of the salaf.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, every prerequisite of a wajib act is itself wajib by entailment, based on the necessity of avoiding command of the impossible.

Understanding the Condition in a Vow of I'tikaf During Ramadan
Answer to a question about fasting in a vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that a vow includes its essential condition, and derive the obligations when the condition is missed.

Core Idea
A vow of i'tikaf includes fasting as a condition; missing both means only i'tikaf with the condition is owed.

This passage addresses a subtle usul question: when someone vows i'tikaf during Ramadan, does the already obligatory fast become part of the vow? The author explains that the vow of i'tikaf implicitly requires fasting as a concurrent condition. This distinction determines what one must make up if either or both are missed. Understanding this sheds light on how the Hanafi school treats conditions in vows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The question is about a person who vowed to perform i'tikaf during Ramadan: what is owed if the fast is missed? The key is to understand the relationship between the vow (nadhr) and its condition (shart).
2
State the Hanafi principle
The principle is: 'The vow of the conditioned includes the vow of its condition' (النذر بالمشروط يتضمن النذر بالشرط). When someone vows i'tikaf, the fasting (as a necessary condition for i'tikaf in Ramadan) is included in the vow.
But since fasting in Ramadan is already wajib, its imposition is not possible again; it is already obligatory.
3
Apply to the case of missing both fast and i'tikaf
If both are missed, the vow of i'tikaf still stands, and it requires the condition of that same fast. Therefore, the person owes only the i'tikaf accompanied by the missed fast, not an additional separate fast.
The condition (fast) is considered to accompany the i'tikaf as it was initially vowed.
4
Apply to the case of performing fast but missing i'tikaf
If the person fasted (as required by Ramadan) but missed the i'tikaf, then the i'tikaf is owed unconditionally (without an accompanying fast because the fast was already fulfilled). To satisfy the condition, the vow imposes a new fast to accompany the i'tikaf that is owed.
Otherwise, the conditioned would be imposed without its condition.

المقالة الثانية في الأحكام دليل يلزم أن يصح في صوم الكفارة أو المنذور الآخر؟ والجواب وبالله التوفيق : أنا لا نقول شرطه الصوم المقصود وإنما نقول شرطه الصوم والنذر بالمشروط يتضمن النذر بالشرط المقارن له، فالنذر بالاعتكاف كان موجباً لهما، إلا أنه لم يظهر أثره في الصوم لكونه واجباً بنفسه، وإيجابه غير ممكن، فأوجب النذر اعتكافاً فحسب، لكن مقارناً لصوم الشهر المبارك، وإلا كان إيجاب المشروط من غير شرط، فإذا فات الصوم مع الاعتكاف بقي على ذمته اعتکاف مقارن لهذا الصيام بإيجاب النذر كما كان، فحينئذ لا يحتاج إلى إيجاب صوم آخر، بخلاف ما إذا صام ولم يعتكف فإنه بقي الاعتكاف في ذمته مطلقاً عن مقارنة صوم فأوجب النذر صوماً آخر مقارناً ،له وإلا عاد المحذور المذكور من وجوب المشروط بدون الشرط، هذا ما عندي ولقد طوّل المتأخرون في هذا المقام في أسفارهم، ولم يأتوا بشيء يرتفع به قلق القلوب وما نبه عليه عسى الله أن يهدي به الطالبين .
The second article on rulings: A proof is required to be valid in the fast of expiation or the other vowed fast? The answer, and with Allah is success: We do not say its condition is the intended fast, but rather we say its condition is the fast, and the vow of the conditioned includes the vow of the condition that accompanies it. So the vow of i'tikaf necessitated both, except that its effect did not appear in the fast because it is obligatory by itself, and its imposition is impossible. So the vow imposed only i'tikaf, but accompanying the fast of the blessed month. Otherwise, the imposition of the conditioned without a condition would occur. So if the fast together with i'tikaf were missed, there remains on his responsibility i'tikaf accompanying this fast by the imposition of the vow as it was. Then at that time, he does not need to impose another fast. Contrary to if he fasted and did not perform i'tikaf, then the i'tikaf remains on his responsibility absolutely without accompanying a fast, so the vow imposed another fast accompanying it, otherwise the mentioned danger of imposing the conditioned without the condition would return. This is what I have, and the later scholars have spoken at length in this place in their books, and they did not bring anything to remove the anxiety of hearts. What was pointed out [here] may Allah guide students thereby.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a response to a question about whether a vowed fast (e.g., for expiation or another vow) must be valid alongside a vow of i'tikaf in Ramadan. The author clarifies that the condition for a vow of i'tikaf is not that the fast be the intended one, but rather that the vow includes the fast as a concurrent condition. Since the Ramadan fast is already obligatory, the vow of i'tikaf does not impose a separate fast but rather the i'tikaf is conditioned upon the presence of the fast. Thus, if someone misses both the fast and the i'tikaf, they only owe the i'tikaf with the condition of that particular fast, not another fast. Conversely, if they fast but miss i'tikaf, they owe i'tikaf unconditionally, and the vow imposes another fast to accompany it. This is a fine point in Hanafi usul about the relationship between a vow and its conditions.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow; a commitment to perform an act of worship, which becomes obligatory upon the person.
الاعتكاف (al-iʿtikāf): Seclusion in a mosque for worship; in this context, it is vowed and must be performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows i'tikaf during Ramadan, fasts all days, but misses the i'tikaf, what does he owe according to the passage?
A) He must make up the i'tikaf only, without any fast.
B) He must make up the i'tikaf with an additional fast.
C) He must make up the fast only.
D) He owes nothing because he fasted.

Key Takeaway
The key takeaway is that a vow (nadhr) of i'tikaf inherently includes its condition (fasting), and the obligation is determined by whether the condition was present or missed.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Obligation of Prerequisites of the Absolute Wajib
Defining al-wajib al-mutlaq and arguing that its prerequisites are obligatory via rational proof, with objection responses and branches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that prerequisites of an absolute wajib are obligatory by entailment, and evaluate the objections and branches presented in the text.

Core Idea
Obliging an act without its prerequisites would oblige the impossible — so prerequisites are wajib by entailment.

This lesson explores a foundational usul rule: the prerequisites of an absolute wajib are themselves wajib. The text defines al-wajib al-mutlaq, records differing opinions, provides the key rational proof, defends against objections, and illustrates the rule with two branches. By the end, you will understand how Hanafi usul derives indirect obligations and resolves practical scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
State that the prerequisites of an absolute wajib are wajib, and note that the absolute wajib is defined as one whose obligation does not depend on any prerequisite.
Absolute here means the obligation itself is unconditional, not the act.
2
Map the opinions
List the three opinions: all prerequisites wajib; only causes wajib; only legal conditions wajib; none wajib. Identify which is the Hanafi position.
Ibn al-Hajib's choice is mentioned as restricting to legal conditions.
3
Understand the rational proof (taklif bi'l-muhal)
If the prerequisite were not wajib, commanding the main act would be commanding an impossibility, because the act cannot occur without the prerequisite. This is the core Hanafi argument.
The text also cites consensus that causes of wajib are wajib and causes of haram are haram.
4
Examine objections and Hanafi responses
Objections include: prerequisites not explicitly commanded, need for intellection, multiple sins, and requirement of intention. The Hanafi responses clarify indirect obligation, distinguish between explicit and entailment, and note the verbal nature of the dispute.
The text concludes the dispute is largely verbal.
5
Apply the rule to the furu'
Analyze the two branches: the case of confusion between a wife and stranger (both forbidden to avoid haram) and the claim that exiting prayer is obligatory as a prerequisite for entering another prayer. Apply the rule and evaluate the Hanafi stance.
In the prayer exit case, the text denies that exit by action is obligatory.

Because we say: the obligation of the wajib is either concomitant with the prerequisite—that is what is claimed—or absolute such that it is suitable for concomitance or not; at that time, the obligation is an obligation that encompasses the impossible. So the impossible is obligated, even in part. This is very clear, so do not neglect. (If the married woman is confused (with a stranger, if two women enter a house and the agent has married one of them, and the husband does not know the wife specifically, and the agent has died, then) the married woman becomes forbidden (because refraining from the forbidden) i.e., intercourse with the stranger (is wajib, and that is by refraining from both due to the confusion. And if a man addressing his two wives said (One of you is divorced, both become forbidden, because avoidance of the divorced one is certain, so avoidance is in both). So it is in the books of the Shafi'is. But according to us, in that case divorce does not occur on the specific one, but rather in the ambiguous (mubham); it only occurs on the specific by clarification. So before clarification, he may have intercourse with whichever he wishes as substitution, but intercourse with one of them becomes clarification of the divorce on the other. So there is no obligation to refrain from one of them such that refraining from both would be a prerequisite of the wajib. Yes, if he divorced a specific woman with a irrevocable divorce and then forgot the divorced woman, it should be that both are forbidden, because this is from the cases of confusion of the married woman with a stranger. (I say) And if the obligation of the prerequisite is established, then the goal is included in the goal) even if it is a prerequisite to know the existence of the goal, for knowledge of the existence of the goal is dependent on its inclusion. And the generality of dependence in all goals is subject to reflection; rather, it is only valid in some, which are prerequisites for the goal.
(Another branch: They said: The exit of the pray-er by his action is obligatory, because from the necessities of entering into another prayer is his exit from the first, and entering into another is obligatory, so likewise exit from the first. But this slave does not understand it; for being from the necessities does not necessitate that the exit by the action of the pray-er from it is obligatory. Moreover, it is permissible to exit a prayer after completing the essential parts without choice; indeed, that is apparent from the hadith.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on whether the prerequisites (muqaddimat) of an absolute wajib (an obligation not dependent on a prerequisite) are themselves obligatory. The text defines 'al-wajib al-mutlaq' as an obligation whose fulfillment does not depend on a prerequisite, yet its prerequisites become obligatory indirectly. Several opinions are recorded: some say only causes are obligatory, others only legal conditions, and some deny obligation for any prerequisite. The Hanafi position, argued here, is that all prerequisites are wajib, because otherwise the obligation would entail obliging the impossible (taklif bi'l-muhal). For example, prayer without wudu is impossible; if wudu were not wajib, then the command to pray would be a command to an impossible act. The text then defends against objections: that prerequisites are not explicitly commanded, that the obligation would require prior intellection, and that it would lead to multiple sins. The author concludes that the obligation of prerequisites is derived from the obligation of the original act, not by a separate command. Two branches (furu') illustrate the principle: the case of a confused wife and the case of exiting a prayer. In the first, when a wife is confused with a stranger, both women become forbidden to avoid the haram. In Hanafi law, if a man ambiguously divorces one of two wives, he may still have intercourse with either until clarification. The second branch critiques the claim that exiting a prayer by one's own action is obligatory as a prerequisite for entering another prayer, arguing that necessity does not entail obligation.

Key Terms
الواجب المطلق (al-wājib al-muṭlaq): The obligatory act whose obligation is not conditional upon any prerequisite; its prerequisites become obligatory indirectly through it.
المقدمة (al-muqaddima): A prerequisite or means to an obligatory act, whether a cause, condition, or customary necessity.
التكليف بالمحال (al-taklīf bi'l-muḥāl): Commanding something impossible; the central proof that prerequisites must be wajib to avoid obliging the impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the central Hanafi proof for the obligation of prerequisites of an absolute wajib?
A) Explicit Quranic verses commanding the prerequisites.
B) The principle that without obliging prerequisites, the main act would be impossible to command.
C) Consensus (ijma) on the obligation of all prerequisites.
D) The Prophet's explicit command to perform wudu before prayer.

Key Takeaway
The prerequisites of an absolute wajib are wajib by entailment to avoid obliging the impossible.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Does Command Imply Prohibition of the Opposite?
Analysis of whether command includes prohibition of its opposite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that commanding an act implies prohibition of its opposite as a subordinate implication resulting in dislike (karaha), and evaluate the counter-arguments from Mu'tazila and Shafi'is.

Core Idea
Commanding an act implicitly prohibits its opposite, but only as a subordinate implication that yields dislike, not outright haram.

A key usul question: when Allah commands an act, does He also prohibit its opposite? The answer affects how we count sins and understand the strength of the prohibition. The Hanafi school navigates between two extremes — total denial and full prohibition — by introducing the concept of implied address (khitab dimni) that results in dislike (karaha) rather than haram. This lesson unpacks the logical proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the main positions
State the question: Does the command of a thing imply prohibition of its opposite? Identify at least three positions: (1) no implication (Mu'tazila, Shafi'is), (2) full prohibition (some Hanafis like al-Jassas), (3) implied dislike (chosen Hanafi view).
The Hanafi view distinguishes between opposites that eliminate (yufawwit) the commanded and those that do not.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof: necessary concomitant
Follow the logical argument: refraining from the opposite is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of obligation. If the opposite were permissible alongside the commanded, either the opposition or the obligation would be negated. Therefore, prohibition of the opposite is entailed.
The proof relies on the concept that the obligation of the act itself makes refraining from the opposite wajib.
3
Distinguish between explicit and implied address
Understand that the prohibition of the opposite is not explicit but implied (khitab dimni). The implied address is weaker, so it establishes karaha, not haram. This explains why the opposite is not equal in weight to the commanded act.
The author (al-Bihari) uses the example: command to stand is not explicitly a prohibition of sitting; the prohibition is only by implication.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If one leaves the commanded act and engages in the opposite, how many sins? Hanafi (inclusion view) says one sin (leaving the commanded) because the prohibition of the opposite is subordinate. Opponents may count two sins (one for leaving, one for doing the opposite).
Ibn al-Humam noted this difference. The Hanafi view avoids multiplying sins unnecessarily.

مسألة [ وجوب الشيء وحرمة ضده] (وجوب الشيء يتضمن حرمة ضده المفوّت (وقيل) الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده وقيل الأمر بالشيء نفس النهي عن ضده، فمنهم من عمم في أمر الوجوب والندب، فجعلهما الضد تحريماً وتنزيهاً فالندب إلى الشيء نفس كراهة الضد (ومنهم من خصص) الحكم بأمر الوجوب فليس ضد المندوب مكروهاً (وقيل ليس) الأمر (نهيا) عن الضد (ولا متضمناً عقلاً، وعليه المعتزلة وعامة الشافعية ثم الخلاف في النهي كذلك) فالمختار أنه متضمن للأمر بالضد، وقيل نفس الأمر به سواء كان تحريماً أو تنزيهاً وقيل إذا كان تحريماً فقط، وقيل يقتضي كون الضد بمعنى سنة (إلا) أن الأمر ) بالشيء (نهي عن جميع الأضداد) لأن كل واحد منها مفوّت للواجب المأمور به (بخلاف النهي فإنه أمر بأحد أضداده) مخيراً، وقيل: ليس النهي أمراً بضد ولا متضمناً عقلاً كما في الأمر (وقيل) في النهي (لا) يتضمن الأمر بضد ولا نفسه بخلاف الأمر لنا أن الامتناع عن الضد من لوازم وجوب الفعل) وإلا فإن جاز الإتيان بالضد مع وجود الفعل ارتفع التضاد وإلا ارتفع الوجوب، وإذا كان من لوازم الوجوب فيتحقق وجوب الفعل بتحقق وجوب الامتناع عن الضد، فلزم حرمته بتبعية الوجوب وخطابه، وهو المراد من التضمن، كما أن جعل الملزوم وهو بعينه جعل اللازم، ولا يحتاج إلى جعل مستقل، كذلك لا يحتاج الامتناع عن الضد إلى موجب سوى موجب الفعل، والمصنف أفاد هذا المعنى بالتمثيل وقال : واللوازم مجعولة بجعل الملزوم لا بجعل جديد وإلا تكن كذلك لزم إمكان الانفكاك أي كما أن جعل الملزوم هو جعل اللازم كذلك إيجاب الملزوم هو بعينه إيجاب اللازم وبمثله يقال في النهي) يعني أن الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم كف الفعل، فإذا تحقق إيجاب الكف لا بد من تحقق إيجاب الاشتغال بالضد ولو تخييراً (وفيه شيء) لأن كون الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم الكف عن الفعل ممنوع، فإنه قد يوجد الكف ولا يخطر ضد بالبال، الاشتغال هذا الكف، وليس منه، وكذا ليس من لوازم عدم الفعل فإن العدم فضلاً عن بسوی، ربما يكون من عدم العلة لا بوجود المانع الذي هو الضد فلا يلزم الإيجاب (فالخطاب) هنا (واحد (بالذات متعلق بالفعل وبالضد والتفاوت بالأصالة والتبعية فبالأصالة للواجب وبالتبعية للكف عن الضد (كما في إيجاب المقدمة فعلى هذا إذا ترك الواجب مع الاشتغال بالضد فالمعصية معصية واحدة هي معصية ترك الواجب، وإنما ينسب إلى الضد بالعرض، وعلى هذا فلا يليق أن يخالف في هذا الحكم وجعل الشيخ ابن الهمام فائدة الخلاف في هذه المسألة أن عند قائلي التضمن أو العينية ،معصيتين وعند المنكر معصية واحدة وعلى هذا فالخطاب عند القائلين بالذات وأصالة إلى الواجب والكف عن الأضداد، وعند المنكرين ليس كذلك، وعلى الدليل قطعاً، لأنه لا يلزم من تعلق الخطاب بالملزوم تعلقه باللازم بالذات، لكن المصنف كما لا يخفى، وحينئذ فالنزاع لا طائل تحته (ومن ها هنا قيل يقتضي) الأمر بالشيء (كراهة ضده، فإن خطاب الضمن أنزل من خطاب الصريح) فلا بد من الفرق بينهما في الإطلاق ليعلم أنه ذكر الإمام فخر الإسلام أولاً ثلاثة مذاهب في ضد المأمور به والمنهي عنه : الأول: أنه لا حكم للأمر والنهي في الضد أصلاً. الثاني: : وقد نسبه إلى الشيخ أبي بكر الجصاص قدس سره تحریم ضد المأمور به ووجوب ضد المنهي عنه إن كان واحداً. ثم الثالث : كراهة ضد المأمور به وكون ضد المنهي عنه في معنى سنة واجبة وقال : هذا أصح عندنا، قال بعد ذكر حجج الفريقين الأولين واحتج الفريق الثالث بأن الأمر على ما قال الجصاص، إلا أنَّا أثبتنا بكل واحد من القسمين أدنى ما يثبت به لأن الثابت بغيره ضرورة لا يساوي المقصود بنفسه وأما الذي اخترناه فبناء على هذا وهو أن هذا لما كان أمراً ضرورياً سميناه اقتضاء، ومعنى الاقتضاء ها هنا أنه ضروري غير مقصود، فصار شبيهاً بما ذكرنا من مقتضيات أحكام الشرع ثم قال بعد عدة سطور: وفائدة هذا الأصل أن التحريم لما لم يكن مقصوداً بالأمر لم يعتبر إلا من حيث يفوّت الأمر، فأما إذا لم يفوّته كان مكروهاً، كالأمر بالقياس ليس بنهي عن القعود قصداً، حتى إذا قعد ثم قام لا تفسد صلاته، ولكنه مكروه، ولهذا قلنا : إن المحرم لما نهى عن لبس المخيط كان من السنة لبس الإزار والرداء إلى آخر ما ذكر من التفريعات كما هو دأبه الشريف، وقد تحير العلماء الأعلام في حل هذا البحث، فحمله بعضهم على ما أشار إليه المصنف وحاصله أن ما يفيده خطاب الضمن وهو أنزل من الصريح لا يثبت به التحريم، بل أنزل منه وهو الكراهة، ومن هذا الوجه سماه كراهية، فلا في المعنى بينه وبين الشيخ أبي بكر، وعلى هذا لا يستقيم قوله : وأما إذا لم يفوته كان مكروهاً، إلا أن يقال من ها هنا شرع في كلام آخر فأراد بالمكروه ها هنا المتعارف من المعنى لا ما ثبت بخطاب غير صريح كما أراد سابقاً، ثم أورد المصنف عليه بقوله: (لكن يلزم على هذا إطلاق المكروه على الممتنع أي الحرام وهو بعيد جداً، وحمله آخرون على أن مقصوده رحمه الله إثبات الكراهة في غير المفوت من الأضداد وتقرير كلامه أنا أثبتنا لكل من الأمر والنهي أدنى درجة، وهو الخطاب الضمني الضروري الذي سميناه اقتضاء باصطلاح، وإذا ثبت الخطاب الضمني ففائدته أن الضد إذا غير مقصود بالحكم بالأمر، ولم يعتبر إلا من مخالفة، من
Issue: Obligation of a thing and prohibition of its opposite. The obligation of a thing includes the prohibition of its opposite that eliminates it. It is said: the command of a thing implies the dislike of its opposite. It is said: the command of a thing is the very prohibition of its opposite. Some made it general in the case of obligation and recommendation, making the opposite of the two as prohibition and dislike, so recommendation of a thing is the very dislike of its opposite. Some specified the ruling to the command of obligation, so the opposite of a recommended thing is not disliked. It is said: the command is not a prohibition of the opposite nor does it include it rationally, and the Mu'tazila and the majority of the Shafi'is hold this. Then the dispute is likewise in the prohibition. The chosen view is that it includes the command of the opposite; it is said it is the very command of it, whether it is prohibition or dislike; it is said only if it is prohibition; it is said it implies that the opposite is in the sense of a sunna. Except that the command of a thing is a prohibition of all opposites, because each one eliminates the commanded obligation. Unlike the prohibition, for it is a command of one of its opposites, by choice. It is said: the prohibition is not a command of an opposite, nor does it include it rationally, as in the command. It is said in prohibition: it does not include the command of an opposite nor is it itself, unlike the command. For us, refraining from the opposite is a necessary concomitant of the obligation of the act. Otherwise, if performing the opposite together with the act were permissible, the opposition would be removed, and if not, the obligation would be removed. When it is a necessary concomitant of the obligation, then the obligation of the act is realized by the realization of the obligation of refraining from the opposite, so its prohibition is necessitated by way of subordination to the obligation and its address. And that is what is meant by inclusion. Just as the making of the necessary condition is the very making of the necessary, and it does not need an independent making, likewise refraining from the opposite does not need an obligator other than the obligator of the act. The author conveyed this meaning by example and said: The necessary concomitants are made by the making of the necessary condition, not by a new making; otherwise, separation would be possible. That is, just as the making of the necessary condition is the making of the necessary, so too the obligation of the necessary condition is the very obligation of the necessary. Similarly is said regarding the prohibition, meaning that engaging in the opposite is a necessary concomitant of refraining from the act. So when the obligation of refraining is realized, the obligation of engaging in the opposite must be realized, even if by choice. But there is something: that engaging in the opposite is a necessary concomitant of refraining from the act is denied, for refraining may exist and no opposite occurs to the mind. Engaging is this refraining, and it is not from it. Likewise, it is not a necessary concomitant of the non-existence of the act, for non-existence may be due to the absence of the cause, not due to the existence of the obstacle which is the opposite, so obligation is not necessitated. So the address here is single in essence, pertaining to the act and the opposite, with difference in originality and subordination: originally for the obligatory, subordinately for refraining from the opposite, as in the obligation of the prerequisite. Thus, if one leaves the obligatory while engaging in the opposite, the sin is one sin—the sin of leaving the obligatory—and it is attributed to the opposite only accidentally. Therefore, it is not appropriate to differ on this ruling. Ibn al-Humam made the benefit of the dispute in this issue that according to the proponents of inclusion or identity, there are two sins, and according to the denier, one sin. On this, according to the proponents, the address is originally to the obligatory and to refraining from the opposites; according to the deniers, it is not so. And this is by proof decisively, because the attachment of the address to the necessary condition does not necessitate its attachment to the necessary in essence. But the author, as is not hidden, and then the dispute has no substance. From here it is said: the command of a thing implies dislike of its opposite, for the implied address is lower than the explicit address. So there must be a difference between them in usage. Know that Imam Fakhr al-Islam first mentioned three schools regarding the opposite of the commanded and the forbidden: First: that there is no ruling for command and prohibition regarding the opposite at all. Second: attributed to Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas—the prohibition of the opposite of the commanded and the obligation of the opposite of the forbidden if it is single. Third: dislike of the opposite of the commanded and that the opposite of the forbidden is in the sense of an obligatory sunna. He said: This is more sound according to us. After mentioning the proofs of the first two groups, the third group argued that the command is as al-Jassas said, except that we established for each of the two categories the lowest level that is established, because what is established by other than itself necessarily does not equal the intended by itself. As for what we chose, it is based on this: that since this was necessary, we called it iqtida' (necessary implication), and the meaning of iqtida' here is that it is necessary but not intended. So it became similar to what we mentioned of the necessary implications of the rulings of Sharia. Then he said after several lines: The benefit of this principle is that since prohibition is not intended by the command, it is only considered insofar as it eliminates the commanded. So if it does not eliminate it, it is disliked, like command to stand is not a prohibition of sitting intentionally, such that if one sits and then stands, his prayer is not invalidated, but it is disliked. Therefore we said: that when the pilgrim is forbidden from wearing sewn garments, it is sunna to wear a lower garment and a cloak, etc., as is his noble habit. The great scholars have been perplexed in solving this research. Some interpreted it as what the author indicated: that what the implied address conveys is lower than the explicit, so it does not establish prohibition, but rather something lower than it, which is dislike. From this perspective he called it dislike, so there is no difference in meaning between him and Abu Bakr. On this, his statement 'and if it does not eliminate it, it is disliked' is not consistent, except if one says that from here he began another statement and intended by 'disliked' here the conventional meaning, not what is established by non-explicit address. Then the author objected to him by saying: 'But this necessitates applying the term disliked to the forbidden, i.e., the prohibited, which is very far-fetched.' Others interpreted it that his intention was to establish dislike in the non-eliminating opposites, and the gist of his words: we established for each of command and prohibition the lowest degree, which is the necessary implied address that we called iqtida' by convention. When the implied address is established, its benefit is that the opposite, since it is not intended by the ruling of the command, is only considered from the aspect of opposition.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of a core usul principle: whether the command of a thing (e.g., 'Stand') implies the prohibition of its opposite (e.g., 'Sit'). The text begins by stating the chosen view: obligation of a thing includes prohibition of its opposite that eliminates it. Then it presents several opinions: some say command implies dislike (karaha) of the opposite, some say the command itself is a prohibition of the opposite, while the Mu'tazila and most Shafi'is deny any implication. The discussion extends to whether the prohibition (nahy) similarly implies a command of its opposite. The author (presumably Muhibbullah al-Bihari) argues that refraining from the opposite is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of obligation, thus the prohibition of the opposite is subordinated to the obligation. He provides a logical proof: if performing the opposite were possible with the commanded act, then either opposition would be negated or the obligation would be negated. Therefore, the obligation to refrain from the opposite is automatically entailed. The difference between the implied (ضمني) and explicit (صريح) address is important: implied address is lower in strength, so it establishes dislike rather than outright prohibition. The text also cites three schools from Imam Fakhr al-Islam: (1) no ruling on the opposite, (2) prohibition of the opposite of commanded and obligation of the opposite of forbidden (attributed to al-Jassas), and (3) dislike of the opposite of commanded and that the opposite of forbidden is like an obligatory sunna. The author favors the third. The debate over whether the sin is one or two (for leaving the commanded and committing the opposite) is also discussed. Ibn al-Humam says that according to those who hold inclusion/identity, there are two sins; according to deniers, one sin. The preferred view seems to be that it is one sin because the violation is essentially leaving the commanded, and only accidentally involves the opposite.

Key Terms
ضد (didd): An opposite that prevents or eliminates the commanded act.
خطاب ضمني (khitab dimni): An implied address that conveys a lower-strength ruling than the explicit address.
اقتضاء (iqtida'): A necessary but unintended implication of a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the chosen Hanafi view from the view of al-Jassas?
A) Al-Jassas said the opposite is haram, while the chosen view says it is always makruh.
B) Al-Jassas said the opposite is haram, while the chosen view says it is haram only if it eliminates the commanded, otherwise makruh.
C) Al-Jassas said there is no ruling on the opposite, while the chosen view says it is haram.
D) Al-Jassas and the chosen view are identical in all respects.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view that commanding a thing implies prohibition of its opposite is nuanced: the implication is subordinate and yields dislike (karaha) unless the opposite eliminates the commanded act, in which case it is haram.

Combining Obligation and Prohibition in One Genus
Rule on combining obligation and prohibition in the generic category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-wahid bi al-jins and al-wahid bi al-naw', and apply the Hanafi rule that obligation and prohibition can combine in the genus but not in the same species or individual.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition can combine in the same genus only when attached to different species.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul question: can the same generic act be both obligatory and prohibited? The Hanafi school answers yes, but only for different species (types) of that genus. The authors use the example of prostration (sujud) to clarify the logic and refute objections from the Mu'tazila. The deeper distinction between 'one by genus', 'one by species', and 'one by individual' is essential for correct taklif analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
Understand the difference between 'al-wahid bi al-jins' (one by genus) and 'al-wahid bi al-naw'' (one by species). The genus is a universal category; species are its specific types. Rulings can differ across species of the same genus.
2
Analyze the example
Examine the example: prostration to God (wajib) and prostration to the sun (haram). Both are prostrations (same genus) but differ in object (species). This shows that obligation and prohibition can attach to different species of the same genus.
3
Trace the Mu'tazili objection
The Mu'tazila redirect the discussion from the act to the intention (veneration). They claim the act itself is neither wajib nor haram; only the intention carries the ruling. Understand why the Hanafi response rejects this as futile: veneration is itself a genus with types.
4
Understand the preference for species
The author prefers discussing 'one by species' over 'one by individual' because taklif attaches to the type (naw') in reality, not to the individual act. The individual act only acquires its ruling as an instance of the type.
This explains why a single act cannot be both wajib and haram simultaneously.
5
Conclude the rule
The rule: obligation and prohibition can combine within a genus (different species) but cannot combine within the same species or individual. This aligns with the famous principle that a single act cannot be both wajib and haram.

مسألة
(يجوز في الواحد بالجنس اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة بأن يكون نوع منه واجباً ونوع آخر حراماً (كالسجود لله و السجود (للشمس) فإنهما نوعان لمطلق السجود الواحد الجنسي، مع وجوب الأول وحرمة الثاني ومنع بعض المعتزلة هذا الاجتماع (مكابرة) لا يلتفت إليه (وصرفهم) السجود إلى قصد التعظيم بأن السجود ليس حراماً ولا واجباً، إنما الواجب تعظيم الله تعالى والمحرم تعظيم الشمس (لا يجدي) في هذا المقام فإن التعظيم واحد جنسي، وأحد نوعيه هو تعظيم الله تعالى واجب والآخر هو تعظيم الشمس حرام (إنما الكلام في الواحد بالنوع) هل يجتمع فيه الوجوب والحرمة بأن يكون شخص منه واجباً وحراماً، فهذا وما هو المشهور من الكلام في الواحد بالشخص متحدان في المآل لكنه إنما عبر بهذه العبارة لأن التكليف بالنوع والشخص إنما يوجد بعد الإتيان، ولا يتصف بالوجوب والحرمة إلا لأنه فرد من النوع، وهذا هو مراده مما قال في الحاشية»، وهذا أولى من المشهور، لأنه لا تكليف إلا بالنوع تحقيقاً، لأن التشخيص بعد الوجود، ولأن النوع إنما يتصف بالوجوب والحرمة باعتبارين بخلاف الواحد بالجنس انتهى ولم يرد به أن ما ذكره القوم باطل، بل أن هذا
Issue: It is permissible in what is one by genus for obligation and prohibition to combine, such that one type of it is obligatory and another type is prohibited. For example, prostration to God and prostration to the sun: they are two types of absolute prostration which is one in genus, with the first being obligatory and the second prohibited. Some Mu'tazilites forbid this combination (out of stubbornness) and it is not heeded. Their turning prostration into the intention of veneration—saying that prostration is neither prohibited nor obligatory, only veneration of God is obligatory and veneration of the sun is prohibited—is of no use in this context, for veneration is one in genus, and one of its types (veneration of God) is obligatory and the other (veneration of the sun) is prohibited. The real discussion is about the one by species: whether obligation and prohibition can combine in it such that an individual of it is both obligatory and prohibited. This and what is famous about the one by individual are the same in outcome, but he only expressed it this way because the taklif (legal duty) by species and individual only exists after the act has occurred, and it is not described as obligatory or prohibited except because it is an individual of the type. This is his intent from what he said in the footnote. And this is preferable to the famous view, because there is no taklif except by species in reality, since specification occurs after existence, and because the type is described as obligatory or prohibited by two considerations, unlike the one by genus. He did not mean that what the people (earlier scholars) said is false, but rather this...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of combining obligation (wujub) and prohibition (hurmah) in a single generic category (al-wahid bi al-jins). The ruling: it is permissible for two different types (anwa') of the same genus to have contrasting rulings. Example: sujud (prostration) as a generic act: sujud to God is obligatory, sujud to the sun is prohibited. Both are prostrations but differ in their specific objects. The Mu'tazilites objected, claiming that the act is not in itself wajib or haram; rather, the obligation is connected to the intention of venerating God, and the prohibition to venerating the sun. The text dismisses this as obstinate, for veneration (ta'zim) is itself a genus, and its types (veneration of God and veneration of the sun) are exactly like the prostration example. So the same issue recurs. The deeper point is about the one by species (al-wahid bi al-naw') and the one by individual (al-wahid bi al-shakhs). The author argues that discussing the one by species and the one by individual leads to the same outcome. He prefers the discussion in terms of species because taklif is truly attached to the type (naw'), not the individual; the individual act acquires its ruling as an instance of the type. Thus, obligation and prohibition cannot both apply to the same individual act simultaneously, but can apply to different types of the same genus. This aligns with the famous principle that a single act cannot be both wajib and haram for one person at one time, but different acts sharing a genus can be.

Key Terms
الواحد بالجنس (al-wahid bi al-jins): What is one in genus (jins), i.e., sharing a common genus but differing in species or specifics.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wahid bi al-naw'): What is one in species (naw'), i.e., sharing the same species, where individual acts are instances of the same type.
التكليف (al-taklif): Legal duty; the imposition of a ruling (obligation or prohibition) on a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view as presented in the passage, can obligation and prohibition combine in the same individual act (al-wahid bi al-shakhs)?
A) Yes, because the genus allows it.
B) No, because the same individual cannot be both wajib and haram simultaneously.
C) Yes, if the intention changes.
D) No, but they can combine in the same species.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, obligation and prohibition can combine in the same genus but only when they attach to different species; the same species or individual cannot have both rulings.

Simultaneous Obligation and Prohibition: Multiplicity of Aspects
Analyzing the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition in one act via ta'addud al-jiha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-wahid bi'l-jins and al-wahid bi'l-naw' and apply the Hanafi principle of ta'addud al-jiha to explain how a single act can be both wajib and haram.

Core Idea
A single act can be both wajib and haram if the aspects differ, like prayer in a usurped property.

Can an act be both obligatory and forbidden at the same time? At first glance, this seems impossible. Yet Hanafi usul al-fiqh provides a sophisticated answer: it depends on how we consider the act. This lesson unpacks the key distinction between genus and species, and the crucial role of multiplicity of aspects (ta'addud al-jiha) in resolving apparent contradictions in divine commands.

Guided Path
1
Define the key terms
Identify and understand 'al-wahid bi'l-jins' (one by genus) and 'al-wahid bi'l-naw' (one by species) as used in the text. The former is a vague reality actualized through different instances; the latter is a specific reality considered in itself.
These terms are specific to Hanafi usul and are critical to the discussion.
2
The agreed case: obligation and prohibition in a genus
When an act is considered as a genus, it can be obligated in one specific instance and prohibited in another without contradiction. This is agreed upon by all.
Example: the genus 'fasting' can be wajib on Ramadan and haram on Eid.
3
The disputed case: obligation and prohibition in a species
If the act is considered as a specific species, combining obligation and prohibition in the same instance is contested. The Hanafi position allows it when the aspects differ.
The classical example is prayer in a usurped property: wajib as prayer, haram as usurpation.
4
Apply ta'addud al-jiha to the prayer example
The existence in the space is one in person but multiple in consideration: as prayer it is an act of worship (wajib), as usurpation it is a transgression (haram). The objects of obligation and prohibition are different.
This is the core of the Hanafi argument against those who claim impossibility.
5
Address the objection of fasting on Eid
The hadith prohibiting fasting on Eid seems to conflict with a vow to fast. The Hanafi response: the prohibition is a preventer (mani') that does not invalidate the essence of the fast, unlike the usurpation case where the prohibition does not target the essence of prayer.
This distinction shows the nuanced application of ta'addud al-jiha.

أما الأول : فلما روى الشيخان عن أبي سعيد الخدري : لا يصح الصيام في يومين يوم الأضحى والفطر، والطبراني عن ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنهما أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وسلم أرسل أيام منى صائحاً يصيح أن لا تصوموا هذه الأيام فإنها أيام أكل وشرب. وأما الثاني : فلما روى أصحاب السنن عن أم المؤمنين عائشة الصديقة رضي الله عنها : لا نذر في معصية وكفارته كفارة يمين وإذا لم ينعقد لم يتحقق الوجوب، قلت: لا نذر ها هنا بالمعصية، فإن الناذر، إنما ينذر الصوم وذاته ليست معصية، وإنما المعصية في أمر مقارن له وهو الإعراض والنهي يقرر المشروعية وألفاظ أبي سعيد رضي الله عنه مختلفة، ففي بعضها نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ولو سلم عدم الصحة كما هو رأي الشافعية (فهو لمانع) عندهم، والتخلف لمانع لا يضر مقصودنا، وهو جواز اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة لأجل جهتين (وهو) الفساد أي المانع النهي الدال على فساد الصوم فيه فإن النهي عندهم يوجب وعدم مشروعية الذات بخلاف النهي عن الغصب فإنه لا يدل على فساد الصلاة إذ لم يرد النهي عنها بخصوصها (والجواب بتخصيص الدعوى أي دعوى الاجتماع (بما إذا كان بينهما عموم من
The second article on rulings. The expression is better and finer. The summary of the second aspect is that obligation of a species is an obligation to perform a particular individual, and its prohibition is an obligation to refrain from all individuals, so the two contradictory [obligation and prohibition] would meet in one person, meeting only from two aspects, unlike the one by genus, because its obligation is obligation of a certain type and its prohibition is necessity of refraining from a type, and there is no contradiction. And if by its prohibition is meant refraining from all its individuals, it is a prohibition of this reality required to be refrained from, so it is a species in this respect. And if the species is prohibited in one person and obligated in another, then in this consideration it is considered vague, so it is a genus. The conclusion: if a vague reality is considered and obligated considering its vague actualizations, then it is the one by genus in this terminology, and this type of combination is permissible by agreement of those whose agreement is counted. If the reality itself is considered and its performance by one of the individuals is obligated without regard to the particular actualization, or if the reality itself is prohibited, meaning the intent is not to perform it itself without specific particularization, then that is what is meant by the one by species, and this type of combination is disputed. There is no doubt that expression by the one by species is better than the one by person. This is the utmost guidance for the author's words, so understand. The summary of the issue: obligating something within some of its types and prohibiting it within some others is permissible, contrary to some of the Mu'tazila who are not counted. The discussion is on the obligation and prohibition of a thing such that it is attributed with both in its individuals, whether that thing is called genus or species. If the aspect is the same in reality or in ruling, as when they are equal, then that combination is impossible, for it necessitates performing it and not performing it, which is combining two contradictories; this command is a command with contradictories. This is not like abrogation of a permanent [ruling], because there the permanent ruling is removed, so the actual ruling is one; here the discussion is on combination. Then he elevated and said: 'Rather, its command is impossible, because from this command arises the combination of obligation and prohibition in one thing in essence and aspect, so it becomes obligatory and forbidden, which is combining two opposites in reality.' The commentator said: 'There is something in it: it is only valid if there is no multiplicity of aspects at all, but if there is a multiplicity of equal aspects, then the locus of obligation and prohibition is different, so there is no combination of contradictions. However, compliance is then impossible, so the command is a command of the impossible, not an impossible command. So reflect. Or if the aspect is multiple in reality and in ruling, such that separation between the two is possible, like prayer in a usurped house. So according to the majority of Hanafis, Shafi'is, and Malikis, this type of command is valid. Thus prayer in usurped land is both obligatory and forbidden together; the one who performs it deserves reward for prayer and punishment for usurpation. Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani said: 'It is not valid and it fulfills [the obligation]' — he meant by that act which has this nature. Imam al-Razi, author of al-Mahsul, deemed it far-fetched, because fulfillment of the command is either by compliance or abrogation, both of which are absent. According to Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal and most theologians, and al-Jubba'i, and the Rawafid: 'This type of command is not valid and does not fulfill the obligation.' Our proof: There is no impediment by imagining two opposing attributes. The lack of unity of the two objects [of obligation and prohibition] in reality is established here. The existence in the space, although one existence in person, is multiple in that it is existence as prayer and worship of Allah, and existence as usurpation and transgression on another's property. By the first aspect it is obligatory, by the second it is forbidden, so there is no unity of the objects at all, so no impossibility. It was said in the glosses of Mirzajan: 'We do not concede that the existence in the usurped property as prayer is obligatory, so that obligation and prohibition combine. It would only be obligatory if the command to prayer included it, which is denied, for the prohibition of existence in the usurped place indicates that the existence sought in the command of prayer is other than this.' This is apparent from the speaker's words. It can be formulated as an objection that it is not valid, because the sought [prayer] is other than this prayer, and the specifier is the prohibition of existence in the usurped place. His statement supports the objection: 'I say' in the answer: 'The indication' — i.e., the indication of the prohibition of existence in the usurped place that the sought prayer is other than the prayer in it — is denied, for it is a branch of the contradiction between the mentioned prohibition and the mentioned command. And when we permit the combination between them, considering that the command is absolute as is its reality, with the difference of aspect and adhering to the reality being necessary if there is no deterrent, then where is the indication that the command does not include this prayer? If the objection is refined by denial, then the formulation of the answer is that existence in the usurped place as prayer is definitely obligatory, because the prayer command seeks absolute prayer, for it is absolute, and restriction must have a deterrent, and no deterrent is imagined except the prohibition of usurpation, and it does not serve as a restrictor unless it indicates invalidity, and indication is based on contradiction, which is false due to multiplicity of aspect. So understand. So our case becomes like when a master commands his slave to sew and prohibits travel, and the slave sews and travels: he is obedient in sewing and disobedient in travel, definitely. Similarly, the objection of fasting on the day of Nahr (Adha) — that if one vows to fast on that day, it should be valid, because prohibition is from one aspect (being on the day of Nahr) and obligation from another aspect (being a vowed fast), so it is like prayer in a usurped land — is refuted because the non-application (of the ruling) is denied here. According to us, one is released from liability by fasting on it; the fast is valid from the aspect of being a vowed fast, and one sins from the aspect of it being on the day of Nahr and turning away from Allah's hospitality. If you say: this vow should not be valid because it is a sin, and a vow of sin is void. As for the first: because the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'Fasting on two days, the day of Adha and Fitr, is not valid.' And al-Tabarani from Ibn 'Abbas that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) sent a caller during the days of Mina proclaiming: 'Do not fast these days, for they are days of eating and drinking.' As for the second: because the authors of the Sunan narrated from 'Aisha: 'No vow in a sin, and its expiation is the expiation of an oath.' If it is not concluded, obligation is not realized. I say: Here there is no vow of sin, for the one vowing only vows the fast itself, which is not a sin; the sin is in an accompanying matter, i.e., turning away. And the prohibition confirms the legitimacy. The narrations of Abu Sa'id differ; in some of them it is: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade.' Even if we concede non-validity as is the view of the Shafi'is, it is due to a preventer according to them. Non-application due to a preventer does not harm our purpose, which is the permissibility of combining obligation and prohibition due to two aspects. And it (the preventer) is the invalidity, i.e., the prohibition indicating the invalidity of fasting on it, because according to them the prohibition causes non-legitimacy of the essence itself, unlike the prohibition of usurpation, which does not indicate the invalidity of prayer since no prohibition was directed at it specifically. The answer is by restricting the claim (i.e., the claim of combination) to when there is a general [relationship] between them...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion in usul al-fiqh about whether a single act can be simultaneously wajib and haram. The author begins by distinguishing between 'al-wahid bi'l-jins' (the one by genus) and 'al-wahid bi'l-naw' (the one by species) in the context of obligation and prohibition. If an act is considered as a vague genus that can be realized through any specific instance, it may be obligated in one instance and prohibited in another without contradiction. But if it is considered as a specific species, then combining obligation and prohibition in the same instance is impossible if the aspect is the same. However, if the aspects differ, as in prayer in a usurped property, the majority (Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis) hold that it is valid: the act is wajib as prayer and haram as usurpation. The text refutes the opposing views of al-Baqillani, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and others, who argue that such a command is impossible. The key proof is the multiplicity of aspects (ta'addud al-jiha). The act of existing in a space is one in person but multiple in consideration: as prayer it is wajib, as usurpation it is haram. The text then addresses an objection about fasting on Eid al-Adha when vowed, explaining that the prohibition in that case is due to a specific textual prohibition that indicates the act itself is invalid, unlike the general prohibition of usurpation which does not invalidate the prayer's essence. Hadiths are cited to support the prohibition of fasting on Eid, but the author argues that the prohibition is a preventer (mani') that does not negate the principle of combining obligation and prohibition due to different aspects. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with further restrictions.

Key Terms
الواحد بالجنس (al-wahid bi al-jins): A reality that is considered vaguely (mubham) and can be actualized through different specific instances; obligation or prohibition attaches to it without regard to a particular actualization.
الواحد بالنوع (al-wahid bi al-naw'): A specific reality considered itself; obligation or prohibition attaches to it directly, so combining both in the same instance is disputed.
تعدد الجهة (ta'addud al-jiha): The consideration of a single act from multiple legal perspectives, allowing it to be both wajib and haram simultaneously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, if a person prays in a usurped property, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid and no reward or punishment is due.
B) The prayer is valid and obligatory, but the person sins for the usurpation.
C) The prayer is prohibited and must be repeated.
D) The prayer is neither obligatory nor prohibited.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, obligation and prohibition can coexist in the same act when the aspects are different, as demonstrated by the case of prayer in a usurped property.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
12 min
Combining Obligation and Prohibition: The Hanafi Position on Jiha
Refuting the impossibility of coexisting obligation and prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the concept of jiha (aspect) to defend the Hanafi position that obligation and prohibition can coexist in a single act, using textual examples and qiyas.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition can coexist in one act if each attaches to a different legal aspect (jiha).

This passage defends the Hanafi position that obligation and prohibition are not inherently contradictory; they can coexist when they stem from different aspects of the same act. The author refutes objections by using examples from vowed fasts, disliked prayers, and prayer in usurped land. Understanding this principle is crucial for resolving apparent conflicts in legal rulings and appreciating the Hanafi emphasis on nuanced legal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Recognize that the core dispute is whether obligation (wajib) and prohibition (haram) can apply to the same act simultaneously. Note the opposing view that this is impossible due to contradiction.
The opposing view is often associated with the Mu'tazila and some Shafi'i positions, though not all.
2
Understand the Hanafi solution: jiha
Hanafis resolve the contradiction by distinguishing between different 'aspects' (jihaat) of the act. The act itself is one, but rulings attach to separate considerations, allowing coexistence.
Jiha is a technical term in Hanafi usul referring to the legal aspect or consideration from which a ruling arises.
3
Analyze the example of vowed fast on Nahr
The fast of Nahr is obligatory due to a vow (نذر) and prohibited because the day is a celebration (يوم الأضحى) where fasting turns one away from God's hospitality. These are two different jihaat.
The author stresses that the vow is general (not tied to Nahr specifically) while the prohibition is specific to that day.
4
Evaluate the qiyas from disliked prayer and usurped land
The argument from disliked prayer: if obligation and prohibition could not combine, then disliked prayer (which combines obligation and dislike) would be invalid. But it is valid by consensus, so combination is possible. Similarly, prayer in usurped land combines obligation and prohibition, yet it discharges the obligation (with sin).
Dislike (karahah) is a weaker form of prohibition, so the reasoning scales.
5
Refute objections: equal aspects and impossibility
The author responds to the objection that obliging the impossible results when the two aspects are equal. He shows this only applies when the general aspect is prohibition, not obligation as in the fast example. He also refutes the claim that consensus prohibits combining, citing Imam Ahmad's exception.
The passage also critiques Abu Hashim's claim about exiting usurped property, showing that implied permission removes the prohibition.
