المقالة الثانية في الأحكام وجه وها هنا ليس كذلك، فإن الصوم لا ينفك عن صوم النحر (لا يدفع النقض عن عموم الدليل) فإن مقدمات الدليل جارية فيه، إذ لا تضاد عند اختلاف الجهتين، فإن قلت يرد النقض على عموم الدليل بما إذا كان بين الجهتين تساو، فإن اعتذر بأن إيجاب ما لا يخلو عن الحرام لا فائدة فيه ولا يليق بشأن الحكيم يعتذر به فيما إذا كان اللزوم من جانب، فإن إيجاب شيء مع لزوم الحرمة لا فائدة فيه مع أن إيفاء هذا النذر والمنهي عنه متلازمان، قلت: لا نقض بمتساوي الجهتين فإنه يلزم فيه الإتيان بكل، لأنه واجب أو لازم واجب، والاجتناب عن كل لأنه حرام أو لازم حرام، ففيه تكليف بالمستحيل بخلاف ما إذا كان اللزوم من جانب، فإنه يجوز أن يكون جهة الوجوب أمراً عاماً يتحقق امتثاله في غير الخاص فلا تكليف بالمحال ؛ نعم : لو كان جهة الحرمة عامة وجهة الوجوب جهة خاصة لزم الاستحالة، لكنا لا نقول بجوازه، ونحن إنما نقول بوجوب صوم النحر لأنه منذور من غير لحاظ إلى خصوص مادة، وحرمته من حيث اشتماله على الأعراض عن ضيافة الله فلا تساوي، وإن اعتبر التساوي على هذا الوجه فالكون الصلاتي في هذا المكان ملازم للغصب وبالعكس، فتأمل وأنصف لعل الحق لا يتجاوز عما ذكرنا (إلا أن يقال : العام المطلق لا حقيقة له في التحصيل إلا حقيقة الخاص لاتحاد الجعل والوجود، فإذا كان جهتا الوجوب والحرمة أعم وأخص كان تحصلهما واحداً (فيلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح في الحقيقة المتحصلة وفي العموم من وجه حقيقتان) اجتماعهما اتفاقي فلا يلزم من كونهما منشأ الوجوب والحرمة اجتماعهما في ذات (فتأمل) فإنه غير واف، لأن ما ذكر إنما يتم فيما إذا كان العام ذاتياً للخاص وأما إذا كان عرضياً فلا، لأن تحصل الخاص غير تحصل العام العرضي كذا في الحاشية»، ثم ها هنا وجه آخر لفساد هذا التوجيه هو أن اجتماع الحسن والقبح في ذات محصلة إنما يستحيل إذا كانت هي المعروضة لهما بالذات، وهو ممنوع، بل المعروض بالذات الجهة العامة والخاصة فلا استحالة، قال في الحاشية» : إذا كان اللزوم ولو من جانب لزم استحالة التكليف بالمحال، وإن لم يلزم استحالة اجتماع المتنافيين فإن الحرمة تقتضي الاجتناب دائماً، والوجوب الإتيان بالفعل، والدائمة والمطلقة متناقضان وهذا غير واف، فإن الجهة العامة إذا كانت جهة وجوب كما فيما نحن فيه لا يلزم فإن الوجوب إنما يقتضي الإتيان في مادة من المواد والحرمة تقتضي الاجتناب عن الأخص، نعم، لو كان جهة العموم جهة الحرمة لكان له وجه على أنَّا نقول : لا يلزم هذا إلا إذا ألزمنا الأداء من جهة الحرمة، وهو ممنوع، بل الوجوب إنما هو ليأتي بالقضاء في وقت لا تكون هذه الجهة المحرمة، وإنما يصح معها لأنه أدى كما اشتغلت الذمة ناقصاً، لكن لارتكاب المنهي عنه ها هنا يكون آثماً وبهذا يندفع أن إيجاب أمر يلازم القبح لا يليق بجناب الحكيم، حدث العبد، كإيجاب هذا فإنه حث على المعصية، مع كذلك بسبب أن إيجاب شيء بصنع الصوم عند وجود النذر من العبد يليق بجناب الحكيم، وهو ليس حثاً على المعصية، فإنه وجوب عند حدوث فعل لم يكن لازماً عليه فتدبر. ثم إن ها هنا جهتا الوجوب والحرمة ليسا أحدهما الآخر، بل بينهما عموم من وجه، فإن جهة الوجوب المنذورية وجهة الحرمة الإعراض عن ضيافة الله تعالى فلا ينفع التخصيص في الجواب أصلاً (ولنا أيضاً: لو لم يصح اجتماع الوجوب والحرمة لما ثبتت صلاة مكروهة لأن الأحكام) كلها (متضادة، والكون) الذي هو الصلاة (واحد) فلو كانت مكروهة لزم وجود الكراهة والوجوب فيها (فإن المكروه إنما هو الفعل، وإن كانت الكراهة لأجل الوصف وهو الواجب، وإن كان باعتبار الذات لزم الاجتماع، فإن جوّز نظراً إلى اختلاف المتعلق فيجوز في الحرمة والوجوب لذلك، وإن لم يجوّز كانت الصلاة المكروهة باطلة، وهو خلاف الإجماع فلا فرق بين نهي التحريم والتنزيه فتدبر) ولا يرد على هذا التقرير ما في «المختصر » أن ها هنا كوناً واحداً، وهو غصب وصلاة وفي الصلاة المكروهة الكراهة من قبل الوصف، ولو فرض الكراهة من قبل الذات يلتزم فساد الصلاة المكروهة، ووجه الاندفاع جلي غني عن التقرير والإيضاح، ولو فرق بأن نهي التنزيه يتعلق في الأغلب بالوصف، وأما نهي التحريم فيوجب فساد الذات، فالجواب عنه أن النهي عن الشرعيات يقرر المشروعية ويرجع إلى الوصف وبعد التنزل، فالكلام فيما إذا دلت القرينة على أنه لأجل الوصف كما في الصلاة في المكان المغصوب، ولا شك أن هذا النهي والوجوب لا يتضادان، كما أن الكراهة والوجوب كذلك فافهم (واستدل) على المختار (لو لم يصح الاجتماع (لما سقط التكليف بما فيه جهة حرمة، كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، فإن غير الواجب لا يكون مسقطاً، وهل هذا إلا كما يقال : الصلاة من غير وضوء غير صحيحة، لكن يسقط بها التكليف واللازم باطل كيف وقال القاضي وقد سقط) التكليف إجماعاً ورد بمنع تحقق الإجماع) واستند بخروج الإمام أحمد، فتعقب بأنه يدعي إجماع من سبق عليه ولهذا عبر المصنف وقال : (إذ لو كان لعرفه (أحمد) فإن شأنه أجل من أن يخفى عليه الإجماع وفيه أنه لعله عرفه وما عمل به، لأنه لا يرى إجماع غير الصحابة حجة، وفي رواية عنه : لا يرى إجماع غير الخلفاء الأربعة رضي الله عنهم حجة، وهذه مناقشات في السند، ولا السند، ولا وجه للمنع إلا بعدم صحة النقل، والقاضي ثقة، وسيجيء أن الإجماع المنقول بخبر الواحد حجة في حق العمل، ثم لما كان ادعى أن في التفريغ عن الغصب حركة هي تفريغ وشغل والأول واجب والثاني حرام فأشار إلى رده وقال : (ثم ادعاء جهتي التفريغ والغصب في الخروج عنها) أي الدار المغصوبة (فيتعلقان) أي الوجوب والحرمة (به) أي بهذا الخروج من خطأ أبي هاشم غير صحيح في نفس الأمر (كيف ويلزم) حينئذ تكليف المحال فإن الامتثال بالوجوب والنهي المذكورين لا يصح إلا لو خرج ولم يشغل المكان المغصوب والخروج والحركة من غير شغل المكان محال (بل) يلزم التكليف المحال فإنه يلزم الأمر بالخروج والنهي عنه قال واقف الأسرار لأبي هاشم أن يقول : الخروج نفس نقل الأقدام لا وجوب فيه ولا حرمة لكنه مشتمل على وصفين : شغل مكان الغير والتفريغ وبينهما عموم من وجه اتفق اجتماعهما في الخروج، فالأصلح أن يقال : ليس هناك شغل هو غصب، بل شغل بإذن المالك دلالة، لأنه يرضى بتفريغه، فلا وجه للحرمة فتدبر فيه فإنه محل تأمل واستصحاب المعصية في هذا الخروج (حتى يفرغ زجراً) له عن هذا
The second article on rulings and aspect. Here it is not so, for the fast is inseparable from the fast of Nahr (the sacrifice day). (It does not repel the objection against the generality of the proof) because the premises of the proof apply to it, since there is no contradiction when the two aspects differ. If you say: 'The objection applies to the generality of the proof when the two aspects are equal' – then if one excuses by saying that imposing an obligation that is inseparable from the forbidden has no benefit and is unbecoming of the Wise, this excuse applies when the necessity is from one side: imposing something with the necessity of prohibition has no benefit, even though fulfilling this vow and the prohibited are inseparable. I say: There is no objection in the case of equal aspects, for one is required to perform each because it is obligatory or a necessary consequence of an obligation, and to avoid each because it is forbidden or a necessary consequence of the forbidden, so this is an obligation to do the impossible, unlike when the necessity is from one side, for it is permissible that the aspect of obligation be a general matter whose fulfillment occurs in something other than the specific, so there is no obligation to do the impossible. Yes, if the aspect of prohibition were general and the aspect of obligation specific, impossibility would arise, but we do not say this is permissible. We only say that the fast of Nahr is obligatory because it is vowed without regard to a particular material, and its prohibition is due to its including turning away from God's hospitality, so they are not equal. And if equality is considered in this way, then the prayer act in this place is inseparable from usurpation and vice versa. So reflect and be fair; perhaps the truth does not go beyond what we have mentioned. (Unless it is said: The absolute general has no reality in actualization except the reality of the specific due to the unity of the ruling and existence. So when the two aspects of obligation and prohibition are more general and more specific, their actualization is one, so the conjunction of good and evil in the actualized reality would be necessary. And in the case of 'general in one respect', there are two realities, and their conjunction is accidental, so from their being the basis of obligation and prohibition, their conjunction in the essence does not follow. (So reflect) for it is insufficient, because what is mentioned only holds when the general is essential to the specific, but if it is accidental, then it does not, for the actualization of the specific is not the actualization of the accidental general. Thus in the gloss. Then here is another aspect for the corruption of this direction: that the conjunction of good and evil in an actualized essence is only impossible if the essence itself is the subject of both essentially, and this is denied. Rather, the essential subject is the general and specific aspects, so no impossibility. He said in the gloss: 'If there is necessity even from one side, the impossibility of obliging the impossible would follow, even if the impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories does not follow, for the prohibition demands avoidance always, and the obligation demands performance of the act, and the permanent and the absolute are contradictory, and this is insufficient. For if the general aspect is an aspect of obligation as in our case, it does not follow, for obligation only demands performance in some material, and prohibition demands avoidance of the specific. Yes, if the general aspect were the aspect of prohibition, it would have some force. However, we say this only follows if we require performance from the aspect of prohibition, and this is denied. Rather, obligation is to perform an act at a time when this prohibited aspect is not present, and it is valid along with it because he performs it as the obligation was imperfectly fulfilled, but by committing the prohibited he becomes sinful. And with this, the objection that imposing something that entails evil is unbecoming of the Wise is repelled. The servant's action, like imposing this, is an encouragement to sin, similarly because imposing something by making the fast at the occurrence of the vow from the servant is becoming of the Wise, and it is not encouragement to sin, for it is an obligation upon the occurrence of an act that was not necessary for him. So ponder. Then here the aspects of obligation and prohibition are not identical; rather, there is a general-in-one-respect relationship between them, for the aspect of obligation is the vowed aspect and the aspect of prohibition is turning away from God's hospitality. So the specification in the answer does not help at all. (And we also argue: If the combination of obligation and prohibition were invalid, then no disliked prayer would be established, because the rulings) are all contradictory, and the act (which is prayer) is one. So if it is disliked, there would be the existence of dislike and obligation in it. (For the disliked is the act itself, even if the dislike is due to the description, and the act is obligatory. If it is due to the essence, combination follows. If one permits it due to the difference in the object of attachment, then it is permissible in prohibition and obligation for that reason. If one does not permit it, then the disliked prayer would be invalid, which is contrary to consensus. So there is no difference between prohibitory and admonitory prohibition; so ponder.) And this exposition is not refuted by what is in 'al-Mukhtasar' that here there is a single act, which is usurpation and prayer, and in disliked prayer the dislike is from the description. If dislike is assumed from the essence, the invalidity of the disliked prayer would be necessary. The way to repel this is clear and does not need exposition. If one distinguishes by saying that admonitory prohibition mostly relates to the description, while prohibitory prohibition necessitates the invalidity of the essence, the answer is that prohibition in religious matters confirms legality and goes back to the description. After concession, the discussion is when the evidence indicates that it is due to the description, as in prayer in usurped land. There is no doubt that this prohibition and obligation are not contradictory, just as dislike and obligation are not. So understand. (He argued) for the chosen opinion: (If combination were invalid, then obligation would not be discharged by an act that has an aspect of prohibition, like prayer in usurped land, for something not obligatory cannot discharge. Is this not like saying: Prayer without ablution is invalid, but it discharges the obligation? And the necessary consequence is false. How can it be while al-Qadi said that obligation has been discharged by consensus? It was responded by denying the occurrence of consensus, and he relied on the exception of Imam Ahmad. So it was objected that he claims consensus of those before him. Hence the author said: (If there were consensus, Ahmad would have known it, for his status is too high for consensus to be hidden from him.) But it is possible that he knew it and did not act upon it, because he does not consider consensus of other than the Companions as authoritative. In one narration from him, he does not consider consensus of other than the four caliphs as authoritative. These are criticisms of the chain, and there is no basis for denial except lack of authenticity of transmission, while al-Qadi is trustworthy. And it will come that consensus transmitted by a single report is authoritative in practice. Then, since he claimed that in vacating the usurped property there is a movement that is vacating and occupying, and the first is obligatory and the second is forbidden, he indicated its refutation and said: (Then the claim of the two aspects of vacating and usurpation in exiting it) i.e. the usurped house (then they relate) i.e. the obligation and prohibition (to it) i.e. this exit, is an error of Abu Hashim, not correct in reality. (How so? And then the obligation to do the impossible follows) because compliance with the mentioned obligation and prohibition would only be possible if he exits without occupying the usurped place, and exiting and movement without occupying the place is impossible. (Rather) obligation to do the impossible follows, for it requires the command to exit and the prohibition of it. The author of 'Asrar' said that Abu Hashim may say: The exit is itself the movement of the feet; it has no obligation or prohibition in itself, but it includes two descriptions: occupying the place of another and vacating, and between them is a general-in-one-respect relation. They happen to coincide in the exit. So it is more appropriate to say: There is no occupation that is usurpation, rather occupation with the owner's permission by implication, because he is pleased with his vacating. So there is no basis for prohibition. So ponder, for it is a place of reflection, and the continuation of sin in this exit until he vacates, as a deterrent to him from this.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the claim that obligation and prohibition cannot coexist in a single act. The author defends the Hanafi position that they can, provided they attach to different aspects. The discussion begins with the example of a vowed fast on the day of Nahr, which is both an obligatory vow and prohibited due to the sacredness of the day. The author argues that because the obligation arises from the vow and the prohibition from turning away from God's hospitality, they are different aspects, so no contradiction. The text then addresses several objections, including the need for benefit in divine commands, the problem of general and specific aspects, and the impossibility of obliging contradictory actions. The author uses analogies from disliked prayers (makruh) and prayer in usurped land to show that dislike and obligation can combine, and by extension prohibition and obligation. The argument also responds to the claim of consensus against combining them, citing Imam Ahmad's exception and questioning the authenticity of that consensus. The passage concludes with a critique of Abu Hashim's analysis of exiting usurped property, showing that his claim of two contradictory aspects fails because the exit occurs with implied permission.

Key Terms
الوجوب (al-wujub): The legal quality of being obligatory; a command that requires performance without alternative.
الحرمة (al-hurma): The legal quality of being forbidden; a prohibition that requires abstention.
الجهة (al-jiha): The aspect or consideration from which a ruling attaches to an act; the specific ground for the ruling.
العموم من وجه (al-umoom min wajh): A relationship between two concepts where they share some instances but differ in others; neither is fully inclusive of the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the Hanafi solution to the problem of coexisting obligation and prohibition?
A) The act is considered based on its dominant ruling; the stronger ruling overrides the weaker.
B) The rulings attach to different aspects (jihaat) of the same act, not to the act as a single essence.
C) Obligation and prohibition cannot coexist; one must be invalid or abrogated.
D) The act is valid but the obligation is suspended until the prohibition is removed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that obligation and prohibition can coexist in a single act by attaching to different legal aspects, a principle essential for resolving apparent contradictions in Islamic law.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
15 min
Coexistence of Obligation and Prohibition in One Act
Argues that obligation and prohibition can coexist in one act, using disliked prayer as proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that obligation (wujub) and prohibition (hurma) can coexist in a single act, citing the example of disliked prayer and the consensus on its validity, and differentiate the Hanafi view from opposing positions.

Core Idea
Obligation and prohibition can coexist when the prohibition attaches to a description, not the essence.

One of the key debates in usul al-fiqh is whether a single act can be simultaneously obligatory and prohibited. At first glance, these rulings seem contradictory. The Hanafi school, however, argues that they can coexist, and uses a powerful example from fiqh: the validity of disliked prayer (salat makruha). In this lesson, we will unpack the Hanafi argument and its logical underpinnings, addressing the objections of those who deny such coexistence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
State the precise point of disagreement: can one act be both wajib and haram at the same time? Note that this is not about different times or persons.
The debate is about simultaneity in one act, not sequential obligation and prohibition.
2
State the Hanafi position and the key proof
The Hanafis hold that coexistence is possible. Their primary proof is the universally accepted validity of disliked prayer (e.g., prayer in a usurped place). In such prayer, obligation (to pray) and dislike (karaha) coexist. If obligation and dislike can coexist, so can obligation and prohibition, since the underlying logic is the same.
The analogy rests on the fact that both karaha and hurma are rulings that can attach to a description of the act.
3
Examine the opposing view and its proof
Some scholars (e.g., Abu Hashim and some Shafi'is) deny the possibility, arguing that a single existence cannot bear two contradictory rulings. They say that an act is either commanded or forbidden, not both.
This view is based on the principle that rulings are tied to the essence (dhat) of the act, not to aspects.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: aspect theory
The Hanafi response is that the prohibition does not attach to the essence of the act but to a description (wasf) – for example, in prayer in a usurped place, the prohibition is due to the usurpation (ghasb), not the prayer itself. The act as a whole is the subject of the command, but one of its descriptions is the cause of the prohibition. Thus, there is no contradiction because the rulings concern different aspects.
This is the core of the Hanafi argument: the rulings are not on the same thing.
5
Consider the additional argument from removal of obligation
The text also argues that if coexistence were impossible, then performing an act that has an aspect of prohibition (like prayer in usurped land) could not remove the original obligation (e.g., the missed prayer). But it does, by consensus (ijma). Therefore, obligation and prohibition must be able to coexist for the obligation to be fulfilled.
This is a second proof, using the principle that the obligatory act must be valid to remove the obligation.
6
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of this dispute: in the Hanafi school, prayer in a usurped place is valid but makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked), while in some other schools it may be invalid (batil). This affects the validity of the prayer and the need for repetition.
The difference is not merely theoretical; it directly affects whether a Muslim's prayer is accepted and whether the obligation is discharged.

الكراهة من قبل الوصف، ولو فرض الكراهة من قبل الذات يلتزم فساد الصلاة المكروهة، ووجه الاندفاع جلي غني عن التقرير والإيضاح، ولو فرق بأن نهي التنزيه يتعلق في الأغلب بالوصف، وأما نهي التحريم فيوجب فساد الذات، فالجواب عنه أن النهي عن الشرعيات يقرر المشروعية ويرجع إلى الوصف وبعد التنزل، فالكلام فيما إذا دلت القرينة على أنه لأجل الوصف كما في الصلاة» في المكان المغصوب، ولا شك أن هذا النهي والوجوب لا يتضادان، كما أن الكراهة والوجوب كذلك فافهم (واستدل) على المختار (لو لم يصح الاجتماع (لما سقط التكليف بما فيه جهة حرمة، كالصلاة في الأرض المغصوبة، فإن غير الواجب لا يكون مسقطاً، وهل هذا إلا كما يقال : الصلاة من غير وضوء غير صحيحة، لكن يسقط بها التكليف واللازم باطل كيف وقال القاضي وقد سقط) التكليف إجماعاً ورد بمنع تحقق الإجماع) واستند بخروج الإمام أحمد، فتعقب بأنه يدعي إجماع من سبق عليه ولهذا عبر المصنف وقال : (إذ لو كان لعرفه (أحمد) فإن شأنه أجل من أن يخفى عليه الإجماع وفيه أنه لعله عرفه وما عمل به، لأنه لا يرى إجماع غير الصحابة حجة، وفي رواية عنه : لا يرى إجماع غير الخلفاء الأربعة رضي الله عنهم حجة، وهذه مناقشات في السند، ولا السند، ولا وجه للمنع إلا بعدم صحة النقل، والقاضي ثقة، وسيجيء أن الإجماع المنقول بخبر الواحد حجة في حق العمل، ثم لما كان ادعى أن في التفريغ عن الغصب حركة هي تفريغ وشغل والأول واجب والثاني ،حرام فأشار إلى رده وقال : (ثم ادعاء جهتي التفريغ والغصب في الخروج عنها) أي الدار المغصوبة (فيتعلقان) أي الوجوب والحرمة (به) أي بهذا الخروج من خطأ أبي هاشم غير صحيح في نفس الأمر (كيف ويلزم) حينئذ تكليف المحال فإن الامتثال بالوجوب والنهي المذكورين لا يصح إلا لو خرج ولم يشغل المكان المغصوب والخروج والحركة من غير شغل المكان محال (بل) يلزم التكليف المحال فإنه يلزم الأمر بالخروج والنهي عنه قال واقف الأسرار لأبي هاشم أن يقول : الخروج نفس نقل الأقدام لا وجوب فيه ولا حرمة لكنه مشتمل على وصفين : شغل مكان الغير والتفريغ وبينهما عموم من وجه اتفق اجتماعهما في الخروج، فالأصلح أن يقال : ليس هناك شغل هو غصب، بل شغل بإذن المالك دلالة، لأنه يرضى بتفريغه، فلا وجه للحرمة فتدبر فيه فإنه محل تأمل واستصحاب المعصية في هذا الخروج (حتى يفرغ زجراً) له
هذا
عن
الفعل الشنيع كما ذهب إليه إمام الحرمين ليس ببعيد قال صاحب البديع» و«المختصر» أنه بعيد، فإنه لا وجه لاستصحاب المعصية في امتثال الأمر، فدفع بأن إدامة الشغل معصية موجبة للزجر على أنه مسبب عن معصية والحق) أن التوبة (ماحية للذنوب فلا وجه للزجر، والخروج بنية التفريغ توبة والله يقبل التوبة عن عباده
It will come later. Fawatih al-Rahmut sharh Musallam al-Thubut volume one. Turning away from the hospitality of Allah Almighty, so specification does not benefit the answer at all. (And we also: if the combination of obligation and prohibition were not valid, then no disliked prayer would be established because all rulings) are mutually contradictory, and the existence (which is prayer) is one. So if it were disliked, it would necessitate the presence of both dislike and obligation in it. (For the disliked is indeed the act, even if the dislike is due to the description which is the obligatory, and if it is considered with respect to the essence, combination is necessary. If it is permitted in view of the difference of the subject, then it is permitted in prohibition and obligation for that reason, and if it is not permitted, then disliked prayer would be invalid, which is contrary to consensus. So there is no difference between the prohibition of prohibition and the prohibition of detestation, so ponder.) And what is in al-Mukhtasar does not refute this presentation, that here there is one existence, which is usurpation and prayer, and in disliked prayer the dislike is due to the description, and if the dislike were assumed to be due to the essence, the invalidity of the disliked prayer would be necessitated. The manner of rebuttal is clear, needless of presentation and clarification. And if one distinguishes by saying that the prohibition of detestation relates mostly to the description, while the prohibition of prohibition necessitates the corruption of the essence, then the answer to that is that the prohibition regarding religious matters establishes legitimacy and goes back to the description, and after concession, the discussion is about when the evidence indicates that it is due to the description, as in prayer in the usurped place. There is no doubt that this prohibition and obligation are not contradictory, just as dislike and obligation are similarly, so understand. (And he argued) for the chosen view: (if the combination were not valid, then obligation would not be removed by something that has an aspect of prohibition, like prayer in usurped land, for that which is not obligatory is not remover. And is this not like what is said: prayer without ablution is invalid, yet obligation is removed by it? And the consequent is false. How, and the judge said: obligation has been removed by consensus? It was replied by denying the verification of consensus) and it was based on the exception of Imam Ahmad. He countered that he claims consensus of those before him, and for this the author said: (If he had known it, Ahmad would have known it, for his status is too great for consensus to be hidden from him. But it is possible he knew it and did not act on it, because he does not consider the consensus of other than the Companions as a proof, and in one narration from him: he considers only the consensus of the four Caliphs as proof. These are discussions about the chain, and there is no basis for refusal except the lack of authenticity of the transmission, and the judge is trustworthy. It will come that consensus transmitted by a solitary report is a proof in right of action. Then, when he claimed that in vacating the usurped property there is a motion that is vacating and occupying, and the first is obligatory and the second is forbidden, he indicated its rebuttal and said: (Then the claim of the two aspects of vacating and usurpation in exiting from it) i.e., the usurped house, (so they both relate) i.e., obligation and prohibition, (to it) i.e., to this exit, is an error of Abu Hashim, not correct in reality. (How, and it necessitates) then the imposition of the impossible, for compliance with the aforementioned obligation and prohibition is not valid except if he exits and does not occupy the usurped place, and exiting and motion without occupying the place is impossible. (Rather) it necessitates the imposition of the impossible, for it necessitates the command to exit and the prohibition of it. The master of secrets said to Abu Hashim: Exit is the very movement of feet; there is no obligation or prohibition in it, but it includes two descriptions: occupying another's place and vacating. Between them is general-and-particular-from-one-aspect; they happen to combine in the exit. So it is more appropriate to say: there is no occupation that is usurpation, rather occupation with the owner's permission by indication, because he is pleased with his vacating, so there is no basis for prohibition. So ponder it, for it is a place of reflection. And continuation of sin in this exit (until he vacates as a deterrent) to him. This is about the reprehensible act, as Imam al-Haramayn adhered to, not far-fetched. The author of al-Badi' and al-Mukhtasar said it is far-fetched, for there is no basis for continuation of sin in compliance with the command. It is rebutted by saying that continuation of occupation is a sin necessitating deterrence, on the basis that it is caused by sin. And the truth is that repentance (eliminates sins, so there is no basis for deterrence, and exiting with the intention of vacating is repentance, and Allah accepts repentance from His servants.)
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute on whether obligation (wujub) and prohibition (hurma) can coexist in a single act. The author argues they can, using disliked prayer (salat makruha) as a proof: if coexistence were impossible, the universally accepted validity of disliked prayer would be negated. The discussion proceeds through several arguments: (1) The initial argument from disliked prayer: since a disliked act is still valid (e.g., prayer in a usurped place is valid but disliked), obligation and dislike coexist, hence obligation and prohibition can also coexist when the prohibition is due to a description. (2) Rebuttal of an objection from al-Mukhtasar: the objection claims a single act cannot have both aspects, but the author clarifies that the prohibition (like dislike) relates to the description, not the essence. (3) A second argument: if coexistence were invalid, then performing an act with an aspect of prohibition (like prayer in usurped land) would not remove the original obligation (e.g., the missed prayer), but practice and consensus show it does. (4) Discussion of Imam Ahmad's view on consensus, noting that his differing stance does not break the consensus of earlier scholars. (5) Critical analysis of Abu Hashim's position on exiting usurped land: he claimed two aspects (vacating and occupying) attach to the same act, leading to impossible demands. The author rejects this, explaining the exit can be considered a single act with permissible vacating. (6) The issue of continuing sin: some say sin persists until the person vacates, but the response is that repentance erases sin, so exiting with intention of vacating is repentance. The core Hanafi position affirmed throughout is that obligation and prohibition can coexist in one act when the prohibition is due to a secondary description, not the essence.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujub): Obligation or command to perform an act
حرمة (hurma): Prohibition or forbidden nature of an act
كراهة (karaha): Dislike or detestation, either tahrimi (prohibitive) or tanzihi (recommended avoidance)
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of scholars, used as proof for validity of disliked prayer
نهي التحريم (nahy al-tahrim): Prohibition that indicates an act is haram (forbidden)
نهي التنزيه (nahy al-tanzih): Prohibition that indicates an act is makruh (disliked) but not sinful

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the argument that obligation and prohibition cannot coexist refuted by the example of disliked prayer?
A) Because disliked prayer is invalid in all schools, so it proves nothing.
B) Because disliked prayer is agreed to be valid, showing that obligation (wujub) and dislike (karaha) coexist, so prohibition can similarly coexist with obligation.
C) Because the prohibition in disliked prayer is on the essence, not the description.
D) Because Imam Ahmad denied consensus, so the argument fails.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that obligation and prohibition can coexist in one act due to the distinction between essence and description, a principle derived from the consensus on the validity of disliked prayer.

Is the Recommended Act (Mandub) Truly Commanded?
A dispute on the literal vs. metaphorical application of 'command'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether the mandub is literally commanded and explain why the school uses a metaphorical reading.

Core Idea
True command implies obligation; the mandub is only metaphorically commanded.

In this lesson, we explore a key usul dispute: does the term 'command' (amr) apply literally to recommended acts (mandub) or only to obligatory ones? The Hanafi school insists on a strict definition, while other verifiers see the imperative form as sufficient. This disagreement affects how we understand the nature of divine address and the scope of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
Read the passage and locate the Hanafi claim: the mandub is not commanded except metaphorically (majazan). Note that this is the position presented first.
The Arabic uses 'عند الحنفية' (according to the Hanafis).
2
Identify the opposing view
Find the opposing position: 'قيل عن المحققين : نعم إنه مأمور به حقيقة' – some verifiers, including al-Qadi al-Baqillani, say it is truly commanded.
The word 'قيل' (it was said) introduces the alternative.
3
Understand the core reasoning
The Hanafi stance rests on the usul principle that a true command (amr haqiqa) necessarily implies obligation (wujub). Since mandub does not obligate, it cannot be a true command.
This is a function of the haqiqa vs. majaz distinction.
4
Analyse the practical difference (thamarah)
Consider the consequence: if a person vows to perform 'every command of Allah', does that include mandub acts? Hanafis would restrict it to obligatory acts; Baqillani would include recommended ones.
This shows how a seemingly abstract dispute has real fiqh implications.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The passage presents the Hanafi view as the madhhab position. No explicit tarjih argument is given here, but the structure implies the Hanafi view is the relied-upon one.
Other usul works provide more extensive tarjih arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المندوب هل هو مأمور به؟ فعند الحنفية (لا يكون مأموراً به إلا مجازاً، وقيل) في شرح المختصر عن المحققين : نعم إنه مأمور به (حقيقة) وهو قول القاضي الباقلاني
Issue: (Is the mandub (recommended act) commanded? According to the Hanafis: it is not commanded except metaphorically. And it was said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar from the verifiers: Yes, it is truly commanded. And that is the saying of al-Qadi al-Baqillani.)
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This short passage presents a classic usul dispute: Is the mandub (recommended act, also called mustahabb or sunna) actually an object of a command (amr)? The Hanafi position is that the mandub is not truly (haqiqa) commanded; rather, the term 'command' is applied to it metaphorically (majazan). This is because a true command entails obligation (wujub) and binding enforcement, whereas the mandub is not obligatory. In contrast, some verifiers (muhaqqiqun), including al-Qadi al-Baqillani (a major Ash'ari theologian), hold that the mandub is truly commanded, meaning that the divine address to perform it is a genuine command, but it is not binding—the command is for recommendation, not obligation. The difference reflects a subtle point in usul: whether the essence of command (amr) necessarily implies obligation or whether it can be used for less than obligation depending on qarina (evidence). Hanafis reserve the term 'command' for what is wajib/fard, and consider the mandub as merely recommended, hence the metaphor.

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act, whose performance is rewarded but omission is not punished
مأمور به (mamur bih): That which is commanded; object of a command
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal or true sense
مجازاً (majazan): Metaphorically or figuratively

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, why is the mandub not considered truly commanded (mamur bih haqiqa)?
A) Because the Prophet did not command recommended acts.
B) Because true command necessarily implies obligation, and mandub is not obligatory.
C) Because mandub acts are not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because only al-Qadi al-Baqillani considered them commanded.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that true command (amr haqiqa) entails obligation; the mandub, being non-obligatory, is only metaphorically commanded.

Defining Taklif: Why the Recommended (Mandub) Is Not a Legal Obligation
Defines taklif and excludes mandub through ease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi definition of taklif and explain why mandub and mubah are not considered taklif.

Core Idea
Taklif implies burden; mandub leaves ease, so it cannot be a taklif.

In Hanafi usul, taklif is a key concept that defines what is obligatory or prohibited. This passage clarifies that recommended acts (mandub) are not part of taklif because they leave room for ease. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the categories of the Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The passage begins with the claim: 'Mandub is not a taklif.' Recognize this as the thesis.
2
Understand the rationale
The reason given: 'because one is in ease (sa'a) regarding its abandonment, and there is no taklif in ease.' Taklif implies haraj (difficulty), not ease.
The term 'sa'a' is key—ease/flexibility is the opposite of burden.
3
Analyze the opposing view
The text mentions Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini who apparently called mandub a taklif. The author interprets him as meaning the obligation to believe the act is recommended, which is indeed a taklif, but that is a different ruling.
The dispute is resolved as verbal: if taklif is defined broadly as any divine address, it could apply; but Hanafis define it narrowly.
4
Apply the Hanafi position
Thus, in Hanafi usul, taklif only applies to commands and prohibitions that impose a burden. Mandub and mubah are outside taklif because they lack that burden.
This affects how we classify acts: a mandub is not a duty, so its omission incurs no sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المندوب ليس بتكليف، لأنه في سعة من تركه ولا تكليف في السعة (خلافاً للأستاذ) أبي إسحاق رحمه الله ، ولما كان كلامه بظاهره فاسداً ولا يليق بشأن التحرير أن يتفوه به، أوّلوا كلامه، وأشار إليه المصنف وقال : ( ولعله أراد وجوب اعتقاد الندبية أي مندوبية المندوب، ولا شك أنه تكليف ولهذا جعل المباح تكليفاً لأن اعتقاد إباحته واجب (لكن ذلك حكم آخر) لا يلزم منه كون المندوبية والإباحة تكليفاً، فالنزاع لفظي ولو) جعل نفس خطاب الشارع) بالإباحة كان أو بالندب أو بالتحريم أو بالكراهة، لا مطلق الخطاب الذي يعم القصص، فإنه بعيد جداً ( تكليفاً لم يبعد) ويؤول النزاع حينئذٍ أيضاً إلى اللفظ فقط (فافهم).
Issue: The recommended (mandub) is not a taklif (legal obligation), because one is at ease in leaving it, and there is no taklif in ease. Contrary to al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq (may Allah have mercy on him). Since his statement in its apparent form is corrupt and not befitting the status of Tahreer to utter, they interpreted his statement. The author referred to it and said: Perhaps he meant the obligation of believing in the recommendedness of the mandub, and there is no doubt that this is a taklif. For this reason, the permissible (mubah) is made a taklif because belief in its permissibility is obligatory. But that is another ruling; it does not necessitate that mandubiyya (recommendedness) and ibaha (permissibility) are themselves taklif. So the dispute is verbal. If one makes the very address of the Lawgiver—whether about permissibility, recommendation, prohibition, or dislike—a taklif (not any address, like stories, for that is farfetched), then it would not be farfetched, and the dispute then would also be merely verbal. Understand.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling that the recommended (mandub) is not a taklif (legal obligation that imposes burden). The reason is that there is ease (sa'a) in leaving it, and taklif implies difficulty, not ease. The text notes a contrary view from Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, but his statement is interpreted to mean that the obligation is to believe in the recommendedness of the act, which is indeed a taklif (obligation of belief). Similarly, the mubah (permissible) is not a taklif in itself, but believing its permissibility is obligatory. The dispute is thus verbal: if one defines taklif as any address of the Lawgiver (be it about permission, recommendation, etc.), then it could be considered taklif, but that is just a matter of terminology. The Hanafi position is that taklif properly applies only to commands and prohibitions that impose a burden, thus excluding mandub and mubah.

Key Terms
التكليف (at-taklīf): A legal obligation that imposes a burden on the mukallaf, restricted to commands and prohibitions that entail haraj.
السعة (as-sa'a): Ease or flexibility; the opposite of haraj. The presence of sa'a in an act removes it from taklif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the mandub not considered a taklif?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because there is ease in leaving it
C) Because it is not rewarded
D) Because it is not sinful to do

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, taklif is defined by the presence of burden (haraj); acts like mandub and mubah have ease (sa'a) and are therefore outside the scope of taklif.

Makruh: Disliked but Not Prohibited – Similar to Mandub
Defines makruh by analogy to mandub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define makruh in Hanafi usul, identify its absence of prohibition and taklif, and compare it with mandub.

Core Idea
Makruh is not prohibited; it shares with mandub the absence of legal obligation.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut states that the makruh (disliked) is like the mandub (recommended) in that neither involves a prohibition (nahy) nor a legal obligation (taklif). The proof is the same as previously discussed for mandub. Understanding this classification is essential for distinguishing between categories of hukm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
What are makruh and mandub? They are two of the five ahkam taklifiyyah.
Makruh is disliked; mandub is recommended.
2
State the common feature
Both lack nahy (prohibition) and taklif (obligation). That is, doing makruh is not haram, and leaving mandub is not wajib.
3
Recall the proof
The text refers to a proof that passed (likely the same as for mandub: that the command or prohibition is absent). This is a qiyas: just as mandub is not wajib, makruh is not haram.
The proof is not restated due to truncation.
4
Apply the definition
Given any act, determine if it is makruh by checking if there is a dispraise that does not amount to prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المكروه كالمندوب لا نهي ولا تكليف والدليل عليه هو الدليل) الذي مر في عدم
Issue: The disliked (makruh) is like the recommended: there is no prohibition and no taklif. The proof for it is the proof that passed regarding the non-existence of...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This brief passage asserts that the makruh (disliked) shares the same status as the mandub (recommended) in that neither involves a prohibition (nahy) nor a legal obligation (taklif). The proof is said to be the same proof that was mentioned earlier (presumably in the previous discussion about mandub). The text is incomplete, likely referring back to earlier arguments. This is a standard Hanafi position: the makruh is not sinful to do, just as the mandub is not sinful to leave. Both are categories that do not carry the weight of prohibition or obligation.

Key Terms
المكروه (al-makrūh): An act that is disliked but not prohibited; leaving it is rewarded but doing it is not sinful.
المندوب (al-mandūb): An act that is recommended but not obligatory; doing it is rewarded but leaving it is not sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Both are obligatory
C) Both lack prohibition and legal obligation
D) Both are recommended

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, makruh is defined by the absence of both prohibition (nahy) and legal obligation (taklif), similar to mandub.

The Definition of Amr and the Status of Mandub
Dispute with Shafi'is over whether mandub is commanded

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on amr (command) and distinguish it from the Shafi'i view by analyzing the hadith of siwak and logical proofs.

Core Idea
Amr is haqiqah only for obligation; the mandub is not commanded but merely recommended.

The meaning of 'amr' (command) is a foundational usul issue with direct impact on how we classify acts. This lesson examines a key dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is: is the mandub (recommended act) truly commanded? The Hanafi school, through linguistic and textual proofs, holds that amr in its real sense applies only to obligations, while the Shafi'is argue for a broader usage. We will work through the arguments to grasp the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the core question: Is the mandub (recommended act) ma'mur bihi (commanded) in the real sense of amr?
The Hanafi school says no, the Shafi'is say yes.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafis argue that amr is haqiqah (literal) only for obligation (wujub). Therefore, mandub is not commanded; it is merely called to (mad'uww) metaphorically.
This is based on the definition of amr as a specific utterance (if'al) that demands action with determination.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
Examine the two proofs: (a) Hadith: 'Were it not that I would impose hardship, I would have commanded them with siwak' – showing that command is distinct from recommendation. (b) Logical: If mandub were commanded, leaving it would be a sin, but there is no sin in leaving it.
These proofs demonstrate that amr in its real sense is tied to obligation.
4
Understand the Shafi'i objections
The Shafi'is object using linguistic division and consensus: (a) Linguists divide amr into command of obligation and command of recommendation. (b) Mandub is obedience by consensus, and obedience is acting on a command.
They argue that amr is shared (mushtarak) between wujub and nadb.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafis rebut: (a) The linguistic division is of the imperative form (sighah), not the meaning of amr. (b) Obedience includes both commanded and recommended acts. (c) The same linguists divide amr into threat and permission, but no one says those are real commands. Thus, the Shafi'i argument is invalid.
The passage concludes that mandub is not ma'mur bihi in reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجمهور الشافعية لنا أن الأمر حقيقة في القول المخصوص هو أفعل (وذلك القول حقيقة في الإيجاب فقط) فالأمر حقيقة فيه، قال واقف الأسرار الإلهية قدس سره، إن كون اللفظ حقيقة لفظ لا يلزم أن يكون حقيقة فيما هو حقيقة فيه، بل هو حقيقة مطلقاً، سواء كان هذا اللفظ حقيقة أو مجازاً، ألا ترى الفاتحة حقيقة في القول المخصوص وإن كان فيها ألفاظ مجازية، والحق أن يقال : الواو في قوله : وذلك القول للحال من القول المخصوص والحاصل أن الأمر حقيقة في القول المخصوص حال كونه للوجوب، فالمندوب ليس مأموراً به لعدم الحتم هناك لكن ينبو عن هذا التوجيه بعض عبارات الكتب الأخر، نعم إنه دليل مستقل (وأيضاً: لو كان) المندوب مأموراً به لكان تركه معصية لأنها مخالفة الأمر أو النهي واللازم باطل فإنه لا حرج على تارك المندوب إليه (و) أيضاً لو كان المندوب مأموراً به لما صح) قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم : «لو لم أشق على أمتي لأمرتهم بالسواك عند كل وضوء») رواه النسائي (لأنه) يفهم أنه ما أمرهم وندبهم إليه والقول بالتجوز خلاف الأصل لا يصار إليه، وأمثال هذه العبارات شائعة الشافعية قالوا : أوّلاً إنه أي المندوب (طاعة إجماعاً، والطاعة فعل المأمور به قلنا لا نسلم أنه فعل المأمور به فقط (بل) هو (و) فعل (المندوب إليه) أيضاً (و) قالوا (ثانياً: أرباب اللغة قسموا الأمر (إلى أمر إيجاب وأمر ندب ومورد القسمة مشترك) فالأمر مشترك بين أمر الندب وأمر الإيجاب قلنا : هم قسموا أيضاً إلى أمر تهديد وأمر إباحة إلى غير ذلك فيلزم أن يكون المهدد عليه والمباح مأمورين ، ولم يذهب إليه ذاهب، هذا نقض، ثم أشار إلى الحل بقوله : (فهم توسعوا عن حقيقة الأمر وقسموه أخذاً بالمعنى المجازي فتدبر. وأيضاً ما قسموا مدلول الأمر، إنما قسموا صيغة الأمر إليهما، فلا يلزم أن يكون أمر الندب أمراً حقيقة، فإن قلت : فيلزم أنه صيغة أمر، قلت: لا بأس به، فإنه صيغة أمر في اصطلاحهم، كذا في «الحاشية».
And the majority of the Shafi'is: For us, that 'amr' is a reality in the specific utterance 'if'al' (do), and that utterance is a reality only in making incumbent; thus 'amr' is a reality in it. Said the founder of divine secrets, may his secret be sanctified: That a word is a reality as a word does not require that it be a reality in what it is a reality for, rather it is a reality absolutely, whether this word is real or metaphorical. Do you not see that al-Fatiha is a reality in the specific utterance even though it contains metaphorical words. The truth is to say: The 'waw' in his statement 'and that utterance' is for the state from the specific utterance. The conclusion: 'amr' is a reality in the specific utterance when it is for obligation; thus the mandub is not commanded because there is no determination there. However, some expressions of other books are repelled by this directive. Yes, it is an independent proof. (And also: If the mandub were commanded, leaving it would be disobedience because it is contravening the command or prohibition, and the consequent is false, for there is no burden on the one who leaves the mandub ilayh.) (And) also, if the mandub were commanded, his saying, peace and blessings be upon him and his family: 'Were it not that I would impose hardship on my community, I would have commanded them with the siwak at every wudu'' – narrated by al-Nasa'i – would not be valid (because) it is understood that he did not command them but encouraged them, and resorting to metaphor is contrary to the principle and is not to be pursued. Such expressions are common. The Shafi'is said: Firstly, that it (the mandub) is obedience by consensus, and obedience is the doing of the commanded act. We said: We do not accept that it is only the doing of the commanded act, but rather it is also (and) the doing of (the mandub ilayh). (And) they said: Secondly, the linguists divided 'amr' into a command of obligation and a command of recommendation, and the subject of division is common; thus 'amr' is shared between the command of recommendation and the command of obligation. We said: They also divided into a command of threat and a command of permission, etc., so it would follow that the one threatened and the permitted are commanded, but no one has held that. This is a refutation. Then he pointed to the solution by his saying: 'They were expansive about the reality of amr and divided it taking the metaphorical meaning, so reflect.' And also, they did not divide the meaning of amr; rather they divided the imperative form into the two, so it does not follow that the command of recommendation is amr in reality. If you say: It follows that it is an imperative form. I say: No problem, for it is an imperative form in their terminology. Thus in the Hashiyah.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafis and the majority of Shafi'is concerning the definition of amr (command) and whether the mandub (recommended act) is genuinely commanded (ma'mur bihi). The author first states the Hanafi position: amr (the imperative form افعل) is haqiqah (a real term) only for obligation (wujub), not for recommendation. The argument begins by clarifying that a word being a reality (haqiqah) in a specific utterance does not mean it must be a reality in its referent; rather, it is a reality absolutely regardless of whether the utterance contains metaphors. An analogy is given with Surah al-Fatiha, which is a reality as a specific utterance even though it contains metaphorical expressions. The correct analysis is that the waw in 'wa dhalika al-qawl' is for the state (hal) of the specific utterance, meaning amr is a reality when the utterance is for obligation. Thus, the mandub is not commanded because there is no determination (hazm) in it. However, some expressions from other books deviate from this directive. The passage then provides independent proofs: (1) If mandub were commanded, leaving it would be a sin (ma'siyah) because sin is contravening a command or prohibition, but there is no burden on the one who leaves a mandub act. (2) The hadith that the Prophet did not command siwak at every wudu' out of mercy shows that he did not command but only recommended (nadb), and resorting to metaphor is contrary to the original principle. The Shafi'is object: (a) The mandub is obedience (ta'ah) by consensus, and obedience is acting on the commanded (al-ma'mur bihi). The Hanafis respond that obedience is not limited to the commanded but also includes the mandub ilayh. (b) Linguists divide amr into command of obligation and command of recommendation, implying that amr is shared (mushtarak) between them. The Hanafis counter by noting that linguists also divide amr into command of threat (tahdid) and command of permission (ibahah), but no one says the threatened or permitted person is commanded. This is a refutation (naqd). The solution is that the linguists were being expansive (tawassu'u) about the reality of amr, using the metaphorical sense. Moreover, they divided the imperative form (sighah), not the meaning of amr, so it does not follow that the command of recommendation is amr in reality. It is merely an imperative form in their terminology. The passage concludes with a reference to the Hashiyah.

Key Terms
أمر (amr): The imperative form (صيغة أفعل) used for demanding an action with a sense of determination (إيجاب).
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its original, literal sense (what it was coined for).
مندوب (mandub): An action whose performance is recommended but not obligatory; leaving it is not sinful.
إيجاب (ijab): Making an action obligatory; the assignment of a legal obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the word 'amr' is shared between obligation and recommendation.
B) Because if it were commanded, leaving it would be a sin, but there is no sin in leaving a mandub act.
C) Because the linguists divided amr into only two categories.
D) Because the Prophet never used the imperative form for recommendation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines amr strictly for obligation, thus mandub is not truly commanded, supported by the hadith of siwak and logical reasoning.

Mandub is Not Taklif: The Scope of Legal Obligation in Hanafi Usul
A usul rule defining the scope of taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafis consider mandub not to be a taklif and identify the verbal nature of the dispute with Abu Ishaq.

Core Idea
Taklif implies constraint; mandub has no constraint, so it is not a legal obligation.

This passage addresses a fundamental question in usul al-fiqh: when does a divine command become a legal obligation (taklif)? The Hanafi position distinguishes between commands that carry a burden (haraj) and those that leave the mukallaf in ample scope (sa'ah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly categorizing acts in fiqh and avoiding confusing mandub with wajib.

Guided Path
1
Define taklif in Hanafi usul
Start with the definition: taklif is a legal address that imposes a constraint or hardship on the mukallaf. The key element is haraj (difficulty) in compliance or abstention.
This definition is from the legal_definition_dissector.
2
Define mandub and its key property (sa'ah)
Mandub is an act whose performance is rewarded but whose abandonment is not sinful. The performer has wide latitude (sa'ah) to leave it without blame.
Sa'ah contrasts with the constraint inherent in taklif.
3
Apply the definition of taklif to mandub
Since mandub by definition involves no constraint in its abandonment, it cannot satisfy the condition for taklif. Therefore, it is outside the scope of legal obligation.
This is the core inference (talil) of the passage.
4
Address the counter-view (Abu Ishaq)
Abu Ishaq considered mandub a taklif. The Hanafi response reinterprets his view: he may have meant the obligation to believe in the mandubiyyah (recommendability) of the act, which is indeed a taklif. This shows the dispute reduces to wording.
If the very address of the Lawgiver is called taklif, the dispute becomes purely verbal.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi rule
The correct Hanafi position is clear: mandub is not taklif. The practical consequence is that there is no sin or punishment for leaving a mandub act, unlike wajib acts.
This principle underlies many rulings in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المندوب ليس بتكليف، لأنه في سعة من تركه ولا تكليف في السعة (خلافاً للأستاذ) أبي إسحاق رحمه الله ، ولما كان كلامه بظاهره فاسداً ولا يليق بشأن التحرير أن يتفوه به، أوّلوا كلامه، وأشار إليه المصنف وقال : ( ولعله أراد وجوب اعتقاد الندبية أي مندوبية المندوب، ولا شك أنه تكليف ولهذا جعل المباح تكليفاً لأن اعتقاد إباحته واجب (لكن ذلك حكم آخر) لا يلزم منه كون المندوبية والإباحة تكليفاً، فالنزاع لفظي ولو) جعل نفس خطاب الشارع) بالإباحة كان أو بالندب أو بالتحريم أو بالكراهة، لا مطلق الخطاب الذي يعم القصص، فإنه بعيد جداً ( تكليفاً لم يبعد) ويؤول النزاع حينئذٍ أيضاً إلى اللفظ فقط (فافهم).
Issue: The mandub is not a legal obligation (taklif), because he is in ample scope regarding its abandonment, and there is no taklif in ample scope. Contrary to the master Abu Ishaq, may Allah have mercy on him. Since his statement in its apparent form is corrupt and not befitting the status of the author to utter, they interpreted his statement. The author pointed to it and said: (Perhaps he meant the obligation of believing in the recommendability (nadbiyyah) i.e., the mandubiyyah of the mandub, and there is no doubt that it is a taklif; for this reason, he made mubah a taklif because believing in its permissibility is obligatory. (But that is another ruling) it does not necessarily follow that mandubiyyah and ibahah are taklif. So the dispute is verbal. And if) one makes the very address of the Lawgiver (bi'l-ibahah, whether it is by recommendation or by prohibition or by abomination, not absolute address that includes stories, for that is very far) (a taklif, it would not be far). And the dispute then also reduces to mere wording. (So understand).
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) establishing that the mandub (recommended act) is not a taklif (legal obligation) because the one who leaves it is in ample scope (sa'ah) — there is no hardship or constraint. This is contrary to Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini, who considered it a taklif. The author notes that Abu Ishaq's apparent statement is corrupt, so his followers reinterpreted it. The author suggests he may have meant that believing in the mandubiyyah (recommendability) is itself wajib, which is indeed a taklif. But this is a separate ruling; it does not make the mandub itself a taklif. Thus the dispute is verbal (lafzi). If one were to define the very address of the Lawgiver (whether permission, recommendation, prohibition, or abomination) as taklif, then the dispute would still reduce to wording. The text concludes with 'so understand.'

Key Terms
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act whose abandonment is not sinful.
تكليف (taklif): Assigning a legal obligation that entails constraint on the mukallaf.
سعة (sa'ah): Ample scope; freedom from constraint in choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because abandoning it is not sinful and there is no constraint.
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because there is no textual evidence for it.

Key Takeaway
The mandub is not a taklif because taklif requires constraint, and the one who leaves a mandub act is free of constraint—this is a foundational distinction in Hanafi usul.

The Makruh is Like Mandub: No Prohibition or Taklif
Usul rule: qiyas between makruh and mandub

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qiyas between makruh and mandub to determine that a disliked act does not involve a genuine prohibition or legal obligation.

Core Idea
Makruh shares with mandub the absence of binding demand and constraint — no nahy, no taklif.

In Hanafi usul, makruh (disliked) is often confused with haram (prohibited). This passage establishes a clear analogy (qiyas) with mandub (recommended): just as mandub is not a commanded act (ma'mur bihi) and imposes no legal obligation (taklif), so too makruh is not a prohibited act (manhiyy anhu) and imposes no obligation. The proof is the same as previously given for mandub, and the ikhtilaf with other schools mirrors that earlier discussion. Understanding this analogy refines your grasp of the categories of hukm shari and the nature of legal demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl (mandub)
Recall what was established about mandub: it is not a commanded act (ma'mur bihi) and involves no taklif (binding obligation). Its performance is rewarded but not required.
This was covered in the preceding lesson.
2
Identify the far (makruh)
The text asserts that makruh is parallel to mandub in that it also lacks a genuine prohibition (nahy) and does not impose a taklif. The commission of a makruh act is not sinful, but avoidance is preferable.
In Hanafi terminology, this refers to makruh tanzihan (disliked), not makruh tahriman (prohibited-like).
3
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The shared illa is the absence of determination (hazm) and constraint (haraj). Both categories lack the emphatic and binding nature that characterises command and prohibition.
This illa justifies transferring the ruling from mandub to makruh.
4
Construct the qiyas jali
Since the asl (mandub) is not ma'mur bihi and not taklif, and the far (makruh) shares the same illa (lack of hazm and haraj), the ruling is transferred: makruh is also not manhiyy anhu and not taklif.
This is a clear analogy (qiyas jali) because the illa is explicit and shared.
5
Note the ikhtilaf with other schools
The text says 'the difference here is the difference there', meaning the disagreement with those who consider makruh to involve a weak prohibition mirrors the disagreement about whether mandub is commanded. Other schools (e.g. Shafi'is) may hold that makruh does involve a type of nahy.
This ikhtilaf is methodological: it hinges on the definition of nahy and its scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المكروه كالمندوب لا نهي ولا تكليف والدليل عليه هو الدليل الذي مر في عدم كون المندوب مأموراً به وتكليفاً والاختلاف ها هنا هو الاختلاف هناك فتذكر.
Issue: The makruh is like the mandub: no prohibition (nahy) and no legal obligation (taklif). The proof for it is the proof that passed regarding the fact that the mandub is not commanded (ma'mur bihi) and not a taklif. The difference here is the difference there, so remember.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling (hukm) stating that makruh (the disliked act) is analogous to mandub in that it is not genuinely prohibited (nahy) and not a legal obligation (taklif). The proof is the same as the one cited earlier for mandub not being commanded and not being a taklif. The text says 'the difference here is the difference there' meaning the points of ikhtilaf between Hanafis and others regarding makruh mirror those regarding mandub. The reader is advised to recall the previous discussion.

Key Terms
مكروه (makruh): An act whose avoidance is preferable but whose commission is not sinful; it is discouraged.
نهي (nahy): A demand for abstention; a prohibition
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act; performance is rewarded but not obligatory.
تكليف (taklif): A binding legal obligation that entails reward for compliance and punishment for non-compliance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The same proof used for mandub not being commanded or a taklif.
C) Consensus of the companions.
D) Rational argument alone.

Key Takeaway
Makruh, like mandub, involves no genuine prohibition or legal obligation; it is merely discouraged.

Ibahah is a Legal Ruling (Hukm Shar'i)
Definition of ibahah as hukm shar'i with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify ibahah as a hukm shar'i, explain why even original ibahah depends on the Shari'ah, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Mu'tazila claim of pre-Shari'ah rulings.

Core Idea
Even default permissibility is a Lawgiver's ruling, arising from the absence of any proof for constraint.

This lesson establishes that ibahah (permissibility) is a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) like obligation or prohibition. It addresses a subtle but critical point: even what is called 'original ibahah' — the default permissibility of acts before any revealed command — is itself a ruling from the Shari'ah. The text explains that the very absence of a legal basis for constraint (haraj) is the legal basis (mudrik shar'i) for the ruling of choice (takhyir). This refutes the Mu'tazila who argued that rulings like ibahah precede revelation. Understanding this strengthens your grasp of the Hanafi usul principle that all legal rulings are constituted by the Lawgiver's address.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
The passage asserts that ibahah is a hukm shar'i. Determine what definition of hukm shar'i is being used: it is the address of the Lawgiver (khitab al-shar') concerning the acts of the responsible person. Ibahah is a type of this address giving choice (takhyir).
Note the precise term: 'khitab al-shar''.
2
Understand the argument for original ibahah
The text argues that even 'original ibahah' (al-ibahah al-asliyyah) — the permissibility of acts where no explicit ruling is found — is a hukm shar'i. It claims that the absence of a legal basis for constraint (haraj) in doing or leaving an act is itself a legal basis (mudrik shar'i) for the Lawgiver's ruling of choice. So, the absence of evidence of prohibition becomes evidence for permissibility.
This is a key Hanafi principle: the default of permissibility is based on the Shari'ah, not on pure reason.
3
Contrast with the Mu'tazila view
Some Mu'tazila held that rulings such as ibahah exist before the Shari'ah (i.e., are known by reason alone). The text rejects this, stressing that ibahah only exists after the Lawgiver's address. The act of declaring permissibility is a ruling, and rulings only arise from the Lawgiver.
This is a fundamental difference in usul: Hanafis require a textual or analogical basis for all rulings, even permissibility.
4
Trace the legal reasoning (talil)
The argument follows this pattern: Because the Shari'ah did not provide any evidence for constraint, the act remains optional. This 'absence of evidence for constraint' is taken as the Lawgiver's implicit address of choice. Thus, the ruling of ibahah is derived from the Shari'ah via the principle that lack of prohibition indicates permissibility.
This reasoning is used to ground the default ruling (al-asl fi al-ashya' al-ibahah) within the Shari'ah framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الإباحة حكم شرعي لأنه خطاب الشرع (تخييراً والخطاب هو الحكم الشرعي والإباحة الأصلية نوع منه أي من الخطاب بالتخيير لأن كل ما عدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج في فعله وتركه فذلك أي عدم المدرك الشرعي لهما مدرك شرعي لحكم الشارع بالتخيير والإباحة ( الأصلية لا تكون إلا في موضع عدم المدرك الشرعي للحرج في الفعل والترك بل بحكم بخصوصه أصلاً، فهناك مدرك شرعي للحكم بالتخيير، فالإباحة الأصلية فيها حكم بالتخيير فهي لا تكون إلا بعد الشرع، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بالإباحة وغيرها من الأحكام قبل الشرع (وقد تقدم منا أيضاً إحقاق الحق هناك فتذكر.
Issue: Ibahah (permissibility) is a legal ruling (hukm shar'i) because it is the address of the Shari'ah (with option/takhyir). The address is the legal ruling. Original ibahah is a type of it, i.e., from the address of option, because whenever there is no legal basis (mudrik shar'i) for constraint (haraj) in its doing and leaving, then that very absence of legal basis for the two is a legal basis for the ruling of the Lawgiver with option. Original ibahah only occurs in a place where there is no legal basis for constraint in the act and leave, rather it is by a specific ruling originally. So there is a legal basis for the ruling of option. Thus original ibahah has a ruling of option, so it only exists after the Shari'ah. Contrary to some Mu'tazila, for they say ibahah and other rulings exist before the Shari'ah. (And we have also previously established the truth there, so remember.)
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines ibahah (permissibility) as a hukm shar'i (legal ruling) because it is the address (khitab) of the Lawgiver giving choice (takhyir) between doing and leaving. Even what is called 'original ibahah' (al-ibahah al-asliyyah) — the default permissibility of things before revelation — is only a ruling after the Shari'ah has established it. The absence of a legal basis for constraint (haraj) itself becomes a legal basis (mudrik shar'i) for the Lawgiver's ruling of choice. This contradicts some Mu'tazila who claim that ibahah and other rulings exist before the Shari'ah (before revelation). The Hanafi position: all legal rulings (including ibahah) are post-Shari'ah, as the Lawgiver's address is constitutive.

Key Terms
إباحة (ibahah): Permissibility: a ruling of choice (takhyir) from the Lawgiver.
خطاب الشرع (khitab al-shar'): The address of the Lawgiver contained in revelation (Quran and Sunnah).
تخيير (takhyir): Giving free choice; the opposite of obligation (ijab) and prohibition (tahrim).
المدرك الشرعي (al-mudrik al-shar'i): The legal basis or evidence from the Shari'ah that supports a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Mubah is Not a Genus of Wajib
Refutation of the claim that mubah is a genus of wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definition of mubah from a partial definition and explain why mubah and wajib are considered mutabayan (distinct) types in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Mubah and wajib are distinct because mubah requires equality of do/leave, while wajib prohibits leaving.

This passage tackles a subtle logical classification: is mubah (permissible) a broad genus under which wajib (obligatory) falls as a species? At first glance, both involve permission to act. But the Hanafi school insists they are separate types. The key lies in the complete definition of mubah: equality in doing and leaving. Understanding this distinction prevents conceptual confusion in legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Some argue that mubah is a genus (jins) of wajib, because wajib includes permission to act (al-izn fi al-fi‘l), and mubah is also defined as ‘permitted in action’. The text calls this claim into question.
The opponents are unnamed, possibly from other madhhabs or internal Hanafis.
2
Recall the complete Hanafi definition of mubah
Mubah is defined as ‘that whose doing and leaving are equal in the Shari‘ah’ (mutasawi al-fi‘l wa al-tark shar‘an). This is the full haqiqah.
Permission alone is only part of the definition.
3
Contrast with the definition of wajib
Wajib is ‘that which is permitted in action but prohibited from leaving’ (ma'dhun fi al-fi‘l, mamnu‘ ‘an al-tark). Note the crucial restriction on leaving.
This restriction distinguishes wajib from mubah.
4
Analyze the opponent's partial definition
The opponent focuses only on ‘permitted in action’, ignoring the equality condition. They treat this as the essence of mubah, leading to a false genus-species relationship.
This is a classic case of using a partial definition (tamām al-ḥaqīqah vs. juz’ al-ḥaqīqah).
5
Understand the verbal nature of the dispute
The text suggests the disagreement may be verbal (lafẓī): if one defines mubah as ‘permitted in action’ (jā’iz al-fi‘l), then it could be a genus; but if one uses the full definition (jā’iz al-fi‘l wa al-tark), it is distinct.
The Hanafi position adopts the fuller definition to maintain precision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المباح ليس بجنس للواجب، لأنهما نوعان متباينان من الحكم) فإن المباح المتساوي فعله وتركه شرعاً، والواجب المأذون في الفعل الممنوع عن الترك (وظن أنه جنس له، لأن المباح هو المأذون في الفعل، وهو جزء حقيقة (الواجب لأنه المأذون في الفعل مع الحرج في الترك :(قلنا : لا نسلم أن ذلك أي المأذون في الفعل تمام حقيقة المباح، بل هو المتساوي فعلاً وتركاً) فالمأذون في الفعل جزء الحقيقة ولعل النزاع لفظي) فمن جعله جنس الواجب أخذه بمعنى جائز الفعل ومن جعله مبايناً له أخذه بمعنى جائز الفعل والترك.
Issue: Mubah is not a genus (jins) for wajib, because they are two distinct types (naw'an) of ruling. For mubah is that whose doing and leaving are equal in the Shari'ah, and wajib is that which is permitted in doing but prohibited from leaving. (And one thought it is a genus for it, because mubah is that which is permitted in action, and that is part of the reality of wajib, because it is permitted in action with constraint in leaving.) We said: We do not accept that, i.e., that which is permitted in action, is the complete reality of mubah; rather it is that which is equal in doing and leaving. So what is permitted in action is part of the reality. Perhaps the dispute is verbal. For whoever makes it a genus of wajib takes it in the meaning of 'facultative in action' (ja'iz al-fi'l), and whoever makes it distinct takes it in the meaning of 'facultative in action and leaving.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage discusses a logical classification: is mubah (permissible) a genus under which wajib (obligatory) falls as a species? The text rejects this, stating that mubah and wajib are two distinct types (naw'an mutabayanan) of rulings. Mubah is defined as an act whose performance and abandonment are equal in the Shari'ah; wajib is that which is permitted (ma'dhun) to do but prohibited from leaving. Some argue that since mubah means 'permitted in action' (ma'dhun fi al-fi'l), and wajib also includes permission to do (along with constraint), mubah is a part of wajib's reality. The Hanafi response: the complete reality of mubah is equality in doing and leaving, not just permission to do. Permission to do is only one part. Thus the dispute is verbal (lafzi): if one takes 'ja'iz al-fi'l' as the meaning, then mubah can be a genus; if one takes the full definition (equality), it is distinct.

Key Terms
مباح (mubah): Act whose doing and leaving are equally permissible (mutasawi al-fi'l wa al-tark).
واجب (wajib): Act that is permitted to do but prohibited from leaving (ma'dhun fi al-fi'l, mamnu' 'an al-tark).
جنس (jins): A genus; a broader category that includes species.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the complete definition of mubah includes equality of doing and leaving, whereas wajib prohibits leaving.
C) Because mubah is a higher category than wajib.
D) Because there is no textual evidence for such a classification.

Key Takeaway
Mubah and wajib are distinct types in Hanafi usul because mubah requires equality in doing and leaving, while wajib prohibits leaving.
