Refutation of al-Ka'bi: Mubah is Not Wajib
Dispute with al-Ka'bi over mubah and wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi refutation of al-Ka'bi's argument that mubah is wajib and evaluate the causal reasoning used.

Core Idea
Mubah is not wajib because non-existence of haram can be due to absence of will, not the mubah act.

In this lesson, we examine a classic usul dispute between the Hanafis and al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila. al-Ka'bi argued that every permissible act (mubah) is necessarily obligatory (wajib) because performing a mubah act prevents one from committing a haram act, and avoiding haram is wajib. The Hanafis counter that the haram may be absent simply due to lack of will, not because of the mubah act. Understanding this refutation clarifies the Hanafi position on the nature of obligation and causality in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State al-Ka'bi's argument
Identify the premises: (1) Every mubah entails leaving a haram. (2) Every leaving of haram is wajib. Therefore, every mubah is wajib.
This argument relies on the principle that a prerequisite of a wajib becomes wajib.
2
Identify the Hanafi denial
The Hanafis deny the first premise: the haram may not occur because the will (irada) to commit it is absent, not because of the mubah act.
The cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause of existence.
3
Analyze the causal principle
The text states: 'the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence.' If the will to commit haram is absent, the haram does not occur regardless of the mubah act.
The mubah act is only a preventer (mani') accidentally, not the direct cause.
4
Examine the critiques from the Hashiyah and al-Badi'
The Hashiyah suggests that avoiding haram requires either absence of will or performance of mubah, so both are wajib. But the author refutes this, stating that non-existence (of haram) is pure nothingness and cannot be wajib. Al-Badi' attempts to revive the argument but is dismissed.
The key point: a prerequisite is only wajib as long as it is a prerequisite, and mubah is not always a prerequisite.
5
Note the truncation
The passage is incomplete; the final sentence about 'performing the mubah is not' suggests the argument continues. Be aware that the full refutation may include additional points.
This illustrates the importance of consulting complete texts in usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة المباح ليس بواجب بالضرورة خلافاً للكعبي من المعتزلة (واحتج بأن كل مباح ترك حرام أي يلزمه ترك حرام وكل ترك حرام) أو ملزومه واجب ولو مخيراً) فكل مباح واجب ولو مخيراً قلنا : الصغرى ممنوعة أما أولاً فلجواز انعدام الحرام بانعدام المقتضى وهو الإرادة القديمة أو الحادثة (مثلاً) بناء على أن علة العدم عدم علة الوجود، وحينئذ لا يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى فعل المباح الذي هو المانع لوجود الحرام، كيف لا وأن عدم المقتضى كافٍ في عدم الحرام، فوجود المباح بعد ذلك لا دخل له في عدم الحرام ، قال في الحاشية» لدفعه أنه لا بد لترك الحرام من أحد الأمرين : إما عدم الإرادة، أو فعل المباح فكل واجب ولو تخييراً، ثم قال وفيه ما فيه، ووجهه أن المقتضى للعدم بالذات هو عدم الإرادة، وأما المانع فعلة بالعرض لا ينسب إليه العدم إلا عند وجود المقتضى، وأن العدم لا شيء محض لا يصلح للوجوب، ولو أريد الكف فلا نزاع في وجوبه . قال في «البديع وغيره : الحق أنه لا مخلص عنه بعد تسليم أن مقدمة الواجب واجب فإن فعل المباح مقدمة لترك الحرام الذي هو الواجب، وهذا ليس بشيء، لأن المقدمة لا تجب إلا ما دامت مقدمة، وفعل المباح ليس
Issue: Mubah is not wajib necessarily, contrary to al-Ka'bi from the Mu'tazila. (He argued that every mubah entails leaving a haram, i.e., it necessitates leaving a prohibited act, and every leaving of haram or its necessary implication is wajib, even if mukhayyar. So every mubah is wajib, even if mukhayyar.) We said: The minor premise is denied. First, because it is possible for the haram to be non-existent due to the non-existence of the effective cause (muqtada), which is the eternal will or the temporal will (for example), based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence. In that case, its non-existence is not attributed to performing the mubah, which is the preventer (mani') of the existence of the haram. How not? And the non-existence of the effective cause is sufficient for the non-existence of the haram, so the existence of mubah after that has no role in the non-existence of the haram. Said in al-Hashiyah: To refute it, that avoiding the haram requires one of two things: either the non-existence of will (irada), or the performance of mubah. So every wajib is also mukhayyar. Then he said: And in it is what is in it. Its explanation: The effective cause for non-existence per se is the non-existence of will; as for the preventer, it is a cause accidentally; non-existence is not attributed to it except when the effective cause exists. And non-existence is pure nothingness, and is not fit for obligation. And if what is intended is avoidance (kaf), then there is no dispute about its obligation. Said in al-Badi' and others: The truth is that there is no escape from it after accepting that a prerequisite of the wajib is wajib, for performing the mubah is a prerequisite for avoiding the haram which is wajib. And this is not valid, because a prerequisite is only obligatory as long as it is a prerequisite, and performing the mubah is not [constant].
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) with al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila, who argued that every mubah (permissible act) is necessarily wajib (obligatory) because performing a mubah act prevents the commission of a haram act (e.g., by occupying time). Since avoiding haram is wajib, and performing the mubah is a prerequisite, the mubah becomes wajib. The Hanafis deny the minor premise: the haram may fail to occur due to the absence of the will (irada) to commit it, not due to the mubah act. The non-existence of the cause (will) is sufficient. Thus the mubah is not a necessary condition. The Hashiyah adds a counter: avoiding haram requires either absence of will or performance of mubah, so mubah could be wajib. But the author critiques this, noting that the effective cause of non-existence is absence of will; the mubah is only accidental. Non-existence is not a positive attribute that can be wajib. Al-Badi' argues that if we accept that prerequisites of wajib are wajib, then mubah as a prerequisite would be wajib, but this is unsound because a prerequisite is only wajib as long as it is a prerequisite, and the mubah is not always a prerequisite. The text cuts off mid-argument.

Key Terms
مباح (mubah): An act whose performance and non-performance are equal; permissible.
مقتضى (muqtada): An effective cause or requisite that necessitates a thing's existence.
مانع (mani'): A preventer that blocks the existence of something.
علة العدم عدم علة الوجود (illat al-'adam 'adam illat al-wujud): The cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Mubah acts are never prerequisites.
B) The haram may not occur because the will to commit it is absent, not because of the mubah act.
C) Avoiding haram is not wajib.
D) Mubah acts are always less important than wajib acts.

Key Takeaway
Mubah is not wajib because the non-existence of haram can be caused by the absence of will, not by the mubah act, and non-existence itself cannot be the object of an obligation.

Ibahah as a Hukm Shar'i: Definition and Refutation of Pre-Shar'i Permissibility
Defining ibahah as a Shar'i ruling and refuting pre-Shar'i ibahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ibahah as a Shar'i ruling (hukm shar'i) and argue against the Mu'tazila claim of rational pre-Shar'i permissibility using the Hanafi proof that the absence of restriction is itself a Shar'i evidence.

Core Idea
Ibahah is a Shar'i ruling, not a rational pre-legal state.

In this passage, the author defines ibahah (permissibility) as a Shar'i ruling, specifically the Lawgiver's address giving choice (takhyeer). He argues that even where no explicit text prevents an act, the absence of restriction itself constitutes Shar'i evidence for permissibility. This refutes the Mu'tazila who held that acts are inherently permissible before revelation. Understanding this definition is foundational for usul al-fiqh as it establishes the scope of taklif (obligation) after Shari'ah.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi definition of ibahah
Identify the two key components: ibahah is a hukm shar'i (Shar'i ruling) and it is the Lawgiver's address (khatab al-shar') that gives choice (takhyeer) between doing and leaving.
The passage begins: 'الإباحة حكم شرعي لأنه خطاب الشرع تخييراً'.
2
Identify the proof for ibahah as a Shar'i ruling
The absence of Shar'i evidence for restriction is itself a Shar'i evidence for the ruling of permissibility. This is the central proof: 'كل ما عدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج في فعله وتركه فذلك عدم المدرك الشرعي لهما مدرك شرعي لحكم الشارع بالتخيير'.
The 'absence' (adam al-mudrik) becomes a positive indicator of the Lawgiver's intent.
3
Distinguish original ibahah from pre-Shar'i rational permissibility
Original ibahah (al-ibahah al-asliyyah) only occurs after the advent of Shari'ah because it is a ruling from the Lawgiver. Before revelation, there is no such ruling.
The author explicitly states: 'فالإباحة الأصلية فيها حكم بالتخيير فهي لا تكون إلا بعد الشرع'.
4
Refute the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila claim that ibahah (and other rulings) exist before revelation as rational judgments. The Hanafi response: since ibahah is defined as an address of the Lawgiver, it cannot exist before the Lawgiver's legislation. The absence of restriction only becomes a ruling after Shar'i establishes the framework of taklif.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf, but the Hanafi view is considered stronger due to its grounding in the nature of Shar'i address.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
۹۱
الإباحة حكم شرعي لأنه خطاب الشرع (تخييراً والخطاب هو الحكم الشرعي والإباحة الأصلية نوع منه أي من الخطاب بالتخيير لأن كل ما عدم فيه المدرك الشرعي للحرج في فعله وتركه فذلك أي عدم المدرك الشرعي لهما مدرك شرعي لحكم الشارع بالتخيير والإباحة ( الأصلية لا تكون إلا في موضع عدم المدرك الشرعي للحرج في الفعل والترك بل بحكم بخصوصه أصلاً، فهناك مدرك شرعي للحكم بالتخيير، فالإباحة الأصلية فيها حكم بالتخيير فهي لا تكون إلا بعد الشرع، خلافاً لبعض المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بالإباحة وغيرها من الأحكام قبل الشرع (وقد تقدم منا أيضاً إحقاق الحق هناك فتذكر.
Issue. 91. Permissibility is a Shari'ah ruling because it is the address of the Lawgiver (takhyeeran). And the address is the Shari'ah ruling, and original permissibility is a type of it, i.e., from the address of takhyeer. Because everything in which there is no Shariah evidence for restriction in its doing or leaving, that absence of Shariah evidence for both is a Shariah evidence for the ruling of the Lawgiver regarding takhyeer. And original permissibility only occurs in a place where there is no Shariah evidence for restriction in acting or leaving, but rather it is a specific ruling originally. So there is a Shariah evidence for the ruling of takhyeer. Thus original permissibility contains a ruling of takhyeer, so it only occurs after the Shariah, contrary to some Mu'tazila who say permissibility and other rulings exist before the Shariah. And we have already established the truth there, so remember.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage defines ibahah (permissibility) as a hukm shar'i (Shari'ah ruling). The author explains that ibahah is the address of the Lawgiver that gives a choice between doing and leaving an act. Even where no specific textual restriction exists, the absence of such restriction itself becomes the evidence for the Lawgiver's ruling of permissibility. This original ibahah only exists after the advent of Shari'ah, contrary to some Mu'tazila who claimed that acts have a ruling of permissibility before revelation. The author refutes them by stating that the Shari'ah provides the ruling in all cases.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibāḥah): حكم شرعي بالتخيير بين الفعل والترك
التخيير (al-takhīr): خطاب الشارع بتساوي الفعل والترك
المدرك الشرعي (al-mudrik al-sharʿī): الدليل الذي يثبت به الحكم الشرعي

Key Takeaway
Ibahah is a Shari'ah ruling of permissibility based on the Lawgiver's address giving choice, and it only occurs after revelation.

Mubah Is Not a Genus for Wajib
Disputing the classification of mubah as a genus of wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi definitions of mubah and wajib and explain why the dispute over their genus-species relationship is verbal in nature.

Core Idea
Mubah and wajib are distinct categories; the confusion is lexical, not substantive.

In the study of usul al-fiqh, precise definitions are the foundation of sound legal reasoning. This passage tackles a common conceptual mistake: treating the permissible (mubah) as a broader category that includes the obligatory (wajib). Before you can correctly classify acts or derive rulings, you must grasp the exact Hanafi definitions and see why this dispute ultimately turns on words, not legal substance.

Guided Path
1
1. Extract the two definitions
Identify the Hanafi definition of mubah (equality of act and omission) and wajib (permitted to do, prohibited to leave) as stated in the text.
These are the starting points for the argument.
2
2. Recognize the flawed argument
Some scholars claim mubah is a genus for wajib because both involve permission to do the act. Understand that this argument uses a partial definition of mubah.
The key premise: 'mubah is that which is permitted to do'.
3
3. Analyse the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response denies that 'permitted to do' is the complete reality of mubah. The full definition includes the equality of act and omission.
Permission is only one part of the mubah definition, not the whole.
4
4. Conclude the nature of the dispute
The author states: 'and perhaps the dispute is verbal' (al-nizaʿ lafẓī). This means the disagreement dissolves once the definitions are made precise.
The thamarah (practical effect) is that no real legal difference remains.
5
5. Apply the lesson
In usul al-fiqh, always begin with the exact Hanafi definition of the key terms. The relationship between categories depends on how you define them.
This lesson guards against sloppy classification in legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
المباح ليس بجنس للواجب، لأنهما نوعان متباينان من الحكم) فإن المباح المتساوي فعله وتركه شرعاً، والواجب المأذون في الفعل الممنوع عن الترك (وظن أنه جنس له، لأن المباح هو المأذون في الفعل، وهو جزء حقيقة (الواجب لأنه المأذون في الفعل مع الحرج في الترك :(قلنا : لا نسلم أن ذلك أي المأذون في الفعل تمام حقيقة المباح، بل هو المتساوي فعلاً وتركاً) فالمأذون في الفعل جزء الحقيقة ولعل النزاع لفظي) فمن جعله جنس الواجب أخذه بمعنى جائز الفعل ومن جعله مبايناً له أخذه بمعنى جائز الفعل والترك.
Issue. The permissible is not a genus for the obligatory, because they are two distinct types of rulings. Al-mubah is that whose act and omission are equal in Shari'ah, and al-wajib is that which is permitted to do and prohibited to leave. And one thought it is a genus for it, because al-mubah is that which is permitted to do, and that is part of the reality of wajib because it is permitted to do with restriction in leaving. We say: We do not accept that the permitted to do is the complete reality of mubah; rather, it is that which is equal in act and leave. So the permitted to do is part of the reality, and perhaps the dispute is verbal. So whoever made it a genus for wajib took it in the sense of 'permissible to do', and whoever made it distinct from it took it in the sense of 'permissible to do and leave'.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage clarifies that mubah and wajib are not in a genus-species relationship; they are entirely distinct categories of ruling. Mubah is defined as an act which is legally equal in doing and leaving. Wajib is an act which is permitted to do but prohibited to leave. Some scholars mistakenly thought mubah is a genus for wajib because both involve permission to act. The response is that permission to act is only one part of the meaning of mubah; the complete definition includes the equality of act and omission. Thus the dispute is ultimately lexical: if one defines mubah narrowly as 'permitted to do', it can appear as a genus, but the correct definition includes both sides, making it distinct from wajib.

Key Terms
المباح (al-mubāḥ): ما تساوى فعله وتركه شرعاً
الواجب (al-wājib): المأذون في الفعل الممنوع عن الترك
النزاع لفظي (al-nizāʿ lafẓī): الخلاف في اللفظ لا في المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition in this passage, what is the full reality of mubah?
A) An act that is permitted to do.
B) An act that is equal in doing and leaving according to Shari'ah.
C) An act that is prohibited from leaving.
D) An act that is permitted to do and prohibited to leave.

Key Takeaway
Mubah and wajib are distinct categories; the claim that mubah is a genus is based on a partial definition and is resolved as a verbal dispute.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Hanafi Rebuttal of al-Ka'bi: Mubah is Not Necessarily Wajib
Refuting al-Ka'bi's claim that every permissible is obligatory by necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul distinction between muqtadi (cause) and mani' (preventer) to evaluate whether a mubah act becomes wajib when it precludes a haram.

Core Idea
A mubah act is not wajib by necessity; it is only wajib when one intends its performance to leave a haram that is already intended.

In this passage, we explore a logical dispute with the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi, who claimed that every permissible act is necessarily obligatory because it prevents a prohibited act. The Hanafi response reveals a sophisticated understanding of causality in legal reasoning: the absence of a haram may be due to the absence of its cause (muqtadi) rather than the presence of a preventer (mani'). Moreover, even when the mubah does preclude the haram, obligation only attaches when the agent specifically intends that preclusion. This lesson will walk you through the key arguments and counter-arguments, sharpening your ability to analyze legal claims.

Guided Path
1
State the opposing claim
Al-Ka'bi argues: every mubah is a leaving of haram; every leaving of haram is wajib; therefore every mubah is wajib. Identify the minor and major premises.
The syllogism is: (1) كل مباح ترك حرام (2) كل ترك حرام واجب ∴ كل مباح واجب.
2
Reject the minor premise: absence of cause
The Hanafis argue that the non-existence of the haram can be because its cause (the will/motive) is absent. In that case, the mubah does not cause the absence. Understand the causal chain: cause of existence (muqtadi) → existence; preventer (mani') excludes existence. If muqtadi is absent, the effect is absent even without a preventer.
The text says: 'لجواز انعدام الحرام بانعدام المقتضى وهو الإرادة القديمة أو الحادثة'.
3
If preclusion occurs, intent is required
Even if we accept that the mubah precludes the haram, it only becomes wajib when the agent intends by it to leave the haram. The mubah is only a prerequisite for leaving when the agent actually intends the haram and then performs the mubah to avoid it.
The text states: 'فعل المباح إنما يكون تركاً له لو قصد بفعله تركه'.
4
Address the objector's absolute preclusion argument
The objector says that the mubah precludes the haram absolutely. The Hanafi response distinguishes between intrinsic causality (muqtadi) and accidental causality (mani'). Only when the will to commit haram exists and is counteracted by the mubah does the preclusion attribute to the mubah.
This is elaborated in the Hashiyah commentary within the passage.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and the consensus objection
The practical difference: in Hanafi, a mubah becomes wajib only in the specific scenario of intending a haram and using the mubah to avoid it. Al-Ka'bi's view leads to absurdities (e.g., every haram would also be wajib) and contradicts consensus on the existence of permissible acts.
The text mentions that al-Ka'bi is rebutted by the argument that his view would make every haram wajib, and he tried to limit it by considering different aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
المباح ليس بواجب بالضرورة خلافاً للكعبي من المعتزلة (واحتج بأن كل مباح ترك حرام أي يلزمه ترك حرام وكل ترك حرام) أو ملزومه واجب ولو مخيراً) فكل مباح واجب ولو مخيراً قلنا : الصغرى ممنوعة أما أولاً فلجواز انعدام الحرام بانعدام المقتضى وهو الإرادة القديمة أو الحادثة (مثلاً) بناء على أن علة العدم عدم علة الوجود، وحينئذ لا يكون عدمه مستنداً إلى فعل المباح الذي هو المانع لوجود الحرام، كيف لا وأن عدم المقتضى كافٍ في عدم الحرام، فوجود المباح بعد ذلك لا دخل له في عدم الحرام ، قال في الحاشية» لدفعه أنه لا بد لترك الحرام من أحد الأمرين : إما عدم الإرادة، أو فعل المباح فكل واجب ولو تخييراً، ثم قال وفيه ما فيه، ووجهه أن المقتضى للعدم بالذات هو عدم الإرادة، وأما المانع فعلة بالعرض لا ينسب إليه العدم إلا عند وجود المقتضى، وأن العدم لا شيء محض لا يصلح للوجوب، ولو أريد الكف فلا نزاع في وجوبه . قال في «البديع وغيره : الحق أنه لا مخلص عنه بعد تسليم أن مقدمة الواجب واجب فإن فعل المباح مقدمة لترك الحرام الذي هو الواجب، وهذا ليس بشيء، لأن المقدمة لا تجب إلا ما دامت مقدمة، وفعل المباح ليس مقدمة الترك إلا عند وجود القصد إلى الحرام، وأما قبله فلا يتوقف الترك على فعل المباح، فإنه ينتفي بانتفاء المقتضى لا بفعل المباح الذي هو المانع، فحينئذ لا يلزم وجوب المباح إلا حال القصد إلى الحرام ونحن نلتزمه وعلى هذا ينبغي أن تقيد أن تقيد مسألة وجوب أحد أضداد الحرام بما إذا كان مفوّتاً وفي وقت التفويت لا مطلقاً، فلا يرد أنه حينئذ يبطل ما ادعيتم من أحد أضداد الحرام وإن قول الكعبي ملازم لما ادعيتم فلا يتمشى منكم مخالفته فافهم (وأما ثانياً : فلأن فعل المباح إنما يكون تركاً له أي للحرام لو قصد بفعله تركه، وذلك لا يلزم فإنه ربما يفعل أفعالاً مباحة ولا يخطر بالبال ترك الحرام (نعم)، لو أراد الحرام) أو تخيله (ثم قصد بفعل المباح تركه فإنه يكون واجباً) في هذا الحال كما ورد في الخبر الصحيح من وعد الأجر عليه (ونحن نلتزمه ولا شناعة فيه فإن قلت: فعل المباح مفوت للحرام البتة، سواء قصد به ترك الحرام أو لم يقصد، فحينئذ لا وجه لمنع الصغرى، ولو منع الكبرى بأنا لا نسلم أن كل مفوت للحرام واجب، بل إذا قصد به تفويت الحرام كان له وجه، قلت: كونه مفوتاً أول المسألة ، بل إنما يكون مفوتاً، إذا نسب إليه العدم ولا ينسب إلا إذا قصد به عدمه مع وجود الإرادة، وأما عند عدمها فينسب عدم الحرام وفواته إليه لا إلى المباح، فتأمل فيه فإنه لا يبقى حينئذ كبير فرق بين هذا السند والسند الأول ؛ وأما بعد تسليم كونه مفوتاً فلا وجه لشرط قصد التفويت فإنه وجوب تبعي لا تشترط فيه النية كما تقدم (وألزم عليه) أي على الكعبي ( بأنه) أي وجوب المباح (مصادمة للإجماع فإن الإجماع القاطع دل على أن الأشياء المباحة متحققة البتة فأجاب أنه أي الإجماع على الإباحة بالنظر إلى ذات الفعل) فإنها بما هي هي مباحة لا حرج في نفس فعلها ولا في تركها (وهذا) أي وجوبها (بالنظر إلى ما تستلزمه) من ترك الحرام الذي هو الواجب بنفسه وهذا بالعرض (ونوقض) الكعبي بأنه يلزم أن يكون كل حرام واجباً، لأن كل حرام ترك لحرام آخر هو ضده وكل ترك حرام واجب ولو تخييراً (وأجيب بأن له أن يلتزمه باعتبار (الجهتين فمن جهة نفس ذاته حرام ومن جهة أنه ترك حرام واجب ولا شناعة، وقد تقدم جواب حسن فتذكره.
Issue. The permissible is not necessarily obligatory, contrary to al-Ka'bi from the Mu'tazila. He argued: Every permissible (mubah) is a leaving of a prohibited act (haram) – i.e., it necessarily involves leaving a haram. And every leaving of a haram – or its necessary condition – is obligatory, even if optionally. So every mubah is wajib, even if optionally. We say: The minor premise is rejected. First, because it is possible for the haram to be absent due to the absence of its cause – which is the eternal or created will – based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause of existence. In that case, the non-existence is not attributed to the act of mubah which is the preventer of the existence of the haram. How so? The absence of the cause is sufficient for the absence of the haram, so the existence of the mubah afterwards has no role in the absence of the haram. It is said in the Hāshiyah to refute it: The leaving of the haram must be due to one of two things: either absence of will, or the act of mubah. So every one [of those] is wajib, even if optionally. Then he said: There is something in it. His reasoning is that the cause of non-existence in essence is the absence of will, while the preventer is a cause accidentally; non-existence is only attributed to it when the cause exists. And non-existence is a pure nothingness that is not suitable for obligation. If what is intended is restraint, there is no dispute about its obligation. It is said in al-Badīʿ and others: The truth is that there is no escape after accepting that the prerequisite of the obligatory is obligatory, because the act of mubah is a prerequisite for leaving the haram which is obligatory. But this is nothing, because the prerequisite is only obligatory as long as it is a prerequisite, and the act of mubah is not a prerequisite for leaving except when there is intent to commit the haram. Before that, the leaving does not depend on the act of mubah, because it ceases due to the absence of the cause, not by the act of mubah which is the preventer. So then obligation of mubah only arises at the moment of intending the haram, and we accept that. And on this, the issue of the obligation of one of the opposites of haram should be restricted to when it is preclusive and at the time of preclusion, not absolutely. So it is not objected that then your claim of one of the opposites of haram is invalid, and that the saying of al-Ka'bi is entailed by your claim, so your opposition is not consistent – understand. Second, because the act of mubah is only a leaving of the haram if he intends by his act to leave it. That is not necessary, because he may perform mubah acts without any thought of leaving the haram. Yes, if he intended the haram or imagined it, then intended by the mubah act to leave it, then it becomes obligatory in that situation, as in the authentic report of promising reward for it. And we accept that; there is no shame in it. If you say: The act of mubah precludes the haram absolutely, whether he intends leaving it or not. So there is no basis to reject the minor premise. And if you reject the major premise by saying we do not accept that every precluder of haram is wajib – rather, if he intends preclusion it has basis. I say: Its being preclusive is the core of the issue. Rather, it is only preclusive when the non-existence is attributed to it, and it is not attributed except when he intends its non-existence with the existence of will. When will is absent, the non-existence and preclusion of haram are attributed to it, not to the mubah. So reflect, for then there remains no great difference between this proof and the first. After accepting its being preclusive, there is no basis for the condition of intent of preclusion because it is a subsidiary obligation not requiring intention, as discussed. And it is objected against al-Ka'bi that the obligation of mubah contradicts consensus, for decisive consensus indicates that permissible things are definitely existent. He answered: Consensus is about ibahah in regard to the essence of the act – for as it is in itself, it is permissible, no restriction in its act or leave. While this (its obligation) is in regard to what it entails, i.e., leaving the haram which in itself is obligatory, and this is accidental. And al-Ka'bi is rebutted that it would necessitate that every haram is wajib, because every haram is a leaving of another haram that is its opposite, and every leaving of haram is wajib even if optionally. And he responded that he can accept it regarding two aspects: from the aspect of its own essence it is haram, and from the aspect of its being a leaving of haram it is wajib. There is no shame in that. And a good answer has preceded, so remember it.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a complex logical dispute with al-Ka'bi of the Mu'tazila, who argued that every permissible act is necessarily obligatory because doing the permissible prevents or leaves a haram act. The Hanafi response is twofold. First, the haram may be absent simply because its cause (the will) is absent, not because of the permissible act. Second, even if the permissible act does preclude the haram, it only becomes a preclusion (and thus obligatory) if the person intends by it to leave the haram. The text further discusses objections and counter-objections, including that if every preclusion of haram were wajib, then every haram would also be wajib (since every haram is a leaving of its opposite haram). The author concludes that the correct position is that the permissible becomes obligatory only in a specific circumstance: when one intends a haram and then performs the permissible to leave it. In that case, the permissible becomes wajib, but this is a subsidiary obligation (wajib tab'i). The discussion also invokes consensus to reject the general claim.

Key Terms
المباح (al-mubāḥ): ما تساوى فعله وتركه شرعاً
الواجب (al-wājib): ما يثاب على فعله ويعاقب على تركه
المقتضي (al-muqtaḍī): العلة التامة لوجود الشيء
المانع (al-māniʿ): ما يمنع تأثير العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what specific condition does a permissible act become wajib according to the Hanafi response in this passage?
A) Whenever it is performed.
B) Only when the person intends a haram and then performs the mubah act with the intention of leaving that haram.
C) Only when the mubah act is explicitly commanded in the Quran.
D) Whenever it prevents a haram, regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school firmly holds that a permissible act does not become obligatory simply by coinciding with the avoidance of a prohibited act; it only becomes wajib when one intends to avoid the prohibited act by performing that permissible act.

The Obligation to Complete a Commenced Supererogatory Act (Istinbat from Qur'an)
Proving that a permissible act becomes obligatory once commenced, contrary to Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic verse used as dalil, apply the Hanafi principle that a general command ('amm) implies obligation, and derive the ruling that a nafl act becomes wajib upon commencement.

Core Idea
A supererogatory act once started becomes obligatory – derived from the Qur'an's command not to invalidate deeds.

This lesson explores a distinctive Hanafi principle: the shift from permissible to obligatory upon commencement. The derivation relies on a Quranic verse interpreted as a general prohibition of invalidation. We will examine the proof, contrast with the Shafi'i view, and trace the usul reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source text
Locate the Quranic verse cited in the passage: Sūrat Muḥammad 47:33, 'Do not invalidate your deeds'. This is the primary dalil for the Hanafi position.
The verse is a general command addressing all believers.
2
Classify the text as 'amm
Recognize that the verse is a general prohibition ('amm) – it does not specify which deeds or under what conditions invalidating is forbidden. In Hanafi usul, 'amm is qat'ī al-dalāla (definitive in meaning) and indicates obligation or prohibition for all instances.
The term 'a'mālakum' (your deeds) is general, covering both obligatory and supererogatory acts.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle for 'amm
Hanafi usul holds that a general command (al-amr) indicates obligation (wujūb) unless specified otherwise. The prohibition of invalidation ('lā tubṭilū') implies that once a valid deed is begun, it must be completed; otherwise, invalidating it would be a violation of the command.
The imperative form 'lā tubṭilū' is a prohibition, which in Hanafi usul also conveys necessity (wujūb al-ijtināb).
4
Specify to include supererogatory acts
Since the verse is general, it includes all acts – including supererogatory (nafl) acts. The text explicitly uses the example of nafl: 'kāl-nafl bi al-shurū‘ fa-innahu yaṣīru wājiban' (like nafl by commencement, for it becomes wajib). Thus, the ruling applies to any validly commenced nafl worship.
The Shafi'i view restricts the verse to obligatory acts, but Hanafi takes it as general.
5
Derive the ruling
From the verse, the Hanafi school concludes that completing a commenced supererogatory act is wajib. This is an istinbāṭ (derivation) using the operation of takhṣīṣ (specification) and istilzām (implication). The verse does not explicitly say 'complete what you start', but the prohibition of invalidating logically requires completion to avoid violation.
The ruling is mufta bihī in the Hanafi school, with exceptions for cases where the act becomes fasid (invalid) due to missing conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
صح دعوی
المباح) قد يصير واجباً عندنا كالنفل بالشروع فإنه يصير واجباً (خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله لعله أراد بالمباح ما أذن في الفعل، وهو أعم من المندوب، وإلا لما الوجوب بالشروع ، ثم إنه على هذا التقدير أيضاً لا بد من دعوى جزئية، كما يدل عليه قوله : قد يصير، وعلى هذا فلا يتأتى خلاف الشافعي الإمام فإنه يقول بوجوب الحج والعمرة بعد الشروع، فإذن الأولى في عنوان المسألة ما في كتب مشايخنا النفل يجب بالشروع خلافاً له لنا: الجواز بأن التخيير (ابتداء أي في ابتداء الفعل (لا يستلزم عقلاً ولا شرعاً بقاءه) أما عقلاً فظاهر، وأما شرعاً فالحج النفل بعد الشروع فيه لا يبقى الخيار والوقوع بالنهي عن إبطال العمل) بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُبْطِلُوا أَعْمَلَكُمْ﴾ [محمد : ۳۳] (فوجب الإتمام) صيانة للمؤدى عن
Issue. The claim that the permissible may become obligatory according to us, such as nafl by commencement – for it becomes wajib. Contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. Perhaps he meant by mubah that which is permitted to do, and it is more general than mandub; otherwise, there would be no obligation by commencement. Then, on this estimation, there must be a claim of partiality, as his statement 'may become' indicates. And on this, the disagreement of Imam al-Shafi'i does not arise, for he says the obligation of hajj and umrah after commencement. So the proper title for the issue is what is in the books of our shaykhs: nafl becomes wajib by commencement, contrary to him. Our proof: permissibility (takhyeer) at the beginning of the act does not necessitate, rationally or legally, its continuation. As for rationally, it is apparent. As for legally, hajj as nafl after commencing does not retain choice, and it is established by the prohibition of nullifying deeds by His statement: 'And do not invalidate your deeds' (Surah Muhammad 47:33). So completion becomes obligatory, safeguarding the performed act from [invalidation].
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific rule: a permissible (or supererogatory) act, once commenced, becomes obligatory to complete. The Hanafi position is that beginning a nafl (supererogatory) act such as nafl prayer or hajj obligates its completion. This contradicts Imam al-Shafi'i's view, though the text suggests the disagreement may be terminological. The proof is that the original permissibility (takhyeer) at the start does not guarantee continuation; legally, the Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33) commands completion. The act becomes obligatory to preserve it from being void. Thus, the ruling is derived from this verse via istinbat: the general prohibition of invalidating deeds applies to any valid act.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): A supererogatory act of worship (recommended but not obligatory by original ruling).
الشروع (al-shurūʿ): The commencement or beginning of an act.
الوجوب بالشروع (al-wujūb bi al-shurūʿ): The obligation that arises by virtue of having commenced an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof that a supererogatory (nafl) act becomes wajib once commenced?
A) A hadith stating that intention is enough.
B) The Quranic verse 'Do not invalidate your deeds' (47:33).
C) Consensus of the companions.
D) A rational argument that the act is automatically binding.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, commencing a supererogatory act makes it obligatory to complete, based on the Quranic command not to invalidate deeds.

When Does Mubah Become Wajib? The Hanafi Refutation of al-Ka'bi
Refutation of al-Ka'bi on the obligation of mubah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a permissible act becomes obligatory according to the Hanafi usul, and evaluate the Mu'tazilite counterargument.

Core Idea
A mubah act becomes wajib only when intended as a means to avoid haram; the obligation is subsidiary, not intrinsic.

This lesson unpacks a classical usul dispute between the Hanafis and the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi over whether a permissible act (mubah) becomes obligatory when it results in avoiding a prohibited act. The Hanafi position draws a critical distinction between the act's own ruling and its incidental ruling, emphasizing the role of intention and the nature of subsidiary obligation. By examining the logical arguments and refutations, you will learn to apply this nuanced reasoning to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and the opposing claims
State the dispute clearly: al-Ka'bi contends that any mubah act that leads to avoiding haram becomes wajib unconditionally. The Hanafi position is that it becomes wajib only when one intends by it to abandon the haram, and even then only as a subsidiary obligation (wajib tab'i).
Focus on the conditional nature in the Hanafi view.
2
Trace the Hanafi proof (dalil aqli)
Analyze the logical reasoning: The abandonment of a prohibited act can be attributed to the permissible act only if intention is present; otherwise it is attributed to the absence of the cause. Thus, without intention, the permissible act does not 'cause' the abandonment in a legally meaningful sense.
This uses the principle of causality in legal attribution (isnad).
3
Examine the objections and Hanafi responses
Review the objections: (1) that performing mabuḥ always causes the loss of the haram, so intention is irrelevant; (2) that making mubah wajib contradicts consensus on permissibility; (3) that it entails every haram being wajib because each haram avoids another haram. Study how the Hanafi refutes each: by distinguishing essential vs. incidental rulings, and by accepting the consequence under two aspects.
Notice the layered rebuttals—logical, consensus-based, and reductio.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical difference: If someone is about to steal (haram) and walks away (mubah) with the intention of abandoning the theft, the walk becomes wajib. Without such intention, the walk remains mubah and the abandonment is attributed to the lack of desire to steal.
Concrete example anchors the abstract reasoning.
5
Confirm the tarjih
The Hanafi position is preferred because it preserves the consensus on permissibility while logically accounting for the effect of intention, and avoids over-burdening the believer.
Tarjih is based on both textual coherence and practical ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب مقدمة الترك إلا عند وجود القصد إلى الحرام، وأما قبله فلا يتوقف الترك على فعل المباح، فإنه ينتفي بانتفاء المقتضى لا بفعل المباح الذي هو المانع، فحينئذ لا يلزم وجوب المباح إلا حال القصد إلى الحرام ونحن نلتزمه وعلى هذا ينبغي أن تقيد أن تقيد مسألة وجوب أحد أضداد الحرام بما إذا كان مفوّتاً وفي وقت التفويت لا مطلقاً، فلا يرد أنه حينئذ يبطل ما ادعيتم من أحد أضداد الحرام وإن قول الكعبي ملازم لما ادعيتم فلا يتمشى منكم مخالفته فافهم (وأما ثانياً : فلأن فعل المباح إنما يكون تركاً له أي للحرام لو قصد بفعله تركه، وذلك لا يلزم فإنه ربما يفعل أفعالاً مباحة ولا يخطر بالبال ترك الحرام (نعم)، لو أراد الحرام) أو تخيله (ثم قصد بفعل المباح تركه فإنه يكون واجباً) في هذا الحال كما ورد في الخبر الصحيح من وعد الأجر عليه (ونحن نلتزمه ولا شناعة فيه فإن قلت: فعل المباح مفوت للحرام البتة، سواء قصد به ترك الحرام أو لم يقصد، فحينئذ لا وجه لمنع الصغرى، ولو منع الكبرى بأنا لا نسلم أن كل مفوت للحرام واجب، بل إذا قصد به تفويت الحرام كان له وجه، قلت: كونه مفوتاً أول المسألة ، بل إنما يكون مفوتاً، إذا نسب إليه العدم ولا ينسب إلا إذا قصد به عدمه مع وجود الإرادة، وأما عند عدمها فينسب عدم الحرام وفواته إليه لا إلى المباح، فتأمل فيه فإنه لا يبقى حينئذ كبير فرق بين هذا السند والسند الأول ؛ وأما بعد تسليم كونه مفوتاً فلا وجه لشرط قصد التفويت فإنه وجوب تبعي لا تشترط فيه النية كما تقدم (وألزم عليه) أي على الكعبي ( بأنه) أي وجوب المباح (مصادمة للإجماع فإن الإجماع القاطع دل على أن الأشياء المباحة متحققة البتة فأجاب أنه أي الإجماع على الإباحة بالنظر إلى ذات الفعل) فإنها بما هي هي مباحة لا حرج في نفس فعلها ولا في تركها (وهذا) أي وجوبها (بالنظر إلى ما تستلزمه) من ترك الحرام الذي هو الواجب بنفسه وهذا بالعرض (ونوقض) الكعبي بأنه يلزم أن يكون كل حرام واجباً، لأن كل حرام ترك لحرام آخر هو ضده وكل ترك حرام واجب ولو تخييراً (وأجيب بأن له أن يلتزمه باعتبار (الجهتين فمن جهة نفس ذاته حرام ومن جهة أنه ترك حرام واجب ولا شناعة، وقد تقدم جواب حسن فتذكره.
The obligation of beginning of abandoning except when there is intention towards the prohibited. As for before that, abandoning does not depend on performing the permissible, for it ceases with the cessation of the cause, not by performing the permissible which is the preventer. Therefore, the obligation of the permissible is only necessary in the case of intention towards the prohibited, and we accept that. On this basis, the issue of the obligation of one of the opposites of the prohibited should be restricted to when it is a cause of waste and at the time of waste, not absolutely. Thus it is not objected that then your claim about one of the opposites of the prohibited is invalidated, and that the statement of al-Ka'bi is entailed by what you claimed, so your opposition does not hold. Understand. (Secondly: Because performing the permissible is only an abandonment of it, i.e., of the prohibited, if one intends by his action to abandon it. That is not necessary, for he may perform permissible actions and not think of abandoning the prohibited. (Yes), if he intends the prohibited) or imagines it (then intends by performing the permissible to abandon it, then it becomes obligatory) in that case, as reported in the authentic hadith of promising reward for it. (We accept that and there is no disgrace in it. If you say: Performing the permissible definitely causes loss of the prohibited, whether he intends to abandon the prohibited or not. Then there is no basis for denying the minor premise. And if you deny the major premise by saying we do not accept that every cause of loss of the prohibited is obligatory, but only if he intends by it to cause loss of the prohibited, it has a basis. I say: Its being a cause of loss is the first part of the issue. Rather, it is a cause of loss only if non-existence is attributed to it, and it is only attributed if he intends its non-existence along with the existence of will. As for when it is absent, the non-existence of the prohibited and its loss are attributed to him, not to the permissible. So ponder over it, for then there remains no great difference between this chain and the first chain. As for after accepting that it is a cause of loss, there is no basis for stipulating the intention to cause loss, for it is a subsidiary obligation in which intention is not required, as previously mentioned. (And it is objected against him) i.e., against al-Ka'bi (that it) i.e., the obligation of the permissible (contradicts consensus, for definitive consensus indicates that permissible things are definitely realized. So he answered that it, i.e., consensus, is about permissibility with regard to the essence of the act) for it, in itself, is permissible; there is no difficulty in its performance or abandonment. (And this) i.e., its obligation (is with regard to what it entails) of abandoning the prohibited, which is obligatory in itself. And this is incidental. (And al-Ka'bi was refuted) by the fact that it would entail that every prohibited is obligatory, because every prohibited is an abandonment of another prohibited which is its opposite, and every abandonment of a prohibited is obligatory, even if optionally. (And it was answered that he can accept that considering the two aspects: from the perspective of its own essence it is prohibited, and from the perspective that it is an abandonment of a prohibited it is obligatory. And there is no disgrace. A good answer has already been mentioned, so remember it.)
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazilite al-Ka'bi's position that permissible acts (mubah) become obligatory unconditionally when they result in the abandonment of a prohibited act. The Hanafi position distinguishes between the act's own ruling and its incidental ruling. The passage first explains that the abandonment of a prohibited act is only attributed to a permissible act if it was performed with the intention to abandon the prohibited; otherwise, the abandonment is attributed to the mere absence of the cause. The Hanafi argument uses logical distinctions. It then addresses the objection that this would contradict consensus on the permissibility of mubah acts, answering that the consensus is on the essence, while the obligation is incidental. Finally, it counters the objection that this would make all haram things also wajib, by noting that an act can be haram in itself and wajib in its aspect of being a means to abandon another haram. The key point is that obligation is subsidiary (wajib tab'i) and does not require intention (niyyah) as a condition.

Key Terms
المباح (al-mubah): An act that is permitted without reward or punishment in itself, but may become obligatory or recommended due to external factors.
الوجوب التبعي (al-wujub al-tabi'i): An obligation that is subsidiary, meaning it is not intrinsic to the act but arises from its being a means to fulfill another obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does a permissible act (mubah) become wajib?
A) Whenever it results in avoiding a prohibited act, regardless of intention
B) Only when the person intends by it to avoid a prohibited act
C) Never, because mubah never becomes wajib
D) Only if it is a supererogatory act

Key Takeaway
A permissible act becomes obligatory only when it is performed with the conscious intention of avoiding a prohibited act, and this obligation is subsidiary (wajib tab'i).

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
beginner
8 min
Definition of Rukhsah and its Wad'i Nature
Defining rukhsah and its wad'i classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define rukhsah and azimah, and explain why the classification of a ruling as rukhsah or azimah is a wad'i (declaratory) ruling.

Core Idea
Rukhsah is a wad'i ruling indicating ease due to excuse; its taklifi content remains.

This lesson introduces the foundational usul concept of rukhsah (legal license) and its counterpart azimah (resolve). It clarifies that the classification of a rule as license or resolve belongs to declaratory (wad'i) rulings, not directly to the taklifi (obligatory/prohibitory) category. This distinction is crucial for understanding how dispensations operate in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Locate the author's definition of rukhsah: 'what changes from hardship to ease due to an excuse'. Note that it is defined as a ruling (hukm) that descends after a hardship-bearing ruling.
The change is from 'usr (hardship) to yusr (ease).
2
Analyze the two interpretations of azimah
The text gives two meanings: (1) the ruling that got changed (the original hardship ruling), and (2) any ruling that is originally easy (never came after hardship). Understand that azimah is not simply the opposite of rukhsah but has a broader usage.
The author critiques those who confuse the two.
3
Classify as wad'i
Note the statement: 'كون الحكم عزيمة أو رخصة من أحكام الوضع'. This means labeling a ruling as 'resolve' or 'license' is a declaratory (wad'i) designation, not a taklifi one. Reflect on why this matters: it separates the legal category from the actual obligation/permission.
The author mentions al-Badi' as a source.
4
Address the objection
Consider the objection: some licenses are wajib or haram, so how can they be wad'i? The response: the instantiation (misdāq) of a license may be a taklifi ruling, but the classification as 'license' itself is wad'i. Understand that the dispensation label does not alter the taklifi nature of the act.
Key phrase: 'فالكلام في كون الحكم رخصة أو عزيمة، ولا شك أنه ليس إلا الوضع'.

مسألة (الحكم منه رخصة وهي ما تغير من عسر إلى يسر) أي الحكم ذو اليسر النازل بعد ذي العسر ( بعذر) ومنه عزيمة، ولها تفسيران : الأول : الحكم المتغير عنه ، فحينئذ لا يكون عزيمة إلا حيث يكون هناك رخصة . وثانيهما: ما لم يتغير من العسر إلى اليسر، بل حكم ابتداء كذلك، وكون الحكم عزيمة أو رخصة من أحكام الوضع صرح به في البديع»، وما قيل بعض الرخص واجبة وبعضها محرمة، فكيف يكون الوضع فيها؟ ففيه أن مصداق الرخصة وإن كان حكماً تكليفياً، لكن الكلام في كون الحكم رخصة أو عزيمة، ولا شك أنه ليس إلا الوضع فتأمل (وهي) أي ما يطلق عليه اسم الرخصة أقسام (أربعة) من حيث كونها رخصة وذا يسر (الأول: ما استبيح) أي عومل به معاملة المباح في عدم المؤاخذة (مع قيام) الدليل (المحرّم) إياه (وقيام حكمه) وهو الحرمة كإجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان عند (الإكراه فإنه باق على الحرمة، ودليل حرمته لم يخرج عن الدلالة ولم يصر مباحاً أصلاً، لكن الشارع إذ هو غفور متفضل قبل العذر ووعد بالعفو وأنه لا يخلف الميعاد ( وفيه العزيمة أولى أي في هذا النوع العمل بالعزيمة أولى، لأنه إطاعة للرب عز وجل فإن الحكم باق (ولو) صبر وعمل بالعزيمة و(مات) بهذا العذر (كان) شهيداً (مأجوراً) كما ينادى عليه قصة خبيب رضي الله عنه، ومن هذا النوع الإكراه على الجناية على الصوم والإحرام وإتلاف مال الغير حتى لو قتل كان شهيداً مأجوراً إن شاء الله تعالى، ومنه أكل مال الغير في «المخمصة»، قال الشافعية : الرخصة ما شرع من الأحكام مع قيام المحرم لولا العذر، ويظهر منه أنه يصير مباحاً بالعذر ويخرج الدليل المحرم عن الدلالة، وقد صرح به في المحصول حيث قال : إن ما جاز فعله إمّا جاز مع قيام المقتضى للمنع، أو لا الأول: الرخصة والثاني: العزيمة فعلى هذا يلزمهم أن يكون إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان عند الإكراه مباحاً، فالصابر المقتول يكون عاصياً، لأنه أوقع نفسه في التهلكة بالكف عن المباح، وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُلْقُوا بِأَيْدِيكُمْ إِلَى التَهْلُكَةِ ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۹۵] ولعل في كلامهم تسامحاً والله أعلم بمرادات عباده والثاني ما تراخى حكم سببه مع بقائه على السببية، وأراد بحكم السبب وجوب الأداء لا نفس الوجوب، وإلا لخرج عن السببية ولم يبق بينه وبين الرابع فرق إلى زوال العذر الموجب للرخصة كفطر المسافر والمريض فإن سببية الشهر باقية في حقهما، حتى لو صاما بنية الفرض أجزأ لما روى مسلم والبخاري أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لحمزة بن عمرو الأسلمي : إن شئت فصم وإن شئت فأفطر وتأخر الخطاب عنهما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن كَانَ مِنكُم مَّرِيضًا أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸٤] واعلم أنه قد روي عن بعض الصحابة كابن عمر واختاره الشيخ الأكبر صاحب الفتوحات المكية أنه لا يجزىء الصوم لهما وإن صاما أثماً، ويؤيده ظاهر الآية ويشهد له ظاهر حديث: «ليس من البر الصيام في السفر أخرجه الشيخان ولا يدفعه أن الحديث ورد فيما إذا أضره الصوم، لأن خصوص السبب لا عبرة به بل لعموم اللفظ، ولا جواب إلا بإثبات معارض أقوى يخص لأجله، وهو الذي تقدم وما روى الدارقطني عن أم المؤمنين عائشة قالت: كل قد فعل النبي ، قد صام وأفطر وأتم وقصر في السفر، لكن ترك آخره لما قالت : فرضت الصلاة ركعتين ركعتين أقرت في السفر رواه الشيخان وما روى مالك والشافعي والشيخان وأبو داود عن أنس قال : سافرنا مع النبي الله في رمضان، فصام بعضنا وأفطر بعضنا، فلم يعب الصائم على المفطر ولا المفطر على الصائم، وما روى مسلم والنسائي والترمذي عن أبي سعيد قال : كنا نسافر مع رسول الله له في شهر رمضان فمنا المفطر ومنا الصائم، فلم يجد المفطر على الصائم، ولا الصائم على المفطر، وكانوا يرون أن من وجد قوة فصام فحسن، ومن وجد ضعفاً فأفطر فحسن وما روى النسائي عن أنس : سئل عن الصوم في السفر قال : يصوم، قيل: فأين هذه الآية: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَا﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸٤] قال : إنها نزلت يوم نزلت ونحن نرتحل وننزل على غير شبع، واليوم نرتحل شباعاً وننزل على شبع والعزيمة أي الأخذ بها أولى فيه أي في هذا النوع، لأنه اشتغلت الذمة به لقيام السبب فأولى أن يخلصها ويرضي ربه قبل أن يطالب، لكن إنما يكون أولى ما لم يستضر بها (فلو مات بها أو مرض أثم لأنه أوقع نفسه في التهلكة باختياره ما لم يطلب الله تعالى منه ثم إنهم قيدوا الإثم بما إذا علم بالرخصة وذلك ظاهر لأنه لو لم يعلم فهو مطيع في ظنه والله تعالى لا ينظر إلى صور الأعمال، إنما ينظر إلى القلوب الثالث : ما نسخ عنا تخفيفاً كائناً مما كان على من قبلنا من أصر على الأمم السابقة والحكم الناسخ رخصة (كقرض موضع النجاسة، وأداء الربع في الزكاة، إلى غير ذلك من كون التوبة قتلاً وتحريم السلب وعدم جواز التيمم وعدم جواز الصلاة إلا في المسجد وعدم حل الغنائم (الرابع ما سقط الحكم إليه (مع العذر مع مشروعيته في الجملة) أي ذلك العذر (ويسمى رخصة إسقاط كسقوط حرمة الميتة للمضطر) فإن الله تعالى عن دليل الحرمة والاستثناء تكلم بالباقي بعد الاستثناء، فلم تتعلق به الحرمة لهذا العذر، وكذا للمكره، وفي هذا النوع لو لم يأت واستضر أثم البتة وعند بعض الفقهاء سقوط حرمة الميتة من الأول، وروي هذا عن الإمام أبي يوسف، ثم إنه لا بد للإثم من العلم بالإباحة البتة لما عرفت (قالوا: تسمية النوعين الأخيرين بالرخصة (مجاز) إذ ليس فيهما تغير من العسر إلى اليسر، بل اليسر أصلي فلا رخصة حقيقة (و) النوع (الثالث: أتم في المجازية) إذ لم يبق الحكم الأصلي مشروعاً أصلاً، فلا شائبة لكونه عزيمة بخلاف النوع الرابع، فإن فيه شائبة الرخصة لكونه مشروعاً في غير صورة العذر كالأول) في (الحقيقة) أي كما أن النوع الأول أتم استثناها
Matter: The ruling from Him is a license, and it is what changes from hardship to ease, i.e., the ruling having ease that descends after a ruling having hardship, due to an excuse. And from it is resolve, and it has two interpretations: First: the ruling that is changed from. So then there is no resolve except where there is a license. Second: what has not changed from hardship to ease, but rather is a ruling initially as such. That a ruling is resolve or license is from wad'i rulings, as stated in al-Badi'. And what is said: some licenses are obligatory and some are forbidden, so how can they be wad'i? In it is that the instantiation of license even if it is a taklifi ruling, but the discussion is about the ruling being license or resolve, and there is no doubt that it is only wad'i, so reflect. And they (the divisions of license) are four parts, from the perspective of being license and ease. First: what is deemed permissible, i.e., treated as permissible in not being taken to account, while the prohibitory evidence stands and its ruling (i.e., prohibition) stands, like uttering the word of disbelief upon the tongue under coercion, for it remains upon prohibition, and its evidence of prohibition has not exited from indication and it has not become permissible at all, but the Lawgiver, as He is Forgiving and Gracious, accepted the excuse and promised pardon and does not break the promise. In this type, the resolve is better, i.e., acting by the resolve is better because it is obedience to the Lord, for the ruling remains. If he perseveres and acts by the resolve and dies due to this excuse, he would be a martyr, rewarded, as indicated by the story of Khubayb (may Allah be pleased with him). And of this type is coercion to harm fasting, ihram, and destroying others' property, such that if he were killed, he would be a martyr rewarded inshaAllah. And of it is eating others' property in extreme hunger. The Shafi'is said: License is what is legislated of rulings while the prohibitory evidence stands were it not for the excuse. And it appears from this that it becomes permissible due to the excuse and the prohibitory evidence exits from indication. And it is stated in al-Mahsul where he said: What is permissible to do is either permissible while the basis for prohibition stands, or not. The first is license, the second is resolve. So according to this, they must hold that uttering the word of disbelief upon the tongue under coercion is permissible, so the one who perseveres and is killed would be sinful because he threw himself into destruction by refraining from what is permissible, and Allah said: 'Do not throw yourselves into destruction.' Perhaps there is leniency in their speech, and Allah knows best the intentions of His servants. Second: What is delayed of its cause's ruling while it remains upon causality, and by ruling of the cause is meant the obligation of performance, not the essence of obligation, otherwise it would exit from causality and there would be no difference between it and the fourth until the excuse causing the license ceases, like breaking fast for the traveler and sick person, for the causality of the month remains regarding them such that if they fast with the intention of the obligatory fast, it suffices, due to what Muslim and Bukhari narrated that the Messenger of Allah said to Hamza ibn Amr al-Aslami: If you wish, fast, and if you wish, break fast. And the address is delayed from them in His saying: 'So whoever among you is sick or on a journey, then a number of other days.' And know that it has been narrated from some Companions like Ibn Umar, and chosen by the greatest Shaykh, the author of al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya, that fasting does not suffice for them and if they fast they sin. The apparent meaning of the verse supports it, and the apparent of the hadith: 'It is not righteousness to fast while traveling' testifies for it, as narrated by the two Shaykhs. And it is not repelled by the fact that the hadith was said regarding when fasting harms him, because the particularity of the cause is not considered, rather the generality of the wording. And there is no answer except by establishing a stronger contrary which specifies it, and that has preceded. And what al-Daraqutni narrated from Umm al-Mu'minin Aisha: She said: All have done, the Prophet fasted and broke fast, and completed and shortened while traveling, but he left the later part when she said: The prayer was prescribed two rak'as, two rak'as, and was confirmed while traveling, narrated by the two Shaykhs. And what Malik, al-Shafi'i, the two Shaykhs, and Abu Dawud narrated from Anas: He said: We traveled with the Prophet in Ramadan, some of us fasted and some broke fast, and the faster did not criticize the one who broke fast nor did the one who broke fast criticize the faster. And what Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abu Sa'id: He said: We used to travel with the Messenger of Allah in Ramadan, among us were those who broke fast and those who fasted, and the one who broke fast did not find fault with the faster, nor the faster with the one who broke fast, and they used to consider that whoever found strength and fasted, that is good, and whoever found weakness and broke fast, that is good. And what al-Nasa'i narrated from Anas: He was asked about fasting while traveling, he said: Fast. It was said: So what about this verse: 'So a number of other days'? He said: It was revealed when it was revealed and we used to travel and camp without being full, and today we travel full and camp full. And the resolve, i.e., adhering to it, is better in this type, because the obligation is owed due to the existence of the cause, so it is better to clear it and please the Lord before being demanded. But it is only better as long as he does not suffer harm from it. If he dies or falls sick from it, he sins because he threw himself into destruction by choosing what Allah did not require of him. Then they conditioned the sin upon having knowledge of the license, and that is apparent, because if he did not know, he is obedient in his opinion, and Allah does not look at external forms of actions but only at hearts. Third: What is abrogated from as a lightening, whatever it may be from what was upon those before us of the strictness upon previous nations, and the abrogating ruling is a license, like cutting off the spot of impurity, paying one-fourth in zakat, and other things like repentance being a killing, prohibition of booty, impermissibility of tayammum, impermissibility of prayer except in the mosque, and non-permissibility of spoils. Fourth: What the ruling falls to due to the excuse, with its legislatedness in general, i.e., that excuse, and it is called license of removal, like the removal of the prohibition of carrion for the one forced by necessity, for Allah, the evidence of prohibition and the exception, spoke of the remainder after the exception, so prohibition did not attach due to this excuse, and similarly for the one coerced. In this type, if he does not take it and suffers harm, he sins absolutely. And according to some jurists, the removal of the prohibition of carrion is from the first type, and this is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. Then sin necessarily requires knowledge of the permissibility, as you have known. They said: Naming the last two types as license is metaphorical, as there is no change from hardship to ease in them, rather ease is original, so there is no license in reality. And the third type is more perfect in metaphor, for the original ruling is not legislated at all, so there is no trace of resolve contrary to the fourth type, for there is a trace of license in it because it is legislated in other than the excuse situation, like the first type in reality, i.e., just as the first type is more perfect.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' defines a key usul concept: rukhsah (legal license) and its counterpart azimah (resolve). The author, a Hanafi scholar, explains that rukhsah is a wad'i (declaratory) ruling that indicates a change from hardship to ease due to a valid excuse. He then refutes a potential objection: even if some licenses are obligatory or forbidden in terms of taklif, the classification as license or resolve remains wad'i.

He then enumerates four types of rukhsah:
1) An act is treated like permissible (mubah) with no legal liability, yet the original prohibition remains in effect. Example: uttering disbelief under coercion. The prohibition does not lift, but the person is forgiven. In this type, it is better (azimah) to endure hardship and refrain, and one who dies doing so is a martyr. This is the Hanafi position. The author contrasts it with the Shafi'i view, which holds that rukhsah makes the act actually permissible (mubah), meaning the prohibitory evidence is rendered inoperative. The Hanafi critique is that if so, the one who refuses to utter disbelief and is killed would be sinful for 'throwing himself into destruction'—but the story of Khubayb shows he was praised.
2) The ruling of a cause is postponed while the cause remains effective. Example: the traveler or sick person may break fast, but the month's causality still stands. They may fast and it counts, but the license allows them to postpone. Here azimah is better unless harm occurs; if one dies from fasting, they sin for choosing a hardship Allah did not require.
3) Abrogation (naskh) of a previously severe ruling from earlier nations. Example: previously, one had to cut off the spot of impurity; now it is washed. This is called rukhsah metaphorically because it is not a change from hardship to ease; rather the original hardship is completely gone.
4) Removal of a prohibition due to an excuse, like eating carrion for the desperate. The prohibition is lifted for that specific situation. This is called 'license of removal' (rukhsat isqat).

The author notes that types 3 and 4 are only metaphorically called rukhsah because there is no original hardship being eased—the ease is the original rule. However, type 4 has some resemblance to type 1 because the original prohibition applies in normal circumstances.

The text includes extensive supporting hadith from Bukhari, Muslim, etc., to prove that the traveler's license is valid and that fasting in travel is optional, but the better view is that azimah is preferable if no harm occurs.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): A ruling that changes from hardship to ease due to an excuse; a wad'i classification.
عزيمة (azimah): The original ruling without dispensation; the strict path of adherence.
وضعي (wad'i): Declaratory ruling that classifies or describes a situation without directly imposing an obligation or prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, classifying a ruling as 'rukhsah' or 'azimah' is a ruling of which type?
A) Taklifi (obligatory/permissible)
B) Wad'i (declaratory)
C) Both taklifi and wad'i simultaneously
D) Neither; it is a purely descriptive label

Key Takeaway
Rukhsah and azimah are wad'i classifications, not taklifi rulings; the concrete ruling may be taklifi but the label describes its relationship to hardship.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
First Type: Coercion and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
First type of rukhsah with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on uttering kufr under coercion and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the story of Khubayb.

Core Idea
Under coercion, the act remains haram but the person is forgiven; the resolve to endure is better and earns martyrdom.

This lesson dives into the first type of rukhsah: where the prohibitory evidence and its ruling remain, yet the act is treated as permissible in terms of liability. The classic example is uttering words of disbelief under duress. The Hanafi school insists the act stays prohibited, while the Shafi'is argue it becomes permissible. This sharp difference has profound implications for the coerced person's conduct and status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first type of rukhsah
Read the description: the act is treated as permissible (in terms of no punishment) while both the prohibitory evidence (dalīl) and its ruling (hukm, i.e., prohibition) remain. The example is uttering kufr under coercion.
The act remains haram; only liability is removed.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning
The Lawgiver accepts the excuse and promises forgiveness. However, the prohibition is not lifted. The resolve (azimah) to endure and not utter kufr is better, and if one dies doing so, they are a martyr, as exemplified by Khubayb.
This shows the superiority of azimah when possible.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-position
The Shafi'is define rukhsah as 'what is legislated while the prohibitory evidence stands' but they interpret this to mean the act becomes mubah and the evidence is rendered inoperative. They cite the principle from al-Mahsul.
They conclude that uttering kufr under coercion becomes permissible.
4
Follow the Hanafi critique
If it were permissible, then the one who refuses and is killed would be sinful for 'throwing himself into destruction' (Quran 2:195). But Khubayb was praised, not condemned. Therefore the Shafi'i position leads to an absurd consequence.
The story of Khubayb is a key proof for the Hanafis.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: for the coerced person, Hanafi says he should try to avoid uttering kufr and if he dies, he is a martyr; Shafi'i says he may utter and if he refuses, he is sinful.
This affects fatwa in real coercion scenarios.

و(مات) بهذا العذر (كان) شهيداً (مأجوراً) كما ينادى عليه قصة خبيب رضي الله عنه، ومن هذا النوع الإكراه على الجناية على الصوم والإحرام وإتلاف مال الغير حتى لو قتل كان شهيداً مأجوراً إن شاء الله تعالى، ما جاز فعله إمّا جاز مع قيام المقتضى للمنع، أو لا الأول: الرخصة والثاني: العزيمة فعلى هذا يلزمهم أن يكون إجراء كلمة الكفر على اللسان عند الإكراه مباحاً، فالصابر المقتول يكون عاصياً، لأنه أوقع نفسه في التهلكة بالكف عن المباح، وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُلْقُوا بِأَيْدِيكُمْ إِلَى التَهْلُكَةِ ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۹۵] ولعل في كلامهم تسامحاً والله أعلم بمرادات عباده والثاني ما تراخى حكم سببه مع بقائه على السببية، وأراد بحكم السبب وجوب الأداء لا نفس الوجوب، وإلا لخرج عن السببية ولم يبق بينه وبين الرابع فرق إلى زوال العذر الموجب للرخصة كفطر المسافر والمريض فإن سببية الشهر باقية في حقهما، حتى لو صاما سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸٤] واعلم أنه قد روي عن بعض الصحابة كابن عمر واختاره الشيخ الأكبر صاحب الفتوحات المكية أنه لا يجزىء الصوم لهما وإن صاما أثماً، ويؤيده ظاهر الآية ويشهد له ظاهر حديث: «ليس من البر الصيام في السفر أخرجه الشيخان ولا يدفعه أن الحديث ورد فيما إذا أضره الصوم، لأن خصوص السبب لا عبرة به بل لعموم اللفظ، ولا جواب إلا بإثبات معارض أقوى يخص لأجله، وهو الذي والشيخان وأبو داود عن أنس قال : سافرنا مع النبي الله في رمضان، فصام بعضنا وأفطر بعضنا، فلم يعب الصائم على المفطر ولا المفطر على الصائم، وما روى مسلم والنسائي والترمذي عن أبي سعيد قال : كنا نسافر مع في السفر قال : يصوم، قيل: فأين هذه الآية: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَا﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸٤] قال : إنها نزلت يوم نزلت ونحن نرتحل وننزل على غير شبع، واليوم نرتحل شباعاً وننزل على شبع والعزيمة أي الأخذ
